TERSE AND WORSE
HARRY GRAHAM, who publishes in
Verse and Worse a new collection of gay
little rhymes (Lond., Arnold; 3a. C>d.
net) may be better remembered as “ Gol.
D. Streamer” of the lighter American magas.
His analogue in this book is the Bab balladist, and
a few illus. in Gilbert’s manner would have set off
his quips agreeably. He does not pretend to more
than the tribute of a passing smile; and this he
earns. His first section of “The Baby’s Baedeker”
considers countries skittishly. Here is Trance,
for example:
FRANCE.
The natives here remark, “ Mon Dieu ! ”
“ Que voulez-vovz f " “ Comment ca va ! "
“ Sapristi t Par exemple t Un peu ! "
“ Tiens done ! Metis qu ’est-ce que c 'est que ca ? ”
They shave one portion of their dogs.
And live exclusively on frogs.
They get excited very quick,
And crowds will gather before long
If you should stand and wave your stick
And shout, “ A has le Presidong ! "
Still more amusing would it be
To say, “ Conspuez la Patrie t ’’
The French are so polite, you know,
They take their hats off very well,
And, should they tread upon your toe,
Remark, “ Pardon, Mademoiselle ! ”
And you would gladly bear the pain
To see them make that bow again.
Their ladies, too, have got a way
' Which even curates can’t resist ;
’T would make an alderman feel gay,
Or soothe a yellow journalist;
And then the things they say are so
Extremely — well, in fact —you knoiv !
Moral:
The closest scrutiny can find
No morals here of any kind.
J*'
For the Briton :
Some call his manners wanting, or
His sense of humour poor, and yet
Whatever he is striving for
He as a rule, contrives to get ;
His methods may be much to blame,
But he arrives there just the same.
Moral:
If you can get your wish, you bet it
Does n’t much matter how you get it !
The TJ.S.A. :
(0 Liberty, how free we make !
Freedom, what liberties we take !)
The Spaniard:
His blood, of blue cerulean hue,
Runs in his veins like liquid fire,
And he can be most rude if you
Should rob him of his heart’s desire ;
“ Caramba 1” he exclaims, and whack !
His dagger perforates your back 1
The “moral” of Germany “may touch a
heart” :
A glass of lager underneath the bough,
A long “ churchwarden ” and an ample “frau ”
Beside me sitting in a Biergarten,
Ach ! Biergarten ivere paradise enow !
The “moral” of Turkey adds the force of
parody :
Wives of great men oft remind vs
We should make our wives sublime,
But the years advancing find us
Vainly working over-time.
We could minimise our work
By the methods of the Turk.
Alas!
Graham flashes rarely, but he sparkles. In
Jerome’s phrase, he is “a change from The Hun-
dred Best Books.”
When wilful little Willis Black
Threw all the tea-things at his mother,
She murmured, as she hurled them back,
“ One good tea-wn deserves another ! ”
It is a pity to grow too wise to enjoy such small
literary follies, and Graham can maintain that
level perpetually. Occasionally he is wittier:
Share the experience I have had,
Consider all I’ve knmvn and seen
And Don’t be Good, and Don’t be Bad,
But cultivate a Golden Mean.
What makes Existence really nict
Is Virtue—with a dash of Vice.
Or:
Enough is Very Good, may be ; •
Too Much is Good Enough for Me 1
The parodies are a neat set, and with their
reflected virtue may keep a permanent place.
Lo, Browning for a motor-maniac :
It was chickens, chickens, all the way,
With children crossing the road like mad ;
Police disgv. ised in the hedgerows lay,
Stop-watches and large white flags they had,
At nine & clock this very day.
Here one can pass a lively hour; and the Henley
is to be treasured :
A LONDON INVOLUNTARY.
(After W. E. Henley.)
Spizzicato non poco sbirtsando.
Old Palace Yard!
Hark how their breath draivs lank and hard,
The - low, stern police I
Breaking the desultory midnight peace
With plangent call, to cry
11 Division ” ! This their first especial charge.
And now, low, luminous, and,large,
The slumbrous member hurries by.
Let us take cab, Dear Heart, take cab and go
From out the lith of this loud ivorld (I know
The meaning of the ivord). Come, let us hie
To where the lamp-posts ouch the troubled sky, —
(And if there is one thing for which T vouch
It is my knowledge of the verb to ouch).
So, as we steal
Hameicard together, we shall feel
The buxom breeze, —
(Observe the epithet; an apt one, if you. please).
Down through the sober pawn street,
Which., purged and sweet ,
Gleams in the ambient deluge of the water-cart,
Bemused and blurred and pinkly lustrous, where
The blandest lion in Trafalgar Square
Seems but a part
Of the great continent of light, —
An attribute of the embittered night, —
How new, how naked, and how clean !
Couchant, slow, shimmering, superb !
Constant to one environment, not even seen
Pottering aimlessly along the kerb.
Lo l
On the pavement, one of those
Grim men who go down to the sea in ships,
Blaspheming, reeling in a foul ellipse,
Home to some tangled alley-bedside goes,—
Oozing and flushed, sharing his elemental mirth
\\ ith all the jocund undissembling earth ;
Drooping his shameless nose,
No-r hitching up his drifting, shifting clothes.
And here is Piccadilly ! Loudly dense, (
Intractable, voluminous, immense 1
(Dear, dear my heart’s desire, can I be talking sense 1)
N.S.W. EDUCATIONAL LITERATURE
WE shall try to avoid strong language in
this article; but it will be difficult.
For there is question of the most mad-
dening outrages of all, outrages upon
children, committed by Education Minister
O’Conor, that supreme official ass who there
we are already !
Of course, this is nonsense. Minister O’Conor
would n’t hurt a child. One supposes that he is
pegging away at his educational business, doing
no more harm than he can help and as much good
as he can compass. Nevertheless this is sense.
Minister O’Conor is the head and front of his
Department. If it succeeds he will take praise :
where it fails he must take blame. He is the
Pierpont Morgan of N.S.W. educational finance,
the captain of scholastic industry on whose energy
and acumen the business depends. We shall
swear at him conscientiously. He is the doit who
wrote the stupid article on Northern Queensland
in his approved Fourth Reader. He is the un-
grammatical duffer who, in the same Reader
(p. 101), tells of a time
When the tattered folds of this crimson rag
Was dearer than life or love.
True, R. F. Irvine, M.A., N.S.W. public service
examiner and editor of the book, passed the blun-
ders. But Minister O’Conor is responsible. What
he endorses, he endorses.
Remember that to a nine-year-old urchin “The
Fourth Reader’’ is gospel whose every word is
conned. The voice of the teacher is as the voice
of a god. In a school reader there should be no
more chance of error or misprint than there is in a
religionist’s Bible. Yet a dialogue like this is
reported to us :
Worried Teacher (to class) : In what does a verb agree
with its noun ?
Smart Irritating Boy : A verb agrees with its noun
in person.
W. T. : WrOng. Next?
Next : A verb agrees with its noun in number and per-
son.
5.1.8. ( promptly) : Please, sir, it doesn't agree in num-
ber. In our Header it says, “ The folds of this crimson
flag iva,s dearer.” Lower down it says “ For a people rot
in the lap of ease.” You said a was only used for singular
nouns, and rot is a plural verb.
W.T. (floundering vainly in endeavours to explain, and
sotto voce): D the Header 1
And the sin is clearly on the soul of Minister
O’Conor.
je
When “ The Australian School ” Readers I, 11,
111 appeared, they were fully reviewed here,
justly praised, and in some things blamed. The
praise was accepted : of the blame no notice was
taken. Readers IV and V, issued later, missed
notice. We have just looked through Reader IV.
In the main it is satisfactory : the editor has
done his hard task well, on safe and prudent lines.
The book is not inspired, it is not as good as it
ought to be, as it could be ; but it is good enough
till Minister O’Conor can make a better. The
worst defect is in the illustrations, which are too
few and sometimes unworthy. “The Haunt of
the Lyre Bird'” (153) should have a reference to
locality. In the anecdote of Herschel cooking
meat in the sun (28) the seasonal circumstances
should be stated. Without a qualification, chil-
dren are apt to take the statement generally.
The article “In Northern Queensland” blun-
ders in the same way, for lack of reservations. It
is the only really bad article in the book : for once
the cautious editor has gone beyond his know-
ledge or his authorities. It is bad because it is
inadequate: it purports to picture Northern
Queensland, and fails. It is worse because it is mis-
leading : the ignorant compiler (i.e.. Minister
O’Conor) has taken statements which are or were
true of a particular place at a particular time, and
applied them generally to North Queensland, for
all time ; and things of no importance are given
disproportionate significance. Suppose you read
in a school-book article on New South Wales these
statements :
The rivers and harbours are haunted by ferocious
sharks, which lie in wait for dogs and incautious bathers.
They are powerful enough to bite a man’s leg off. Fortu-
nately the spread of settlement is leading to the gradual
extermination of these monsters.
That is ..practically what Minister O’Conor says,
substituting N.Q. for N.S.W. and alligators for
sharks. It’s ludicrous. Alligators or crocodiles
are, or were, plentiful in the Barron River,
just as sharks are or were plentiful in Sydney
harbour; but crocodiles don’t come within the
range of ordinary knowledge at Cairns any more
than sharks come within the range of ordinary
knowledge at Sydney. The references to “ loath-
some puddles ” and “ siroccos ” are just as stupid :
the article is ignorant and stupid throughout.
The picture of a horse fleeing before a “sirocco”
makes no amends by being comical.
d*
What surprises one is to find Under-Secretary
Board defending the article. And we had hopes
of U.S. Board, Based chiefly on the well-opened
eye of his portrait in Sydney Telegraph. But
Minister O’Conor is the man responsible. W*
hurl his own professional jargon at him. Ignoran-
tia neminem excusat! Qui facit per a/ium faoit
per se !
THE IRISH CHARACTER
F people of Ireland live in the past. The
fl present means little for them, and the
JL future less.
As a people, they have the strangest and
most paradoxical national character in the world.
They are at once high-spirited and full of gloom.
They are warm-hearted and at the same time full
of indifference and reserve. They are conserva-
tive and clannish to the last degree, and still they
are polite and friendly to the stranger. They are
neither genial, nor have they any real sense of
humour, but they are quick and witty and droll.
Drollery is really in its essence a mordant, biting
thing—a half-sister to wit. It has nothing to do
with the sense of ludicrous comparison upon which
true humour is founded.
To the casual eye it might appear that the Insn
were gay. But they are not gay at all. Beneath
the expression of what appears to be gaiety is
always a sadness. In fact, they are the most
melancholy people in the world.
They are a people with most charming manners.
They have that sense of the rights of others which
is the foundation of manners....
J*
But for all that they are the most awkward
people in the world to get along with. You never
know when you have them. It is impossible to
know when or at what they will take offence.
Their lack of real humour makes them fly up at
what other people would pay no attention to.
They have hardly any control over themselves.
You get on best with them when you adopt
towards them an attitude that is conventionally
courteous and correct. You will then be apt to
feel that they are among the nicest people in the
world. And your feeling will be right. So long
as you don’t do or say anything that jars upon
them things will go beautifully smooth.
But you must take no liberties with them, nor
must you, the stranger, make jokes that appear in
the faintest way to work out at their expense. If
you do, you will find them up in arms at once.
Irish people have neither a sense of jokes nor a
sense of clowning. They are a refined people
who take life seriously.
Neither must you argue on things that touch
them deepi.y. It will be well if you let politics
and religion go by the board. For in these
matters the Irish, as a nation, are the most in-
tolerant people in the world. Politics and reli-
gion are to them as the breath of life itself. They
cannot argue about them coolly of reasonably. If
you do not think with them, you are one who is
against them. They glare at you as an enemy...
The Irish are wonderful boasters. The only
good things in the world are Irish ; the only great
administrators in the world are Irish ; the greatest
soldiers are Irish ; the cleverest people in an all-
round, top-and-bottom, outside-and-in sense are
Irish. Ireland and the Irish people are the finest
things that ever happened.
They are intensely local in the race sense. Th ey
won t allow you to be an Irishman if you happen
to be born iii England of Irish parents. You are
then the most English type of Englishman, or, to
put it in another and an Irish kind of way -the
worst type of worst. You may be a Home Ruler
of the deepest dye ; you may cherish and express
the opinion that not half enough landlords were
shot. But it matters not. You are an outsider.
You were not born and reared in Ireland. . . .
They are a strongly religious people. They have
a far more real sense of religion than have any
people even in the south of Europe. They are
strangely affected by the power of ritual and cere-
mony.
A brave people in a military sense, and still a
people who willingly put their necks into yokes,
providing these yokes are of their own making.
They have, curiously enough, a sense of liberty as
it affects individuals, but they have no sense of
liberty as it affects people collectively in masses.
They would be more likely to fight for a falling,
rotten system of authority than they would be to
fight on the side of a revolt against it. Their his-
tory proves to the hilt this statement.
Thev are conservative to the last degree, and
had they been handled with the least intelligence,
or allowed to- handle themselves, they would be
the most loyal subjects of the British Crown.
Loyalty and conservatism are synonymous terms.
They love to possess authority, and though on
the surface it hardly appears to be so, they are
amenable to discipline. In this they contrast
strongly with English people, who, among all the
peoples in the world, are really the least amenable
to discipline. The reason things work so smoothly
in England is because the English people have
more their own way than any other people in the
world.
One is sorry for the people of Ireland. They
have had to put up with a great deal, but they
are an extremely awkward people to deal with.
I think they t'hemselves must own to that.
Their memories are too long, and they live too
much in the past. The past is a poetical place
enough, but the trouble with it is that it has gone
beyond recall. And there comes a time when
people must bury wrongs. Were wrongs never
buried the people of these islands would still be
living in a state of cannibalism. The people of
Ireland must follow the laws of progress and bury
the past.
But someone speaks up and asks me, What
about the people of the north of Ireland ?
Ah, my friend, spare me. The people of the
north of Ireland are not Irish.—Baht Kennedy in
The Daily Mail.

A collective edn. of Swinburne’s dramatic work
is announced, uniform with the collective poems.
Probably the prose will follow.
Printed that Maxim Gorky is not poor, by
means, but is a shrewd business man. “He is
head of a publishing company in St. Petersburg,
and it is estimated that he has made 25,000/. out
of the business.”
J. A. Thomson, M.L. Rhodes scholar, gives
The Otago Witness his impressions of Oxford. He
says the Englishmen’s reception is kindly, and
one’s personal peculiarities are readily excused as
being due to “colonial education,” while any good
-.mints in a man are valued. The breakfasts in-
trigue us. First, a sole apiece; second, a large
dish of bacon and eggs with toast and preserves
unlimited ; after that tobacco. Then lectures 11
to 1, lunch at 1, exercise 2 to 4.30, tea 4.30 to 5,
study (perhaps) 5 to 7, dinner 7 to 7.30; a miscel-
laneous evening follows. “ Where does work
come in? A good day is two hours of lectures
and four hours of reading. Some men get in six
or more hours reading, but they miss the distinctive
features of Oxford life.”
L. WHiams, defying Ruskin’s “aggravating dog-
matism ' in The Studio, urges that
A circular line—a rolling cloud, a wave, a wheel—is
Nature's synonym for motion. It is the womb or bosom
of the sail that urges the vessel onward ; the flattened
sail that stops to seek a mirror in the listless sea.... The
straight line and the curve, each in its own appointed
place, is paramount in beauty. Look at Millet’s Sower—
the wheel-like revolution of the legs and body, the
right arm whirling round upon its ageless task. The
furrows, too, are slightly curved upon the swelling matrix
of the soil. Perfect beauty, perfect unrepose. Look at
the Angelus —straight lines innumerable. Perfect inac-
tion, perfect beauty. Even the furrows are at rest.

Recent pundits: “Insanity is not a disease of
the mind, but an indication of underlying morbid
corporeal conditions.” —J. B. Tuke. “ r lhe effects
of alcohol in the production of mental defect have,
I think, been exaggerated ; and I do hot believe
that 38 per cent, of idiocy, as has been stated, is
attributable to the intemperance of ancestors.
The intemperance of the parent and the idiocy of
the child have often one common cause, pro-
gressive cerebral degeneration.’’—Jas. Crichton
Browne.

This magazine is an unaffected, unconventional
product, and it prints some bad pictures so
honestly that they taste good, because the
humanity hasn’t been “drawn” out of them.
Advts. are “looking up,” and altogether the
maga. has a good healthy pulse in it.

WINCHtSTM
Thumb Trigger Model Rifle
SOMETHING NEW
SOMETHING NOVEL
A novelty in .22 caliber rifles. As its name indicates, the trig-
ger, located upon the upper side of the grip at the rear of the
bolt, is operated by pressing down with the thumb, thus obvi-
ating “pull off” in firing. Simplicity and quickness of action
are its features. It is a take-down, and has the widely imi-
tated Winchester bolt action. Notwithstanding its low price,
its quality is the same as that of all Winchester rifles.

A CASE AT MARRICKVILLE.
A VERY CONVINCING NARRATIVE.
Tha Case of
Mrs. O. COOPER.
(By a Special .Reporter.)
The physical condition of a large
proportion of the public makes con-
tinual appeal to the sympathies of the
thoughtful and kind, who are ever on
the alert to render aid where it is re-
quired. In an interview with our re-
presentative Mrs. Caroline Cooper, of
No. 3 Fitzroy-street, Marrickville,
gives some information to the afflicted
which should help them considerably.
Her statement runs as follows :
“My health is altogether different
now to what it was some years ago,
as there was a time when l was no-
thing but a total wreck, and now peo-
ple aro always telling me how well
and strong L look. Yes. I am not
like the same person at all, for now
I am always contented and happy in-
stead of being intensely miserable like
i used to be. But Heaven knows 1
had enough to make me so, for the
pains in my head were so great that
many a day 1 hardly knew what i was
doing. In each temple there was a
nasty beating sensation, which caused
me so much agony that often 1
thought 1 would surely go mad.
“You would welcome the arrival of
bed-time, so that you could sleep and
forget your afflictions tor a while
suggested the writer.
“(Jne would naturally think so, but
i didn’t, for the simple reason that 1
could seldom get any sleep, and oh !
how those hours of wakefulness* used
to wear me out ! Truly, when l got
up of a morning I felt worse* than if
I had not been in bed at all, and 1
always had a bitter tasto to make me
feel sick for the rest of the day. Some-
times 1 got so thoroughly exhausted
that it would have been impossible to
walk had 1 tried to, but knowing how
weak I was 1 used to stay indoors to
bo on the safe side, for when one is
like that it is such an easy thing to
meet with a serious accident. And
another thing, 1 was accustomed to
taking iits of giddiness, and often J
went right off and did not know any-
thing that was going on, so you sea
1 had to be care.ul. And wasn't 1
nervous ? Goodness ! I only had to
hear a little shout or a whistle and
I d. actually jump again, and it would
be a long time atterwards betore my
nerves would steady down a bit. And
i used to have the same shakiness of
my nerves when .1 wane up from what-
ever litile sleep 1 chanced to get,
which, by-the-way, was always punc-
tuated With the most awlul dreams
that made mo shudder to think of af-
terwards, and i d lind myself covered
Witn cold perspiration, too. Uh i tho ©
were nights of horror if you tike, and
i do pray that none of mine will ever
sutler in the same way. Why, rny
mind was so unhinged through exces-
sive nervousness that i was atraid to
be le*H alone*, and would not have
stayed in the dark for a fortune ; be-
if 1 closed my eyes lor a lew
seconds all sorts of ugly faces could
to seen, and those were the kind of
hideous sights i usually witnessed in
my dreams."
“Have you any idea what caused
your head pains and other ailments
' 1 tnink it was all through receiving
a sunstroke when a girl at my home
in Goulburn, for the doctor said 1
would always suffer with my head, and
the older 1 got the worse it would be.
Other doctors treated me afterwards,
and they all said what a dreadful
state my nerves were in, and also
that my digestion was as bad as it
could be; and the unfortunate part of
the whole affair was that their phy-
sics could not help me any. Howcicis.
medicines, pills galore, and no benc-
iicial result whatever—not, 1 can say
with trutu, up to the period* when .1
purchased some Clements Tonic
through a friend’s advice, and then,
for the lirst time during my long ill-
ness, 1 learned what it was to get
some substantial ease from my tor-
ments. At first l thought the relief
would be only short-lived. It seemed
too much to expect anything else af-
ter the disappointments of the past;
but Clements Tonic soon put an end
to that pessimistic idea by making me
feel better and strong-er every day.'and
when 1 realised that my improvement
was making such regular strides, my
heart was overflowing with pratitudo.
It is only right you should know that
besides the medicines my uoctore had
ordered 1 had taken a large variety
pf other mixtures bought from chem-
ists, and when Clements Tonic was
the only one amongst the lot that
could do mo good it is but ju«t and
proper that tne truth should be ad-
mitted. Nothing l had ever tried be-
fore could make me oat like Clements
Tonic did, and 1 assure you my appe-
tite had been very poor for months
and the case that came to my head
after a few bottles’ us» of that re-
medy was something to make me
thankful for the rest of my day#. The
pains in that quarter eventually sub-
sided altogether, and the difference in
my life then compared to what
it had been was just the differ-
ence existing between night and
day. Continuing with Clements To-
nic T lost the burdensome feel-
ings that affected me after taking
food, and soon the windy spasms that
had often doubled me up were quite
cured. It had been my unhappy lotto
have a feoling in the cheist as if 1 had
swallowed something hard and im-
movable-something that kept the
breath back and almokt smothered me;
but Clements Tonic did away with
that trouble, too, and also gave me
permanent relief from the pains that
used to locate themselves between my
shoulders and about tho small of my
back. .My eyes had always felt dull
and swollen, and 1 used to be irritable
and anxious from morning till night,
tearing that something was going to
happen ; but, strange as it may seem,
after using a few bottles of ClomenU
Tome my mind became as free from
anxious thoughts as a babe's. It gave
me a sense of lightheartedness that had
been lost to me for years, but such
could only be expected to follow after
bo much good had been realised. 1
am glad to say that Clements' Tonic
left nothing undone, for it gave me a
digestion that has remained sound for
many years past, and in regard to
nervousness —well, 1 hardly know how
to express myself. At all events, 1
can say that my nervous weaknesses
were 6oon overcome, and if anybody
sutlers from sleeplessness there’s noth-
ing in this world they need take but
Clements Tonic to make their nights
peaceful and refreshing. That remedy
succeeded in giving me perfect health,
and 1 would rather have a bottle of
Clements Tonic in the house than any-
thing olse ; so would my husband.”
‘‘He has used it too, then V”
‘‘My word, yes ! and thinks the
world of it. Ho has used Clements
Tonic and Ur. Fletcher’s Pills, and
swears by them. Well, 1 hope othors
in need will read my story, so you
may publish it as you feel disposed.”

(With apologies to “ Banjo ” Paterson.) 9
The mailman stopped for supper
With us at Clancy’s camp ;
Dead gidya we was burnin’ —
We didn’t need no lamp.
There was some of us got papers ;
The Boss he got new boots ;
One chap got notes from FINNEY’S
About their BIG BLOCK SUITS.
Before he’d got half through them,
“ Just hark to this ! ” he says,
And takes and reads them to us,
By that dead gidya’s blaze.
“ If you live ’way off in Brisbane
And a new suit you need,
In indigo-dyed serge,
Or in all-wool fancy tweed, ,
“ Will you drop a line to FINNEY’S
For patterns to be sent,
With inch-tape and directions
For your self-measurement ?
“In my wild erratic fancy
Visions come to me of Clancif.”
“BANJO.”
“ Then it you should send an order,
We know you’ll wear with pride
Your SUIT AT FORTY SHILLINGS
That FINNEY’S have supplied.”
Soon us chaps and good old Clancy
In Town turned out most swell,
In our TWO-POUND TOGS from

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Reform.
I was hanging round the township, undecided
where to go,
(I had “ done in ” all my money on the “ tear ”),
When I struck up an acquaintance with a man
’twas good to know—
He looked at things so broadly and so fair.
There was a lot of feeling in his mild, persuasive
tone,
When he said it was most sorrowful and queer
That a man should strain his muscle, wear his
fingers to .the bone,
And sink the whole result in muddy beer.
He wasn’t moralising, and he felt constrained to
say,
That preaching by itself could not redeem—
But he’d practised some reforming in his own
small, simple \. ay,
And knew his was a practicable scheme.
He was rabbiting, he told me, and the job would
“run a mate;”
In fact, he felt inclined to take me in.
And he’d keep me off the liquor for some time, at
any rate,
If I’d come out in the morning and begin.
Of course I would. I thanked him; he was good
and no mistake.
His scheme might be a hobby, and all rot;
But his offer was substantial, and it could n’t be a
fake,
And I wanted work, and did n’t care at what.
So I tramped to where his camp was, fifteen miles
away at least,
On the range, beside a rugged granite seam
That leaned unto the sunrise as it reddened in
the east,
And there I learned his practicable scheme.
There was no prayer about it —it was mostly solid
toil,
I did the blessed trapping, and he cooked ;
And I cleaned and paired the bunnies while he
watched the billies boil;
That was one thing that he never overlooked ;
And then before the camp-fire, after “setting’’
every night,
He’d enlarge upon his plan for my reform ;
He supposed I’d be a tough one, but he’d make an
honest fight,
For his interest in my case was very warm.
He knew the early stages of a case were always
worst;
For, like horses in a freshly-harnessed team,
The patient’s beerful cravings were rebellious just
at first,
And they rose against his practicable scheme.
He always sold the rabbits: “For, you see,” he
said, “ the cure
All depends upon a-strengthening your nerve;
And if you handle money for a while, I’m pretty
sure
To your long-established habit you will swerve.”
There was force about his reason—it was telling,
so to speak;
And, besides, just like a brother he’d become;
So he banked our golden savings at the township
every week,
Until we’d saved a rather handsome sum. '
“ There’ll be something when we’re finished,” he
informed me, with a nudge
(To doubt his word I didn’t even dream),;
“And I’ll see that you don’t drink it—you’ll be
sober as a judge
Through the working of my practicable scheme.”
Well, one night he left a sovereign where I’d see
it on the floor,
And while he was asleep I went a-tramp
To the township; I was thirsty for a drink or two.
no more.
I’d be back ere kookaburra at the camp.
Now, some things a man remembers that ’twere
better to forget,
But they’re far too deeply burned into his brain,
So the memory of that morning makes me blaze
in fury yet,
And I wonder how my reason stood the strain.
The philanthropist was waiting for that simple
chance to scoot,
And was off before the morning’s faintest gleam,
And I didn’t drink my dividend—he’d bolted
with the loot.
Then I realised hia “ practicable scheme.”
VIO.
BEN SUN.
A Rant on the Ant.
Through scented gum, through brigalow and
mulga,
Along the Darling, Paroo and the Culg’a,
From wild Orara to the thirsty Bulga,
Lord stitch my pants !
(’Tis persecution, man, that makes me vulga’)
There’s plaguing ants.
Although I’ve wamdered in the tramp’s regalia,
From sea to sea, into its penetralia,
I’ve never yet discovered in Australia
Wild elephants,
Or that delightful spot (alas, the failure !)
Unknown to ants.
I’ve pitched my camp according to convention,
In every kind of place that you could mention ;
To sand, and loam, and clay I’ve giv’n attention,
Likewise to plants;
But nary once by chance or with intention
Eluded ants.
The night when I proposed to Hannah Thatcher
(There was no girl in all the land to match her),
She bounded up and off—nor could I catch her,
Because —O Cant!
Where ’twould have been indelicate to scratch
her,
There was an ant.
In drought and flood, in every kind of weather,
’Tis certain man and ant will be together;
And though it is a battery ’gainst a feather,
He mostly grants,
When one or two invade his basement leather,
The field to ants.
A spot at times belieth my conclusion,
When man awhile knows freedom from intrusion;
But jam or steak soon shows him his delusion ;
And he descants,
With qualifying adjuncts in profusion,
On swarming ants.
N.S.W.
EDWARD S. SORENSON.

Inventors, scientists, and physicians have for
years been trying to find some method whereby

others had failed to do.
Mr. Minges resides in Brighton, N.Y., and has
devoted the best part of his life to studying and
experimenting on the Cartilage, and his great
efforts have at last been crowned with success. A
large company, composed of Brighton’s leading
citizens, has been formed for the purpose of
placing Mr. Minges’ discovery and inventions

any lady or gentleman who is short to increase her
or his height from two to five inches. These
results are absolutely guaranteed.

himself, and has grown from a short, stunted

THE WHITE HALF-PAGE.
An uninvited guest, who has just arrived to test
The country air where Dante went a-strolling,
Remarked: “ Well, lam blest, if this ain t a cosy nest,
You had better let me do you some log-rolling.
This climate is a prize ; you ought to advertise,
And your imps and friends make up a gorgeous pageant!”
The devil’s goggle eyes nearly fell out with surprise
At the coolness of this advertising agent.
And he sadly murmured“ Git!—the track you’d better
hit
To where the cooler breezes are a-blowing ;
For though you’re full of grit, you’d soon ‘ hoo-doo ’ the
pit,
And before you’d been here long it would be snowing.”
Voice of the Sphinx Club.
JX
FRAUDULENT ADVERTISING.
The curse of commercial life to-day is fraudulent
advertising. Hardly a week goes by in which we
do not hear of some person being cajoled into
buying somebody’s linseed poultice because the
advertisement said it would draw anything draw-
able, from a “Hop” cartoon to an iron-bark
stump.
JX
It seems to us as if the advertising writer is the
prime and responsible party in the perpetration
of fraudulent advertising, since he is the originator
of it. He exercises his ingenuity, knowledge, art
of expression and technical skill for perpetrating
fraud. Yet he is only the small pea-nut man in
the operation.
JX
The greatest sinner in fraudulent advertising is
the newspaper. The newspaper is the instru-
ment of publicity which gives voice to those
fraudulent statements. It is responsible for the
publication of those misleading advertisements,
and yet holds its franchise in the public interest
on the understanding that, in return for freedom
of speech allowed, protection shall be given to its
readers.
JX
SAID JUNIUS:
“ It is necessary to establish the truth of your
first proposition before you presume to draw an
inference from it.”
So far as we know, Junius did not write adver-
tisements j but had he been in the business to-day
he would no doubt have been able to prove that
the best way to stop a leak in an iron roof, tank,
or spout, was to use Ruberine Paint.
Take the modern divine-healer fake—
THE HALF HAS NEVER BEEN TOLD,
HIS TOUCH HAB MADE THEM WHOLE.
An advertisement headed like the above held out
to the sick, diseased and ailing the prospect of
eternal health. Stricken, deluded humanity
flocked to his door, hoping, but hardly believing,
that they would be cured of their ills. Had most
of the applicants spent their money in lining their
houses with Giant Paper, in order to make them
damp and draught proof, there would have been
no need for visiting such a charlatan.
JX
Advertising pays in spite of frauds, and is
believed in spite of frauds. That is evidenced by
the ever-increasing sale of Ruberoid Roofing.
People learn to know what is honest and what is
dishonest very quickly.
jX
It is no doubt pleasing to the family to know
that
OUR PAPA DOES NOT DRINK,
because a new tasteless discovery was put into his
morning cup of coffee. Perhaps we will next hear
that said papa has put his drink-money into a
terrace of houses, and that in the foundations
thereof Ruberoid Dampcourse has been laid.
JX
The well-known advertised article suffers little
from fraudulent advertising. The new line put
upon the market, if a fraud, pays the penalty of
fraud. A reputation has to be built up for genuine
merit, unassisted by the puff of printers’ ink. It
is costly, it is slow ; but if, like Ruberoid Damp-
course, the commodity possesses qualifications
worthy of recommendation, success is sure to
attend the efforts of the advertiser.
J*
The “pleasant-home-employment” advertise-
ments, which offer exceptional opportunities—
WOMEN! HERE’S YOUR CHANCE!
BE INDEPENDENT OF YOUR HUBBAND!
are familiar to all newspaper readers. Ladies
illustrating those advertisements are generally
shown dressed in handsome costumes, seated at
mahogany desks. One woman in a country town
had been induced to take up this work, in the
hope that she would be able to earn enough to
have the galvanised iron removed from the roof
and Ruberoid substituted. Her motive was an
admirable one. Lots of people would like to sub-
stitute Ruberoid Roofing for the hot-as-Sheol-m-
-sumraer and cold-as-a-Laplander’s-nose-in-winter
galvanised iron. We can only hope she succeeded.
There is no doubt that the influence of honest
advertising would be fundamentally greater, and
the necessary expenditure vastly less, if the frauds
could be eliminated, T 9 lay the responsibility of
censorship on the publisher of a newspaper or a
magazine for every advertisement appearing in
his columns is unfair in many ways. It demands
that he immediately cut off his income from those
who are his largest and most constant aid in pro-
ducing his publication. It threatens him with
setting a slower advertising pace among adver-

but to convince prospective purchasers. But,
above all, it should be truthful—like the Anchor

A NEW STORY.
Macjones is a traveller in Fiscal Politics—ham
sandwiches. Yesterday he rushed in with a new
conundrum: “What is the difference between
J. C. Williamson and G. H. Reid?” Of course
we gave it up. We generally do. He explained
that J. C. Williamson had a show, but G. H. Reid
had not. There was nothing excruciatingly funny
about that; in fact, there never is anything funny
about Macjones except his name. He claims
Welsh parentage, but was unlucky enough to be
born while his mother was away in Scotland.
JX
As a side line Macjones wanted to carry
Avenarius, but he is too imaginative. Avenarius
will protect timber above or below ground. It is
a sure exterminator of white ants, but it won t
cure measles or make feathers grow on frogs.
On one of his trips on the Western line Mac.

make neither head nor tail. In desperation he
asked straight out who he was travelling for. Iho
stranger replied: “The biggest firm on earth.
Lord and Church.” “ Don’t know them,” said
Mac, “Where’s their Sydney house?” “Branches
everywhere,” replied the stranger. “ What s your
line?” “Well, judging by what some peoplesay,
principally dry goods.” Mac. has gone through
all the directories and trade protective firm lists
to hunt up Lord and Church. Dry cuss, Macjones,

Ilosv to Address Betters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

THURSDAY, JULY 20, 1905.
FEDERALISING THE STATE DEBTS.
Since the time, a few months ago, when the late
Australian Government dragged its dignity in the
Inud by holding further futile conferences with
the State authorities over the proposed federalising
of the debts, little or nothing has been done. The
only thing that is more negative than the thing
that hasn’t been done since the conference is the
thing that wasn’t done at the conference, and the
thing that it is proposed to do during the coming
Federal session is vague and uncertain. The con-
ference fixed an indifferent eye upon a scheme in
which nobody really felt any great confidence.
Some of the State Premiers promised, in a manner
which carried no weight and exhibited no enthu-
siasm, to submit the matter to their State Parlia-
ments, and some of them hardly promised
even that much. Nobody undertook that he
would bring the matter of federalising the State
debts promptly before his little local legislature ;
drive any necessary measure through with all
the force of his majority; and, having got it
through, that he would then dwell on the door-
step of the Australian Parliament till it did its
share of the work also. The State Premiers were
allowed to make terms, and dictate conditions,
and give back-talk over a matter in which they
should never have been consulted, except as re-
gards minor details, and a weak Federal Govern-
ment took their assumptions quite mildly. But it
is Australia’s misfortune that nobody ever seems
to be in haste or in real earnest over financial
reform, and when such reform is on the board the
tendency always is to take the most circuitous and
the worst-metalled road towards the alleged desti-
nation. If the country was one quarter as cir-
cuitous over floating loans, it would hardly manage
to borrow £250,000 in 24 months.
* # * 41
The Australian Government has power to take
over the State debts as they existed at the com-
mencement of the Commonwealth without asking
any permission whatever from the States, and a
Government which was more bent on business, and
less desirous of merely “saving its face” than the
lately-deceased combination would have kept this
fact in mind. If any State’s share of the customs
and excise revenue is insufficient to pay the in-
terest on _ that State’s debt when taken over, the
Constitution makes the State liable for the
balance, whether the State promises to pay that
balance or not; and the time that George
Turner wasted in asking the States to promise to
pay the balance for which they were responsible
in any case, might have been more healthfully
employed at fishing. In any case, if a State
doesn’t pay its balance, the Australian Parlia-
ment has unlimited taxing powers, and can
recover the money due to it with the strong hand.
The question about whether the sinking fund
on the transferred debts shall be, at some
period in the dim future, 10s. or £1 per
cent, per annum filled much space in recent
conferences, but as it was only proposed, in most
cases, to start a sinking fund as the debts fell due
for renewal, and as some of them don’t fall due
for 46 years yet, this was really a very dim
and remote business. In any case, it is a matter
which the Commonwealth Government should
settle itself, As for the final question as to who
should do the borrowing in future, and whether
the State that wants to pawn itself further should
visit uncle in person or get its Federal parent to see
uncle on its behalf, there is no immediate need
to negotiate even about that. A Government
which was really in earnest would climb over all
these things in its hurry to get to the main
issues, which should be these four :
(1) That the Federal authority intends to do no bor-
rowing: itself, except for the renewal of State debts taken
over.
(2) That it intends to acquire all the State debts, and
will find a way to do so sooner or later, even if it is
necessary to amend the Constitution for the purpose.
(3) That it intends to reduce the aggregate debt, and if
the States continue borrowing that only means that it
will be necessary for the Australian Parliament to coun-
terbalance their extravagance by extra efforts in the way
of paying off.
(4) That the only way to prove the sincerity of the Aus-
tralian Government as regards its sinking fund proposals
—which, as they involve the raising of money, are the
proposals that nobody ever regards very seriously until
they are proved—is by starting at once. Therefore the
sinking fund should begin this financial year, and even if
the debts'are not all formally taken over till next year,
or the year after, or till 1953, the fund will not be wasted.
* * * *
In other words, the theory is that the time to
begin the work of taking over, consolidating and
repaying the public debts of the States is now,
and the place to. begin is just here. True, the
Australian Government may not be in a position
to make a very large beginning, but if it raised
£500,000 annually of extra revenue, and put that
amount each year to a fund which would be
devoted to the purchase of whatever State
securities could be obtained most cheaply, and the
fund accumulated at an average of per cent,
per annum compound interest, the Commonwealth
would at least have established a sinking fund of
ss. per £lOO. And ss, per £lOO in reality is often
more in the end than £1 per cent, in theory.
Even if nothing more were done it would mean
(at per cent.) that the Federal authority would,
in 20 years, have acquired £14,000,000 of the
States’ securities, while in 30 years it would have
acquired nearly £26,000,000. When the Common-
wealth was able to spare £1,000,000 a year,
the good work would go on with twice the
velocity. If, in addition, the Australian Parlia-
ment had the courage to adopt the Canadian
banking system, and require the banks to hold 40
per cent, of their cash securities in Government
paper, it would acquire permanent command,
without interest, of at least £8,000,000. And if it
set aside £2,000,000 of this as a gold reserve to
support its paper issue, and used another £2,000,000
in the acquisition of a Federal capital site and in
laying the foundations of a capital, there would
still be £4,000,000 to throw into the Federal sink-
ing fund for the extinction of the State debts.
These may seem small beginnings, but at least
they would mean that in 20 years the Common-
wealth would have bought up £22,000,000 of State
securities, and that the repurchase of the State’s
debts would then be going on at the rate of
£1,250,000 per annum. And the rate of repur-
chase would be constantly increasing.
* * * *
All this need in no way interfere with anything
the Australian Government might want to do re
the formal taking over of the State liabilities. If
that Government didn’t want to officially and
completely assume the burden till it had split the
last possible hair into two exactly equal strips,
and till it had devised a scheme that would please
the most suspicious State Premier and pacify the
most cantankerous State Treasurer, there would
be nothing save a possible sense of its own dignity
to prevent it negotiating so long as its voice' held
out. The Federal sinking fund would raise no
new difficulty between the Federal and
State Treasurers, and it would neither ag-
gravate nor settle (at least it would
not immediately settle) any old one. But
it would at least be something done.
Every financial reformer in Australia has some
scheme for establishing a sinking fund some day—
George Turner himself had one for founding a
sinking fund by instalments, the great work to get
really and completely under way about fifty years
hence. There have been so many schemes for
beginning to reform ten or twenty or fifty years
hence—N.S.W. State Premier Carruthers, for
instance, was a vast financial reformer in theory,
though in practice he reduced the sinking fund
instead of increasing it—that the relief of seeing
some one commence this year would be beyond
words. Even if the States kept on borrowing
that is no reason why there should not be a
Federal sinking fund to set against their borrow-
ings ; it is only a reason why the Federal
fund should be made larger when possible
so as to be a more adequate counterbalance.
To wait till the States agree to leave off floating
new loans before the Commonwealth commences
buying up and extinguishing old ones is not only
irrelevant—it is a reversal of all logic and reason.
There will be less need for a Federal sinking fund
when borrowing has ceased than there is to-day,
just as a disease which is getting no worse requires
less violent remedies than the one which is becom-
ing more serious all the time.
MOKE OF HOLMAN, M.L.A.
Holman, M.L.A., in rejoinder :
The Bulletin, entirely without warrant, dubs me a
“ convinced Freetrader,” credits me with all its pet Cob-
DEN-ite atrocities, couples me with M‘Millan, and bom-
bards me with all the stereotyped fiscal gags that are kept
in stock for the discomfiture of the Calico JiMMY-ite. The
Bulletin, apparently, cannot imagine that a man can deny
that Protection is a Labor measure without there-and-then
asserting that Freetrade is. It assumes I make that
assertion. I have never made it in my life. In the whole
course of my connection with Labor politics (which is the
whole of my life politically) I have resisted as strenuously
any attempt to tack the Labor movement on to Freetrade
as on to any other form of fiscal mania.
I didn’t say so in my letter, I admit, because, for one
thing (such is the vanity of elected persons), I thought
this was known, and, for another thing, I was only dealing
with one topic—Labor and Protection —and didn’t think
it necessary there to deal with another —Labor and Free-
trade. Had I realised fully the abstracted way in which
The Bulletin looks at State politics, and the violent effort
it requires to recall any State politician to its memory, I
would have taken care that my letter should have been a
complete confession of faith on fiscalism (and I would have
added a few remarks on Predestination and the Book-
makers’ strike, by way of anticipating any possible mis-
understanding on these topics). But my letter was not
intended to cover the entire created universe in its sweep.
I only said I didn’t believe in one thing—High Tariff.
This, says Tiif, Bulletin, means that I do believe in the
opposite thing — just as the Wheelers and Woolls-Rut-
Ledges say it we declare we don’t believe in Twelfth of
July foolishness that we do believe in re-starting 1 the fires
of Smithfield.
Let me say then now that I don’t believe in Freetrade
any more than The Bulletin does. I would oppose, and
always have opposed (hitherto not unsuccessfully), any
attempt to bind us up with it.
The difference between us is that The Bulletin appar-
ently only knows of two policies, while I know of three.
To The Bulletin, apparently, there is nothing for it but
to choose between barracking for the manufacturing
capitalist and barracking for the importing capitalist—
“ the Tory merchant.” I decline to barrack for either.
My creed is Socialism—which X have advocated steadily
for the last fifteen years, and which The Bulletin itself
advocates occasionally—whenever it can wrendh its mind
off the High Court telephones, or the necessity of boost-
ing Private Enterprise by means of a High Tariff.
Thus, in this week’s issue, The Bulletin says Protec-
tion alone will give us an arms faePory. I deny this. Left
at this, I suppose The Bulletin would (if not too tired of
me and the topic) spread a column and a half of blast-
iferous eloquence next week over the infamy of my advo-
cacy of the “ importation ”of arms. It might call me
something worse even than a fluent lawyer (though that
was a most unkind cut). Warned by last week’s experi-
ence, therefore, I rush to say that I don’t dream of advo-
cating importing arms. But an arms factory can be
started here to-morrow without one jot or tittle of the
tariff being touched, by Socialism —by a new industry
being run on a collectivist basis from the start (to use my
own phrase). This is what I “advocate.” This is appa-
rently where The Bulletin and I part company. To
The Bulletin it is clear that either Private Enterprise
must be given the job of making arms, or Private Enter-
prise must be given the job of importing arms. To me
there seems no necessity for Private Enterprise to have
anything to do with it. The State can. make its own
arms (and its own locomotives and uniforms and ships
and tobacco—arms are merely a handy example), and the
tariff need not worry any genuine Labor man.
Plain sailing like this, however, doesn’t seem to appeal
to the untamed Caledonian intellect of the genl who
commented on my letter. According to him we must
(1) invite a Pierpont Morgan to arise and start an arms
factory ; (2) subsidise P. Morgan to the extent of a few
millions, through a high tariff, to encourage him to do it,
and incidentally develop him into a fatted plutocrat ;
(3) pass Arbitration Acts, Eight Hours Acts and Factory'
Acts to keep his waxing fatness a little in check ; and (4)
some day in the distant future buy the fatted Pierpont
Morgan out at a correspondingly fatted price. This is
what the Scotch gentleman in question calls a National
and Socialistic policy. It may be so. But it is one which
I must again respectfully decline to enthuse over.
» ■* * *
Holman, M.L.A., now dodges quite away
from all explanation of the lies contained in Ins
previous letter to The Bulletin —the Freetrade
lie that the aliens in England are mostly Germans,
who have fled there from their own high tariff
country, and the especially' 1 insolent lie that this
paper asks us to build up great steel and iron
works by a high tariff “ in order that the capitalists
who run the works may make large fortunes, and
run ARMIES of Pinkertons, and savagely fight
every step we take towards nationalising the
works ; ” and he adds a new falsehood in saying
that this paprr wants to invite a Pierpont Mor-
gan to start an arms factory, See. Such a slippery
person may seem hardly worth answering, but
some remarks follow on the off-chance :
* * * *
The Bulletin only judges Holman, M.L.A., by his
general career to be a Freetrader, a slavish agent of the
importer, an enemy to the progress of Australian industry
and the representative of a class which is all these things
and worse.
In N.S.W., as in other States, the great majority of the
manufacturer’s employees are workmen ; the great
majority of the importer’s employees are mere clerks.
The (mostly anti-Protectionist) Labor party of N.S.VV. has
been largely responsible for laws and methods which raise
the cost of manufacturing by (quite rightly) compelling
the manufacturer to pay tiigher wages for shorter hours
than in England, while it has carefully left the importer
free to undersell the manufacturer by interfering very
little with the class which forms the bulk of his employees.
It has said and done much about the sweated workman
and very little about the importers’ stand-by, the sweated
clerk. The present system does a great (leal to protect
the local unionist against the cheap local non-unionist
(and thus hampers the manufacturer), but in a multi-
tude of lines it does nothing to protect the local
unionist against the foreign non-unionist (to do
that would handicap the sacred importer). The pro-
duct of the Australian cheap non-unionist (who
would help the local manufacturer to produce cheaply) is
accursed, but the product of the foreign non-unionist (who
helps the importer so that he may sell cheaply) doesn’t
seem to matter. With all this Protection of the foreign
manufacturer and iiis agent, the importer, against the
Australian manufacturer, Mr. Holman seems to have no
complaint. The richest man in Sydney is a man who made
his pile by importing, but no one seems to have yet heard
Mr. Holman sneer at him as a capitalist and a Pierpont
Morgan. His scoffs about “interesting capitalists” are
reserved for a struggling firm which pays far better wages
than the rich importer in question, and which was trying
to resist competition based on shameful piracy of an Aus-
tralian invention. And Mr. Holman was so anxious to
curse the Australian manufacturer that he hadn’t time to
say a word against the foreign pirate.
* * » *
Every great American trust is based, as is well-known,
on private ownership of railways, the manipulation of
railway rates, and the boycotting of competitors per
medium of the railways. Close alongside Australia is
Maoriland, which shows that, with State railway control
and Labor legislation, manufactures can be fostered by
tariff methods without any sign of “ armies of Pinker-
tons” arising. Mr. Holman, in his desperate anxiety to
make out a case against local industries, carefully ignores
this example close at hand. Fiance is a country with
high Protection, vast industries, careful State control of
railways, and probably a more general diffusion of wealth
than is known in any other land on earth. Mr. Holman
apparently never heard of France. His one specimen
horror is the country which is, par excellence, the land of
private railway ownership. It suits the importer, who
doesn’t want his business interfered with by local indus-
tries, to hold up the Standard Oil Monopoly and the Beef
Trust as dreadful consequences of Protection, and their
faithful mouthpiece and echo, Mr. Holman, passes the lie
along just as he passed along the lie about the
alien in England being mainly a German fleeing from his
own high tariff. Mr. Holman’s war whoop would suggest
that there is no other land in existence save the United
States, with its private railway monopoly. In his desire
to suppress local industries, he even scoffs covertly at
his own party’s programme of Arbitration Acts, Factory
Acts and Eight-Hour Acts as things of small value and
not likely to do much good.
« * * *
But Mr. Holman believes, theoretically, in State
enterprise, and has mentioned it occasionally in the last
15 years. Also, when the country has a State enterprise
like the railways, he believes in pawning it to private
capitalists. It was declared the other night in the
Assembly, by members of Mr. Holman’s own party, that
there are 30,000 unemployed in N.S.W. If any one starts
a factory, and pays good wages for eight hours
work to fiirtl profitable employment for these men, Mr.
Holman calls him an “interesting capitalist,” and
conjures up visions of riot, anarchy, and slaughterous
“ Pinkertons.” And as regards founding State industries
to employ them Mr. Holman recently wrote to The Bul-
letin that he was “ prepared to wait,” and he wouldn’t
mind starting industries “if the country is capable of
any new industries.” In the report of that debate about
the hunger and wretchedness of the N.S.W. unemployed
The Bulletin can’t even find a mention of Mr. Holman
having risen to suggest that State industries—which
might interfere with the profits of the blessed importer—
should be started for their assistance. It appears to be
all theory. And the people can’t wait while Mr. Holman
has dreams and theories in the background. They are
hungry. They can’t eat anybody’s beautiful visions of
something in the dim remote. They can’t dine on Mr.
Holman’s heavenly aspect or his fluent vocabulary.
Fifteen years of thinking and waiting haven’t got that
member any further than a decision that it isn’t necessary
to hurry, and meanwhile, on the assurance of Mr.
Holman’s Parliamentary colleagues, many of the people
of N.S.W. have struck a worse fate than being employed
by a Carnegie.
* * * *
The question which concerns the people is this: The
N.S.W. manufacturer is so handicapped as compared with
his chief competitor by having to pay higher wages for
shorter hours, that industries are increasing- almost im-
perceptibly. The importer, with the cheap workman in
England and the cheap sweated shop and office staff in
N.S.W., is able to keep the manufacturer in check and
prevent him providing any new work worth mentioning
for the workless —who can’t all “ go on the land ” for want
of means to go with, even if they knew anything about
land. Mr. Holman and his kind make not one serious
effort to establish State iron, steel and brass works, or
cotton, woollen, silk or linen mills, to find these hungry
masses employment and food—while they starve Mr. Hol-
man writes that he is “content to wait,” and he fixes no
date when his contentment is likely to give way to feverish
activity. He is neither a Protectionist nor a Freetrader,
but finding something very much resembling Freetrade
on hand —something near enough to it to give the Free-
trade importer the advantage and the real worker
the poverty and hunger-—he doesn’t propose to disturb
this blissful condition by any undue haste after State
industries. In fact his original letter (B. 6/7/’O5) suggests
a fearful amount of waiting while the importer
makes his pile and the artisan pines for a job,
for in it he says also “we want to nationalise
industries as they develop,” and as he denounces
any vigorous attempt at development as a conspiracy to
bring along Oarnegies and Pinkerton murderers this
looks like very long delay indeed. And even when Mr.
Holman’s State industries come to pass, these industries
will presumably pay quite as good wages for eight hours’
work as the private manufacturer does, and then foreign
imports will make the State cotton factory quite as hope-
less as the private one is, unless the country has High
Protection—or prohibition, which is the Highest Pro-
tection.
* * * *
It is questionable if Carnegie at his worst was really as
callous as Mr. Holman. That gentleman can hardly sup-
pose that his colleagues were lying when they told their
tale of distress in the Assembly, the other night. And
what consolation has he to offer? “ The land ” is an un-
attainable refuge for most of the poor, even if Mr. Hoi.man
and some of his friends had shown, in the days when they
held the political balance, a great deal more energy
than they did in forcing a graduated land-tax to
break up the great estates of the rich land monopolist.
Most of the unemployed don’t know how to be clerks at
30s. a week in a wealthy importer’s warehouse. The
State can’t borrow enough (even if borrowing were any-
thing more than a postponement and subsequent aggra-
vation of the evil) to relieve more than a fraction
of the alleged distress. It is spending nearly £2,000,000
a year of loan money now, and it can’t borrow
£5,000,000 a year. The Labor legislation to which
so many pinned their faith is scoffed at by Mr.
Holman as so poor and weak a thing that privately-
owned industries must not be encouraged lest it should
prove of no account against the bloated manufacturer.
And the question of State industries is a mere academical
problem which may become something more, it would
seem, when Mr. Holman thinks the country is capable of
them. Mr. Holman, as a mere State legislator, might not
be able to do much, but his attitude is that of the man
who is asked for bread, and doesn’t even give anything so
valuable as a stone. If Mr. Holman were the hired agent
of the importers, what more could he do for them than
denounce Protection, denounce the local manufacturer,
and be “ content to wait ” indefinitely for State manufac-
tures, and give the wealthy anti-Soeialist importer the
clearest possible field in the interim—the indefinitely-long
interim ?
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Battleship, Two Cruisers, and a Few
Torpedo Boats.
When the Deakin Government emerges from its
temporary recess, with its programme in one hand
and the battleaxe of active warfare in the other, it
would be an interesting departure if the pro-
gramme already alluded to included a proposition
that Australia should, without further delay or
ceremony, start building its own defensive fleet.
Almost every political programme nowadays con-
tains some vague allusions to “ Defence,” but
these allusions have never, up to now, got much
further than the alluding stage. It is doubtful if
they are intended to get any further. The idea
has become general that “ Defence ” is a vast and
incredible mystery—a thing that requires 20 years
of consideration, and 20 or 40 more years for the
drawing up of plans, the preparation of reports,
and the collecting of learned opinions from
imported officials with cocked hats—and it is
questionable if any Minister nowadays expects
anything to really happen within his own life-
time. Some general remarks about the necessity
of teaching the school-kid to stick out his chest in
the playground, and the advisability of encourag-
ing the cadet to practise with his pea-rifle on the
laughing jackass, are about all the expansion that
is really looked for. If an Australian Ministry
put baldly on its programme the building of a
good up-to-date battleship, two first-class cruisers,
and say ten torpedo boats as the first beginnings
of an Australian marine defence force, it would be
so surprisingly practical a proceeding that the
Speaker would probably faint in the fastnesses of
his own wig. Yet it is hard to see why such a
thing should not be done. Everybody is
agreed that Australia requires sea defence, and
already broad plain hints come from England that
we may have to provide our own sea defence
or take our chance /among the wolves. Other
countries with small populations—such countries
as Denmark, Greece, Norway, Chili and Peru,
all with less than our population, and such as
Holland and Argentina with not much more—do
more or less in this way as a matter of course.
Holland possesses 17 modern fighting ships and a
swarm of torpedo boats, and Argentina can argue
the point with 20 quarrelsome vessels at a push.
There is no reason why Australia should not do
better than any of these. It isn’t necessary to
get any complete or elaborate programme of
defence prepared ; there will be time to attend to
that while the ships are building. There will be
time also to train the officers while the ships are
building. There will be time to raise the money
(by taxation) while the ships are building. The
first essential is to get out of the lethargy
which makes Australia regard a real army and a
real fleet and a real system of defence as a dream
which is as far off as the millennium, and
which makes it consider the Minister for
Defence as an ornamental ass who writes minutes,
and gives fatherly advice in a general way to the
school-kid to throw out his chest or his box or his
carpet-bag in the playground, and urges the cadet
to take careful aim at the kookaburra with his
popgun. In other words, the only way to do
anything is to begin. And a good beginning
would be to put the immediate construction of
one battleship, two first-class cruisers, and a little
swarm of torpedo boats on the Ministerial pro-
gramme. These wouldn’t amount to a navy in
themselves, but they would be the beginning of
one, and if the same kind of thing were put on
the programme often enough there would be a
real navy by-and-bye. Some day Japan will
assuredly demand free admission for its subjects
to Australia as part of the price of its alliance
with Britain, and it all depends on the greatness
of Britain’s need whether the demand will be
granted or not. Australia, in its present defence-
less condition, could only that the Jap
demand should be refused. Australia, with a
serviceable navy, could reply that its alliance was
worth as much to Britain as that of Japan—which
would be a very different argument.

Labor and Fiscalism.
The inter-State Conference of the Australian
Labor party concluded its sittings in Melbourne
without definitely getting out of the bog which it
calls “fiscal agnosticism,” or something of that


sort, and which it pretends to believe isn’t a bog
of stagnation, but a great living principle. No-
minally, the Labor party still prides itself on its
Solid fidelity to the faith that two and two may
make four, or, on the other hand, may make five,
but the matter is not worth having an opinion
about; and ostensibly it is still willing to spend
some of its time in seeing that the job for
the Australian worker is a well-paid and
fair-hours job* and yet more of its time
in affirming that there is not the slightest
importance to be attached to the issue
as to whether there is any job or not. It is a
deplorable frame of mind for grown men to get
into, without an argument to support it or even a
reasonable sentiment to give it countenance, de-
pending for its existence solely on whirling phrases
and pretty polysyllables that would mean wealth
coming from the mouth of a parrot, but have no
practical use in a reasoning being’s equipment.
Still, whilst the Labor party managers would not
acknowledge the folly of “fiscal agnosticism,”
they were forced by circumstances into agreeing
to a means of solution of the one vital prin-
ciple of Australian politics by affirming the
desirability of holding a Referendum on the
fiscal issue, and pledging the Labor party to
loyally abide by the decision at that poll
of the people. Since most of the voters of
Australia are Protectionist, the result of such
a Referendum is a foregone conclusion, and, as a
vote of the people would give a stability to the
verdict greater than that attaching to a vote of
Parliament, the fiscal Referendum is, on the
whole, a good idea, if it is carried out under
proper conditions. But the conditions are of
prime importance. No referendum should be
taken except simultaneously with a general elec-
tion, as otherwise a heavy vote is unlikely; and
it would be useless to take a vote on the issue of
“Protection versus Preetrade.” There are “Pro-
tectionists ” whose idea of a tariff is 2'j per cent,
art valorem, and The Bulletin knows one genuine
“Freetrader” who, as soon as Protection is
mentioned, foams at the mouth and mutters
“plutocracy, trusts, rings, and public robbery,”
and yet confesses that he favors the absolute pro-
hibition of any imports which would enter into
competition with Australian manufactures. To
ask for a vote on phrases would be folly. Some
definite tariff must be defined, and the handiest
way to effect that purpose would be for the
Die a kin Government, guided by the evidence
before the Tariff Commission, to thoroughly revise
the existing hotch-potch of a Freetection tariff,
put the results in a Bill—something like the old
.Kingston Tariff Bill, but stronger even than that
—before the House, and ask the people to vote
“aye” or “nay” on that Bill. That would pro-
vide for a fair settlement of a vexed question, and
if the Labor party will keep its pledge to assist
that it may be forgiven its polysyllables.

The Deakin-Reid “ Barney.”
There continues a feverish newspaper contro-
versy as to the circumstances leading up to the
downfall of G. H. Reid as Federal Premier ; and
charges of “disloyalty” and “treachery” come
glibly from the Reidites. It is worth while briefly
re-stating the facts. While the Watson Govern-
ment was in office Reid made overtures for an
alliance with Deakin. An agreement was drawn
up, but the Deakin party would not subscribe to it.
and the proposed union fell through. Then Reid
angled for the support of Deakin and so much of
his following as he could get, and Deakin was
led into voting against the Watson Govern-
ment on an issue which Watson had made vital.
R.eid being installed in office, Deakin assisted
him to live through a few troubled weeks of Par-
liament, and then to get into recess. In recess,
Reid looked around for some programme on which
the coalition which he led might appeal to the
country, as a dissolution seemed to be inevitable
on the opening of Parliament. He hit upon anti-
Socialism. The Bulletin, at the time, pointed
out that it was not possible for Deakin to subscribe
to that faked cry, for it would practically mean a
campaign of slander against the very legislation he
had himself carried through. “Anti-Socialism”
opened Deakin’s eyes. He refused to co-operate
with Reid in his new campaign or to bellow for
his new brass god. That, practically, was the end
of the coalition ; and that was four months ago.
But it suited Reid’s purpose to pretend that he
was still allied with Deakin, and he went around
the land beating the anti-Socialistic drum, and, as
he did so, rallying supporters to the Labor party.
Meanwhile another factor obtruded. Attorney-
General Symon opened his historic attack on the
High Court, an attack which was as evil in its
effects as it was mean and paltry in its details.
Half-Premier Reid, for a long while, hesitated
between two courses. The attack on the High
Court was so unreasonable and, its effect in
alienating supporters was so clear, that at
first he was inclined to take the right
side and restrain his colleague. But in Cabinet
discussion Symon, and Reid’s old anti-Federal
prejudices, proved too strong, and the half-Prime
Minister threw his lot in with his Attorney-
General. It was obvious then that there was a
rock ahead for the Ministry when Parliament
opened, and that Deakin would bitterly resent
this anti-Federal attack on a Court which had
been constituted by his Act. Thus matters stood
as the time for opening Parliament drew near.
Deakin spoke first at Perth (W.A.) and later at
Ballarat (Vic.), practically re-affirming what
he had already told Reid privately, months before,
that he wasn’t going with him on any vague
anti-Socialism.” There was nothing dishonorable
or treacherous in these speeches. But they indi-
cated clearly that Deakin, whilst still on the side
of the Government, did not consider himself
bound indissolubly to it, linked by a common fear
of the Labor party. Then the High Court diffi-
culty began to haunt the Cabinet. With the pro-
duction of the correspondence—especially if all
the correspondence were published—the actions of
the Government would have been shown in a
sinister light. A scandal so far new to Australian
politics would have been disclosed and the Govern-
ment inevitably defeated—defeated, too, on a point
of administration which would have left no hope
of a dissolution. As a desperate resource, the
Government came down with a speech practi-
cally dissolving Parliament. If that had
been accepted by the House; it would have
given the Government at least two months
more of office and a chance then of snatch-
ing something out of a general election. The
alternative facing the Reid Government was
a bold coup which might come off, or office for a
fortnight or so with inevitable defeat to follow.
Deakin's Ballarat speech was only of secondary
importance in helping the decision. It was the
wraith of the High Court scandal which rushed
the Cabinet to its doom. Deakin’s speeches only
helped insofar as they intimated that lie was
not—as they imagined— prepared to swallow
everything Reid did. The half-Premier, in short
had reckoned that he had Deakin tied up ■ and
the Ballarat speech showed that that was wrong
and that when the High Court difficulty came up
Deakin could vote honestlv. That meant dis
missal from office, and Reid took a bold course—
as any cornered creature will if the corner is
tight enough.
Closer Settlement on Rocks*
Vic. Premier Bent's golden-haired girl the
Closer Settlement Act—is already turning out to
be the gold-washed fraud she promised to be. So
far the Board has spent about £300,000, and has
got four (a whole four) estates, aggregating 48,000
acres, which will at best supply no more than 200
land seekers, while applications already sent in
number nearly 1000. Not one would-be farmer is
likely to be on his land before next September.
Just here the Board has bumped up against the
alleged law of supply and demand, which the
Tory plausibly quotes as a reason against com-
pulsory purchase. There being a great demand
for land, the supply has suddenly and un-
accountably ceased, and the Board has been
quite unable to come to terms with the
owners of five estates it has inspected since
it bought the first four. As was also pre-
dicted, the State’s need for land has simply caused
the land sharks to raise their already exorbitant
demands. All the land trouble might be avoided
if Parliament imposed a heavy graduated land-tax
to break up large estates automatically, and also
hurried things on with a compulsory purchase law.
There has been so much criticism of the Board’s
last purchase—Overnewton, between St. Albans
and Sydenham, 15 miles from Melbourne —that
Bent lately motored round the estate to see if, as
was alleged, it was a mere stony paddock incapable
of close cultivation. It holds 9000 acres, and
Bent reckons all but 600 or 800 can be cultivated,
but local residents laugh like the benighted jack-
ss, and challenge him to get one of his smartest
Brighton market-gardeners to run a plough 20
yards in a straight line, anywhere on the estate,
without smashing the instrument. Along the
Kororoit Creek, at any rate, there are huge ter-
races of bluestone boulders, running in lines and
extending back goodness knows how far. Bent
says he can get these out for 7s. 6d. per acre—and
the locals are dying to see him try. The Board
paid £60,000 for Overnewton and the adjacent
Arundel estate of 600 acres. Of the lot about 1200
acres are good, deep, chocolate soil, worth about
£l5 an acre; the rest a thin covering sprinkled
over bluestone. And, for the present, Bent’s
Closer Settlement has stopped there.

Flag-Flapping by Law.
Deputy Arbitration Court Judge Gibson, ap-
pointed to deal with breaches of award under the
N.S. W. Act, heard a batch of cases, the other
day, which wrung tears from all the Tory papers.
A number of men had not paid up their union
subscriptions, and the Judge had to give verdicts
against them. This, says the Tory paper, is a
monstrous misuse of the law to compel men to be
unionists whether they want to or not. The
simple fact is that it merely compels men to keep
a bond which they have entered into with em-
ployers and the State. Unions agree to obey
awards of the court; but if members could back
out of the union when an award had been given
against them, or after a case had been stated to
the court, they would be able to snap
their fingers at the award and incur no
penalty. The provision isn’t to safeguard the
unions ; it is to compel workmen to obey an
award when it goes against them, which is the
very thing these Tory papers and the employers
say they want to see done. But while the prin-
ciple is as solid as any principle can be, the unions
do a foolish thing for themselves when they load
their rules with fines for refusing to flag-flap and
things like that. One man was fined because he
wouldn’t join in an Eight Hours procession ; and
the rule that compels a man to trail along through
the streets after a bit of bunting is a fool of a rule.
The Leaguers who want to compel youngsters to
“salute the flag” on Empire Day have the dead
cat of scorn flung at them ; and they deserve it.
They would earn two dead cats if they proposed
to shut the schools against kids who wouldn’t
enter into the foolish flag-flapping business. Yet
that is the position of the unions which have this
rule. All that the law of the land is concerned
with is that the members of the union shall obey
the awards of the court ; and to use it to compel
them to wave a flag, or wear blucher boots, or
christen their eldest youngster Bile, or do any
other irrelevancy, is a bit of tyranny which puts
the whole Arbitration law in danger^
