The Alleged Corruption of the High Court.
At the Sydney 12th of July orgies Dill Macky
made some remarks which the S.M. Herald
reported as follows :
“ What’s in a name ? ” It appeared to him (Dr. Macky)
there was a good deal in a name. Supposing a man
embezzled £6OOO or £7OOO, and after being convicted for
the offence appealed to the High Court, which said that
this man had been charged with larceny. Supposing the
indictment to be good, because it suited the powers that
be to call it larceny, the proceedings were all over, the
prison doors were thrown open, and the man set free.
Those who could not understand this might be inclined
to ask someone else what was in a name, and that some-
one else might be able to say that the difference was
between being within and without the walls of a gaol.
These remarks were made in the course of proceed-
ings during which almost every speaker alluded
to himself as a champion of liberty, who was wait-
ing, sword in hand, to prove his valor and his
patriotism. With all this courage lying around
and going to waste, Dill Macky might reason-
ably have been a trifle more definite. What Dill
meant to convey, pretty well everybody can guess.
But the leader of a meeting of patriots who were
burning to save their country should surely be above
talking in innuendoes. If the High Court, quashed
the conviction of T. M. Slattery, not because of
the law, but because his name was Slattery and
his religion Slattered to keep his name company,
it is much too important a matter for a herfic
patriot with his sword by his side to deal with in
that fashion. If the Court is corrupt; if this
ultimate guardian of the people’s rights and privi-
leges under the Constitution opens the gaol doors
to a Holy Roman and bangs them in the face of a
Protestant; if it inquires what a man’s religion is
first, and gives its decision accordingly, then we
have a scandal absolutely the greatest in the his-
tory of Australian courts. And if Dill
Macky believes these things, what sort of
valorous patriot is lie to merely talk about
them in cloaked words to an excited crowd ?
Let him be definite. Let him prove himself the
fearless patriot, and stand out and make openly
the charges which he has conveyed by miserable
hints and suggestions. Or, if he won’t do that,
let him be branded as a cowardly slanderer, who,
in the pursuit of his wretched insectarian cam-
paign, does not scruple to drag even the highest
Court of the country into the gutter—and, by the
way, is aided by the daily papers in doing so.
The High Commissionership.
One of the first matters to engage the attention of
the new Deakin Cabinet will be the appointment
of a High Commissioner for the Commonwealth
in London —a step to complete the Federal
machinery, which has been far too long delayed,
The lack of a voice to speak for the Australian
people, in a country where many decisions vitally
affecting Australia are come to, has been already
responsible for great material losses, as well
as serious misunderstandings; and if that
lack continues when there call for settle-
ment all the new problems caused by the
uprising of Japan as one of the great military
powers, consequences perilous to Australia, and
disastrous to that “ Empire ” idea which British
politicians cherish, might easily ensue. At present
Australian butter, Australian wood-blocks, and
Australian mutton have representatives in Britain
through the various State Agents-General. But
Australia and its national ideas have none. The
great mass of the British voters, for example,
have no idea of the root meaning of the “White
Australia ” policy and its supreme importance
to our national life. In matters of de-
fence, the Australian view and the Australian
knowledge are not put before Britain, and
that country —partly in consequence—asked for
infantry, instead of mounted rifles, when farm-
burning in Boer-land was the work of the moment,
and insisted upon vetoing the proposal by which
an Australian navy might have been founded —a
proposal which Britain, by an amazingly sudden
change of front, now wants Australia to adopt
immediately. Britain, in its relations with Aus-
tralia, is often in the position of meaning
well, but not knowing, and when it doesn’t know
it is too apt to rely on Horace Tozer for infor-
mation. And that is bad for Britain, and perhaps
worse for Australia. The appointment of a High
Commissioner—if he is the proper person—should
do away with much of that. The Bulletin does
not hesitate to indicate the proper person—the
man who fills most of the requirements, and comes
nearest of any public man offering to the ideal.
B. K. Wise is a good Australian. When the
nation called he was ready to slough off the nar-
rowing bonds- of CoBDEN-ism and come out fear-
lessly as a Federalist. Throughout the heavy
fighting of the Federal campaign and the equally
heavy struggle which the first Ministry had to estab-
lish the machinery of the new Government, Wise
was a notable figure. He is a good democrat and
a good diplomat, and knows the British ruling
folk better than most Australians. For the past
three months he has been holidaying in London,
and has already done something towards modera-
ting the fury of the British press towards Australia.
Probably he would do more in the way of counter-
acting the frenzy of the Stinking Fish organisa-
tion than any other man now in the running for
the ambassadorial position. The one point on
which Wise might be considered open to serious
criticism is that of finance. He has never been
tried, and therefore never proved, as a financial
administrator, though his prompt acceptance and
able advocacy of the Anti-Borrowing policy at last
New South Wales elections shows his capacity at
least for appreciating the obvious—and that is a
capacity which few Australian politicians possess
when it comes to matters of money. Ip any case
the Commonwealth High Commissioner will not
need to be a great financier if the Commonwealth
intends to remain an honest, non-borrowing
Government. If, as The Bulletin devoutly
believes, the time has come when the indebtedness
of Australia is about to be federalised, and then
not further increased, but gradually reduced, there
will be no call for a financial genius in London.
Paying up is a plain, honest policy, calling only
for straightforwardness. It is in the endeavor to
squeeze out another few millions from a reluctant
Cohen, and in the struggle to convince an anxious
and haggard Cohen that there is still room for
another mortgage on top of an already shockingly-
pawned security, that genius of the banking des-
cription would be required in the High Commis-
sioner’s office. And if that sort of genius is
wanted there, it will be a cold, sad day for
Australia.
Climbing Down.
Parson Woolls Rutledge begins to crawl down.
A little while ago he and other Orangemen were
wildly eloquent about an alleged person—no
name given—who got out of the N.S.W. civil
service from some cause not stated, and couldn’t
get back till he turned Catholic. When he
turned Catholic and began to grovel to the Pope,
he was promptly re-appointed to a billet, not
specified, at £1000 a year, commencing his service
at a date not mentioned. Lately there seemed a
prospect of the Assembly inquiring into this
strange matter, so Woolls Rutledge has written
hurriedly to the Telegraph to say that the
person in question, after he turned R.C., was
re-appointed, not immediately, but after a delay
of about six months, at a salary not specified;
that he was raised, after an indefinite period, to
£400 a year; and “has been promoted by the
presentGovernment to a position carrying a salary,
I am told, of about £1000 a year.” This is
not by any means the sort of thing which
one might have expected from the valiant
Woolls Rutledge. In the first place, until there
seemed to be a chance of Parliament inquiring
into the tale, it used to be told in a way that im-
plied that the alleged convert to Rome was ap-
pointed to his £1000 job all at once, but as soon as
an inquiry seemed possible Woolls Rutledge ex-
plains that he first got a mere ordinary job in the
service, and then worked up to £400. And now
Woolls Rutledge says he is “told” that the
awful R.C. convert gets “about £1000.” Seeing
that all civil service salaries can be ascertained by
reference to a very cheap official publication, only
a very sorry coward requires to conceal himself
behind “I am told ” m such a matter. Moreover,
until inquiry seemed probable, the salary of
the debased victim of Romish superstition
used to be a crisp, business-like £1000 a year, and
the sudden shrinkage to “about £1000” —which
may be only £600 —is pathetic. Also, seeing that
the story about the £1000 salary of this alleged
slave to Rome and holy water and such-like
remnants of idolatry, was going round before the
present N.S.W. Ministry took office, it is sad to
find now that it was only since Carruthers came
into pfewer that Mr. Woolls Rutledge is “ told”
the Tnfnion was raised to “about” £1000. The
best thing N,S.W. Assembly can c!o is to sum-
mon Woolls Rutledge and his hesitating tale to
the Bar of House, and ask him for names,
dates, and all other particulars. The charge he
lays is one of direct corruption, and any com-
munity which tries to be politically clean should
inquire into such a charge.

“Regulation 5764” write,:—
A Bill to be introduced to N:S.VV. Parliament-this ses-
sion is the Temporary Officers (Public Service) Bill, of
three short clauses which simply declare that, despite all
the careful provisions of the original P.S. Act, the tem-
porary clerks in three important departments are to be
hereafter permanent, with all the rights and privileges of
permanency. The contention is that some temporaries
have been so long “ temporary ” that it is mere justice to
make them “permanent.” While this may be just to
them, it is far from just to the lower grade men in the
service already
Compare the position of a temporary officer in one of
these favored Departments at, say, 10s. or 12s 6d. a day,
with the ordinary lower division permanent man between
£l5O and £2OO. The last-named was subjected to a com-
petitive exam, before his entrance. Then, before he could
(let £lOO, he passed a second stiff exam. ; and,
before he could get £l5O, another; and he has to
pass yet another before he touches £2CO. The “tem-
porary” man provided for in the Bill, who gene-
rally owed his billet to outside or Ministerial influence,
jumped into the service at over £l5O, having escaped all
the examinations heaped upon the luckless youth who
joined in the regular way. Now he will, in one stride,
obtain senioritj' over many a fellow clerk who has been
toiling up the grades, with poor pay and hard examina-
tions, since 1895. The latter will suddenly find a whole
swarm of men inserted between him and promotion, his
prospects destroyed, and the bargain on which he
entered the service ruthlessly repudiated.
The Bill should at least grade the new “permanent”
men at £l5O or under, and make their further promotion
depend on the same examinations which the men who
were permanent from the first had to go through. The
average “ temporary ” gets 10s. a day, or £156 a year, and
if graded at this, the bulk of the men affected will escape
the examination. And the whole principle of the service
is said to be promotion by exam.

Great Britain has a Parliamentary redistribution
scheme on hand, whereby England is to gain 17
members, Scotland four, and Wales one, while
Ireland is to lose 22. This is putting things more
or less on a population basis, and if England had
been half as anxious to put the House of Commons
on a population basis when the said basis would
have told against England the present proceeding
would be a touching example of honesty. But
during the first half of last century Ireland had
just about half—sometimes much less than half—
the members to which it was justly entitled, and
England was never troubled about honesty and
redistribution in those days. It was because of
this that it was possible to pass the insane law
prohibiting the growth of tobacco in the British
isles ; Ireland is as well suited to the growth of
tobacco as Virginia, and was in a fair way
to make a fortune out of the industry,
when the English importers got a statute
passed whereby the plantations were torn
up and the business prohibited, and it
remains prohibited to this day. Further, it was
largely because Ireland was swindled out of half
the Parliamentary representation to which it was
fairly entitled that the introduction of the Free-
trade system, which finished the ruin of Irish
industry, became possible The bargain made at
the Union as to the share which Ireland was to
have in the Parliament of Great Britain was very
closely adhered to, so long as the bargain was
shamefully and disgracefully against Ireland. Now
Ireland is a miserably depopulated country as the
consequence of English misrule, and the bargain
as to representation has turned against England,
and the English sense of fairness is aroused, and
the Nonconformist conscience can’t sleep at night
through hearing itself yell in its own ear that
justice must be done at all costs.
Missionary Gillan, a son-in-law of the Rev.
John G. Paton— the Grand Old Man of the Ne w
Hebrides mission—is at present touring Victoria
in the interests of the mission ; and, in view of
the repeated efforts being made to interfere with
the operation of the Kanaka Exclusion Act, his
remarks concerning Tommy Tanna are of interest.
Mr. Gillan has been engaged at mission work on
these islands for sixteen years, and should know
what he is talking about. This “ sta ion ”is on the
Urge and populous island of Malekula, which
contains some 8000 natives, of whom about 1000
are more or less Christianised. The certificate of
character which Mr. Gillan gives the islanders
—from whom the bulk of the Queensland
Kanakas are recruited—is something out of the
common. They are, he declares, exceptionally
cruel and brutal, and this cruelty and brutality
are exercised at the expense of their women.
Mercy and kindness are words which are unknown
in their language. They never extend either,
and they never expect them. Cannibalism is
practised, and men have been cooked and eaten
in sight of Mr. Gillan’s own residenee. If
a woman dies, leaving a child too young to
take care of itself, the child is buried—alive—■
with its mother. Old men and women unable to
look after themselves are also buried alive. Mr.
Gillan knows of one case where a man was buried
alive three times. On two occasions he succeeded
in scrambling out of the grave ; but the third time
he stayed under. The islanders who return from
Queensland bring rifles—the deadly-speaking
Winchester for preference:—with them, and they
are continually shooting. Some of them, it is
incidentally added, are very good shots. Mr.
Gillan, like all the experienced New Hebrides
missionaries, is a strong opponent of the Kanaka
trade. And these genial, murderous cannibals
are the same gentlemen whom the Kanakaphile
press, and the Tory party generally, wish to
retain in our continent, and who have already, in
many cases, contracted marriages with white
women. Of all the colored heathen rubbish which
the cheap labor party wishes to mix with white
Australia, Tommy Tanna seems about the worst.

“ Itan ” writes from Queensland :
The Bulletin’s demand (6/7/’O5) that Horace Tozer, as
a mean and shabby backbiter, should be brought to book
for his slanders of Australia, was answered recently, for a
letter is on the way to the Cold Country from Premier
Morgan, curtly stating “that .your utterances have been
used to the detriment of Australia, and therefore to the
detriment of Queensland.” This is one of the sharpest
rebukes that an Agent-General has yet received, and
every loyal Australian will warmly applaud it.
Well, The Bulletin hopes that it is a loyal Aus-
tralian, and it doesn’t applaud much. Merely to
inform Tozer, in effect, that he must leave off talk-
ing as he has been doing isn’t enough. It does
nothing to disprove Tozer’s miserable falsehoods,
and it leaves Horace free to strike an attitude,
like Moses struck by the rock, as an outspoken
person who told the truth ; and to hold up Premier
Morgan as a person who had to smother the truth
(as represented by Tozer) lest evil deeds should
corne to light, Tozer is distinctly reported to
have said that from £20,000,000 to £40,000,000 of
capital was being withheld from investment in
Australia because of Socialistic legislation. A
peremptory demand for full particulars as to the
amounts which make up this £20,000.000 or
£40,000,000, the persons (with their addresses)
who own it, the industries they would have in-
vested it in but for Socialistic legislation, and the
particular features or items' of Socialistic legisla-
tion that seared them off, would have enabled
Morgan to discharge Tozer as a dbhvicted slan-
derer. Anything short of a demand for full par-
(iculars leaves Tozer free to pound his shirt-front
and say, “ I suffer for the Truth’s sake.”

pipemakers. The men thought they were an
extra good lot and worth jnore than the minimum ;
so they struck. And under the Wages Board sys-
tem they had every right to strike. They are
under no obligation not to strike. They have
entered into no bond, The Wages Board system,
which some numskulls have been lately booming
in N.S.W. as part of the campaign against the
Arbitration Act, gives neither employers nor pub-

“The French Maid’’(three nights)
“The Way to Kenmare,” at Her Majesty’s
(Sydney), offers Andrew Mack a new opportunity
to interrupt a crude and conventional melodrama
with four polished and unconventional melodies;
and if he interrupted it with six melodies, there
would still be not quite enough melody and a little
too much melodrama. The story is old and
frayed, and green With age and blarney. The
Earl of Kenmare’s younger son is suspected of
having married a peasant, and Mack is the conse-
quence. The only record of the marriage perform-
ance was in the village church, and that has been
burned; and it was only after this disaster that
the proud Kenmare thought of interesting himself
in the question whether his son’s son was his
heir or only a Kenmare in a casual way of
speaking. Mack, of course, as a good Ameri-
can, doesn’t want an earldom; but, learning
that suspicions have fallen upon his mother’s
“good name,” by reason of the fire afore-
said having licked up the only record of her
wedding. Mack has no alternative but to put his
feelings in his pocket and battle his way to the
peerage. And that is “ the way to Kenmare.”
The necessary love incident (Mack is an Irishman)
is supplied by making a villain out of another
claimant to the Kenmare bogs, and sending the
two of them after the same girl. Mack wins her
in the end. The actor who gets the biggest salary
always has to.
* • • •
The yarn is rugged and unconvincing, and
the incidental happenings are less convincing even
than the principal narrative. In a desperate deci-
sion to pair off the couples before the curtain falls,
the effects wander off on to the fringe of burlesque.
And neither Andrew Mack nor his co. does much
to make the story seem true. Mack has dangerous
gifts of fluency and ease and breeze and confidence,
which lead him on to “gagging.” Thus, in the
critical scene, when the light goes out, and the
girl, for whom the sentimental young man has been
out forlo years earning a gold-mine, doesn't recog-
nise him, he acts as no young man in similar
circumstances could ever be expected to act.
True, the real young man would be an awful bore,
while Mack is a cheerful circumstance who keeps
the house laughing ; and the entire performance
is built on the same lines. As for the songs, how-
ever, “The Rose of Kenmare” is probably the
best which Mack has favored Sydney with —and
that is saying a lot.
* * * «
The support is, on the whole, better than
it was in “ Tom Moore,” though the ancient
Earl (who doesn’t look half ancient enough to be
Mack’s grand-dad) isn’t calculated to increase the
popularity of the peerage. Mrs. Malone of the
earlier piece is Mrs. Maloney in this, and is as
satisfactory with the “y ” as without it. There
are two excellent little character studies, one of a
jaunting-car driver, the other of a hunted, half-
witted old Irishman who, after all, has saved the
precious paper from the Kenmare fire and rescued
the Kenmare honor at the same time. A humor-
ous Dutchman and an extravagant Irish pub.-
keeper; a fairly satisfactory heroine; and a
thunderstorm in which some first-class cloud
effects are introduced may also be mentioned.
There was on the opening night a house packed in
every part, and it gave Mack and his players the
heartiest sort of welcome.
***.'*
John F. Sheridan is a busy man these
times. By day he is putting the finishing touches
on “ The Earl and the Girl,” which as much of
Australia as Sydney Criterion can hold will have
the first chance of seeing next Saturday evening ;
by night he is pursuing a third husband for the
Widow O’Brien and keeping an already mixed
family in the way it should go. Between times he
is picking doubles that win. Over the Widow
O’Brien only a tear of regret can be shed. The
giddy old rustler is still herself, only a trifle more
incoherent than she was in her younger days ; but
as for the rest, the fact that they are the rag
pushed into the broken pane in the window until
the glazier can arrive with something more
elaborate is painfully apparent. “The Earl and
the Girl” is John F.’s trump card ; wherefore he
has spent all the available time in polishing up
that gay and giddy couple, and has let the Widow
live a week on the memory of her old triumphs.
It is rough on the Widow, but it should be good
for the Girl.
*# * *
“The Midnight Mail,” staged at Sydney
Royal on Saturday night, before a sardine-
packed a.udienee that steamed with heat and
appreciation, is not —unhappily—of the “ Face
at the Window” type, in which horror grins
through the mask of farce. It is a quite
serious, conventional melodrama, in which the
probabilities are fairly well observed, a,nd there
is no reason for tortured Coincidence to shriek
in protest. Humor of a robust sort luxuriates
without running riot, and there isimpressed a moral
lesson as to the curse of drink and the evils of bad
company. Altogether it is the sort of play which
goes straight to the heart of the robust bottle-oh-
in the gallery, who, when he’s done a-jumping on-
the police, loves to lie a-basking in the light of a
great Moral Lesson ; and it appeals with earnest-
ness to the affections of the young man in the re-
served stalls, who is contemplating matrimony, and
has his best girl with him. and his hair elegantly
plastered in Corinthian convolutions on his scanty
forehead. In short, “The Midnight Mail ” makes
a sure, popular appeal at 35., 2s. and Is., and that
is manager Anderson’s purpose in life. The stage
carpentering is excellent, and the chief sensational
scene—the attempted wreck or robbery of the
midnight mail—well carried out.
* * * *
About the only notable opportunity given
by the Midnight Mail to the company falls to
Bert Bailey as Billy Brewer, half a clown and
half a villain, whose drunken sententiousness
adorns his rascality as a pumpkin vine does a
rubbish heap. Mr. Bailey as a comedian is often
tiresome ; in this part he shows a good capacity as
a character actor. Miss Eugenie Duggan gives a
forceful reading of a traditional heroine, and
Misses Ada Guildford and Ida Gresham do very
well in smaller parts. Among the men George
Cross, as a hero who is virtuous when he is sober,
but is not always sober, and C. R. Stanford do
best with the little fat that is left after B. Bailey
has had his fill.

mothers.

hasn’t seen a magician of note since the days of
Dante.

E resent. The supply of specimen stone discovered
y manager Rickards when last he drove north

vision of beauty.

The second Saturday night of “Beauty
and the Barge ” was another crowded occasion at
Melbourne Princess’s, when the house roared more
spontaneously than it had laughed a week earlier.
Nevertheless the joke of W. W. Jacobs is about to
be withdrawn to make way for “ The Walls of
Jericho.” The good points of the present per-
formance improve on acquaintance. Captain
Barley. Mrs. Baldwin, Mrs. Porter (the raddled,
peroxided peri of the pub), Tom Codd, who per-
forms an extravagant fit of indisposition for
£5; likewise the ingenuous Ethel and her
sudden young man these are characters
who invite credence. The pantomimic spouse
of Mrs. Porter (played by John Paulton)
simply capers around in Open defiance of proba-
bility, whilst the paternal Majah and his polite
attendants are the tamest creatures of fiction.
There is nothing to be said for the Major’s party,
except that they appear Wet when they come in
out of the alleged rain, “ The Walls of Jericho,”
due on Saturday, is a substantial play, and in this
second production of the Brough-Flemming man-
agement Herbert Flemming will face a Melbourne
audience again after a 13 years’ disappearance.
Also, Miss Beatrice Day will come under local
observation for the first time.
*& * *
The Nance O’Neil season has caught a
chill at Her Majesty’s, Melbourne, otherwise
“Ingomar” would have been favorably received
by a much larger audience last Saturday. “In-
gomar ” is a quaint old r omance that comes easy
to any crowd of actors who cart flourish their arms
and are not disposed to hide their voices under a
bushel. The citizens of Massilia date back to a
misty period when there probably wasn’t
much difference in tone between a bare-
legged son of culture and an outer bar-
barian. In Miss Maria Lovell’s play the
barbarians are all bachelors as far as the
dialogue explains them—loud, hairy bachelors who
shake gigantic dice in a primitive soup-tureen and
stake strange articles of wearing apparel upon the
hazard of the throw. The Massilians are likewise
noisy and weird-looking in the male sex, but they
have a female population on haild, and the nicest
young citizeness rushes forth into the adjacent
forest where the barbarians come from, and offers
to be their slave in lieu of her captured father.
The aged parent is so absurdly useless that the
chief barbarian falls in with Parthenia’s proposal,
and is lost. Henceforth he is the captive and she
the benevolent despot who washes his pannikin
and prepares him for matrimony. Everybody
should see “Ingomar” once. It belongs to a
dwindling collection of dramatic curios.
Miss Nance O’Neil as Parthenia, hand-
some, earnest, not very graceful in gesture or
dainty in speech, needs a thoroughgoing Ingoma
to provide a sufficient contrast, and she gets him m
John Glendinning. The rest of the company
don't matter much ; where they do matter tneie is
little to complain of. Well staged and vigorously
acted, “ Ingomar ” should gather patronage as it
rolls along for its six nights. On baturciay a
revival of “ Trilby ” will he submitted to the iick.e
populace, with Miss 0 Neil as the hypnotised
spinster who “possessed all the virtues except the
one that plays the name-part,” and Gaston Mer-
vale as the Hebrew musician who looked into her
mouth and saw a fortune in the dome thereof.

At Melbourne Royal a change of bill will
shortly overtake the chronic inebriate who fishes
for blue cod and pink eels in “ The Flood Tide.
Bland Holt’s next intention is “Woman and
Wine,” a warm spectacular drama which lives up
to its title every time it is revived. A Japanese
ballet in the 3rd act is one of its gaudiest features,
and this time the Bicknells are to introduce their
whirlwind dancing into the ballet.

At the Opera House a bright programme
last week hardly suited the taste of some people,
who like much salt with their vaudeville. George
Fuller Golden is particularly acceptable to the
other people. He gives new humor and expres-
sion to a familiar form of entertainment, dropping
his best jokes with studied negligence in prefer-
ence to hurling them at his audience’s head.
Nothing is overdone or underdone. His gentle
mimicry of the orthodox parson would win a
gracious smile from the Bishop of Melbourne if
that dignified eurer of souls could be tempted into
Rickards’. Nat Travers is the latest re-arrival,
and Nathaniel, perhaps, supplies the necessary
dash of salt.

. Yan Biene appeared at Adelaide Royal for
four nights, and every evening of the lot was wet.
Business was only moderate, and so was the play,

This passionate communication from
“ Yarrab ” has just arrived, and has had water
poured over it to mitigate the fervor of the com-
bustion :
Anent Ibsen and a criticism from Munsey’s Magazine
(B. 6/7/’O5). It would seem as though the American
monthly, taking an incident and enlarging upon it, would
endeavor to make the generic subservient to one small
detail. Therein the whole Ibsen theme is missed. Essen-
tially a symbolist, his very vividness in that respect oft
causes him to lose beauty of expression in his fixed de-
termination to achieve perfection in the moulding of his
characters. His dramas reflect, mirror-like, the whole of
Nature in its multitudinous phases, its passions and
emotions, its fallacies and facts. Intensely human, this
fighting- Norwegian has striven —and no modern drama-
tist has approximated him in knowledge of the mundane
temperament—to preserve the logical sequence of our
creation. “The stage,” he says, “ should be like a room
with one wall down ’’—through which w r e can view the
picture. Thus are all his pieces character-symphonies,
one unit fitting into the other, each and the whole sym-
bolised. He is to the drama what Wagner is to music,
and, like the Great Teuton, he had to fight for his exist-
ence ; but the strength, the grimness, and the force of
the man triumphed. Though he is best known for his
social dramas—those with a didactically rebellious theme
—he is also dear to the lover of Nature by reason of his
romantic and historical plays, all of which throb with the
heart beats of Nature. A great French writer truly
summed him up as “ the greatest psychological dramatist
since Racine. Still, tht idealogical factor is, perhaps, the
summum bonum of the Ibsen play ; the symbols are classi-
fied, and the logical determination arrived at; and, natu-
rally sceptical and polemic, he delves deep into the social
code and intensely battles with the conservative idea.
Retribution is an uncertain beast, but it
generally catches up with a man some time or
other. It has reached J. W. M‘Cay rather earlier
than usual. He was hounded out of State poli-
tics on account of his honorable pro-Boer senti-
ments; but, unable to resist the temptation of
office, he forswore his Protectionist principles,
and joined the very men who had “ pro-Boered ”
him out-of State politics. He lost his principles
when he joined Reid, and now Reid has brought
down on him a whole barrow-load of trouble.
That thinnest of all Reid’s political tricks, the
Grov.-General’s speech, mentioned but one mea-
sure, and that one promised to rob M‘Cay of his
electorate, for there is no room for Corinella on the
new electoral map. And the result of the speech
has robbed him of office and everything else.
The Wild Cat Column.
Premier Morgan (Q.) says he is not going to get
deeper into debt, and that as the only way of providing-
necessary public works he proposes to sell State lands.
The old Philp crowd also sold the lands, but it didn’t build
any works with the proceeds ; it used them as revenue,
and at the same time borrowed from Cohen all that Cohen
would lend. Morgan’s proposal is, at least, better than
that. The sale of lands is pretty certain to lead to
trouble by-and-bye; but, seeing that the old crowd
heaped on Queensland the greatest debt per head on
earth, and loaded the State with the world’s cham-
pion interest bill per head, it is hard work to avoid
trouble of some kind. In the past 12 months
the whole revenue, including land sales, only amounted
to £3,595,399, and Cohen called for £1,547,091 of that, or
over 43 per cent, of the total. Moreover, much more than
half of the greatest debt on earth isn’t earning interest.
In the past year, when the railways had an unusually
good time, they earned only £596,483 over working
expenses; harbor dues, &c., brought in gross £18,511;
loans to local bodies, &c., probably another £ll,OOO. So
far as can be traced, nothing else in which the greatest
debt on earth has been sunk earns anything. With
£1,547,091 of interest to pay, the loan undertak-
ings therefore earned in an unusually good year
£625,000. In the previous year they earned (Oogh-
lan’s figures) only £587,928. Last year £922,000
of interest had to be paid out of taxation—l2s.
in every £ of interest on the huge debt which the old
crowd fastened on to the country. Further, the old
crowd sold land wholesale, and rioted with the proceeds,
and left its successors less rent to collect. Now the new
crowd has to face that situation.
All the same, if Queensland can’t walk straight,
on account of the sins of its fathers, it ought to walk as
little crooked as possible ; and, if land must be sold, the
proceeds should be passed into a Public Works Fund. The
purchase-money isn’t revenue; it is capital; and the
accountant who mixes revenue and capital is on the road
to ruin. Provided that the land assets are merely ex-
changed for railway assets, and not used to pay current
expenses and to save taxes and fair charges for services,
the roughest edge of land sales is smoothed off. There
will still be other rough edges, but the worst of them can
also be smoothed off with a progressive land-tax. The
first absolutely necessary thing is to establish the Public
Works Fund, and to earmark for it the entire proceeds of
land sales.
Australian States, as a rule, juggle with land
revenue, and distinguish as little as possible between
proceeds of sales and rental receipts. Latest and most
complete information on this point is published by the
Westralian Statist in that State’s “Statistical Register”
for 1903. Figures for all States are, on that authority
Sales. Rents.
N.S. Wales .. £1,100,549 £770,142
Victoria .. .. 251,685 104,577
Queensland ~ 258,761 351,519
S. Australia .. 36,723 123,127
Westralia .. 28 399 162,501
Tasmania.. .. 41,319 30,609
Totals .. £1,717,436 £1,542,475
If Queensland continues at this rate, and earmarks every-
penny of land sale money for its Works Fund, it would;
have from this source, at present rates, a quarter million-
a year to spend on works ; and a quarter million will,,
anyhow, spoil the notion that No-more-borrowing means
No-more-progress. Also, to make dead-sure that the-
land shall not be jobbed away too rapidly,,
and that the projected works shall be con-
sidered hard before they are undertaken, and watched,
hard while they are being undertaken, Queensland ought.
to resolutely set itself to put at least £1 into the Works
Fund from revenue for every £1 got from land sales.
Queensland isn’t a heavily-taxed country by any means,,
and it should do this even if it has to increase taxation.
When a concern makes a supreme mess of things, it isn’t,
too much to expect it, when it comes to its senses, to.
make considerable sacrifices in order to recover its posi-
tion and its good name; and Queensland hasn’t done
anything extra in the way of sacrifice so far. A whole lot
of people, in fact, seem to have been a good deal more
concerned about restoring the Queensland National Bank
than the State of Queensland. In 1904 total revenue per
head was for each State:—
Westralia .. .. .. £l5 12 7
N. Territory .. .. .. 13 16 7
N.S. Wales 7 17 2
Queensland .. .. .. 6 18 1
S. Australia .. .. .. 6 17 11
Victoria .. .. .. 6 10
Tasmania .. .. .. 4 16 1
This is exclusive of local government taxation. Queens-
land and S. Australia have about an equal system of local
government; and yet, though S. Australia never got into
such a bog as Queensland is in, and though the propor-
tion of wage-earning males in Queensland is much higher
than in S. Australia, yet the two States are practically on
the same mark in the matter of taxation per head. It is
up to Queensland to give over thinking of itself as a State
out of which the last ounce is being already taken.

* * * *
The electric plant of the Federal Palace Hotel,
corner Collms and King-streets, Melbourne, furnishes a
good example of a thoroughly successful electric installa-
tion, in technical as well as in commercial respects. This
plant generates all electric current required for the hotel
lighting ; it consists of a twenty horse-power gas-engine
belted to a “ Ganz ” twelve kilowatts dynamo, a “ Ganz ”
switch-board containing all the measuring and controlling
instruments, and a storage battery with 62 cells. The
generating machinery runs continually during the day'
time at its full capacity and highest efficiency, the surplus
current being stored into the battery ; the latter supplies
all necessary electricity required during the night; and it
forms also a substantial reserve in case of accidents.
Compared with the previous method of working, a saving
of £4OO per annum has been effected by this plant, the
contractors for which were the Austral Otis Engineering
Company, Ltd., corner Hanah and Kavanagh-streets,
South Melbourne, as sole Australian agents for Ganz and
Budapest, Hungary.*

The Broken Hill Proprietary Block 14 Co. has
finally closed its extensive smelting and ore-reduction
works at Port Adelaide, and has placed the whole of the
machinery and plant in the hands of Messrs. Butterworth
Handcoek and Co., Port Adelaide, for private sale. The
list covers engines, boilers, steam-pumps, lifts, elevators,
crusher, brick-making machines, weigh-bridges, ore
scales, iron chimneys, shafting, pulleys, piping, and a lot
of sundries. A post card to that firm will ensure an
extended catalogue by return of post.*

• * * *
The British Broken Hill Silver Mining Co.,
after careful consideration of all- types of boilers, have
placed an order with Babcock and Wilcox for three large
boilers, each of 600 h.p. Superheaters, chain grate
mechanical stokers and economiser have also been
ordered from the same firm. This now makes 40 boilers
of the Babcock and Wilcox type supplied to the various
mines and industries at Broken Hill. Wherever economy
in fuel and maintenance of boiler has to be seriously con-
sidered by competent engineers, the Babcock boiler is
installed. Hence the recent unanimous decision of the
Expert Admiralty Committee to install Babcock boilers as
the standard for the British Navy.*

The Wilfley Slime Table which has been in
operation on the Ivanhoe Gold Mines for some months
past has been giving extremely' good results. Mr. H. T
Brett, Metallurgist of the Ivanhoe Gold Corporation, in an
article contributed to the March number of the Chamber
of Mines Journal, W.A., gives particulars of the savings
made, and states that the introduction of the Wilfley
Slime Table will be of great service in treating the low-
grade sulphide ores of the field. Since the future of
Kalgoorlie is so intimately bound up with the most
economical solution of this sulphide problem, this
assurance from Mr. Brett is a most important matter.
The Austral Otis Engineering Co., South Melbourne, are
sole manufacturers for Australasia of this table.*

The strength, lightness, durability and low cost
of spiral rivited pipe have given it a place of superiority
above any other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting strength, and is protected, inside and out, with a
perfect coating manufactured from asphalt. Asphalt
coated steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 y'ears without
any sign of decay, and may last as long again. Spiral
pipe is"manufactured by Mephan Ferguson, at Wanganui,
New Zealand, and at his engineering and pipe works,
Melbourne, where the latest up-to-date machinery is also
used for making boilers, fluming, refrigerators, bolts, nuts,
and every discrintion of cast or wrought iron work.*

Oroya-Brown Hill, Kalgoorlie, was estimated
to have 208,900 tons of 39 - Bdwt. ore in reserve on January
Ist, but results have not borne this out. May return
only averaged 28’7dwt. It has been remarked os a
peculiar thing about last year’s accounts that nothing is
charged against development. But that is easily ex-
plained—no development was done. On Brown Hill
leases it was proved years ago that no pay ore existed
below No. 5 level, and all ore above that level of payable
grade was worked out long ago. A lot of unpayable ore was
left in stopes, and, since acquisition of Oroya blocks, has
being mixed with Oroya rich ore to maintain tonnage.
All the pay pipe in Oroya north block has been rooted out
except about 40ft. adjoining Brown Hill boundary. When
that is depleted there will be a collapse. The pipe was
developed and depleted simultaneously by incline winzes
of about one in four, which practically stoped out the
whole pipe. It looks as if mana>nnent’s intention is to
pig-root balance of pay shoot in Oroya, and it is probably
time to get in out of the wet. Of course, there is Oroya
south block, but that contains only low-grade ore in bulk.
Future developments should be worth watching.

assets which the old spendthrift crowd loaded this bank
with, and it seems probable that the hank has done more
than this. If it hasn’t, or if the assets haven’t recovered
wonderfully, the idea of paying l a div. even eight years
hence ought to have been hit hard.
These figures give, however, some idea of how
things are moving :
June, 1904. June, 1905. Increases.
New private deposits ..£1,109,007 £1,436,520 £327,513

The large increase in deposits is a pretty good sign of
returning confidence in the Q.N.' For many years back
Queensland has been steadily pouring its money into
banks with head-offices outside Queensland —often outside
Australia. Probably the fact that the Q.N. didn’t reduce
its deposit-rates recently is also turning 1 the scale a little
in its favor. Anyhow, there has been a remarkable in-
crease of deposits ; but there hasn’t been finy increase of
advances to make up for it and earn the extra interest
with. Liquid assets represent as much as 72 p.c. of all
new liabilities to the public ; so the bank will probably be
glad to see some good securities come into the premises.
The disclosed profits on shareholders’ funds—capital
(£413,226) and reserves—have moved lately this way :
Dec., 1901 .. £l3 411 p.c. Dec., 1903 .. £9 15 3 p.c.
June, 1902 .. 11 14 4 „ June, 1904 .. 914 4 „
Dec., 1902 .. 10 3 0 „ Dec., 1904 .. 913 0 „
June, 1903 .. 915 6,, June, 1905 .. 9IS 5 „
But it has to be remembered that the bank has £1,377,563
of Government money at £2 12s. 6d. per cent., and
£942,874 of old depositors’ money carrying no interest at
all. If the former carried an extra 1 per cent., and the
latter only carried 3 per cent., they would call for £42,061
a year, which would just about wipe present reported
profits out, and leave the earnings at freezing point, or
thereabouts.
As to the progress made in removing the
ancient dead-horse from the Q.N.’s premises:—
1897. June, 1905. Reduction.
Government deposits
(reconstructed) .. £1,833,327 £1,377,563 £455,764
Interminable stock

tors) 3,116,621 3,116,621 Nil

The debt to old depositors, the third item above, is ex-
tinguishable only out of profits ; and when half the debt
to the State is paid, 3£ per cent. div. may be declared,
according to the very newest arrangement. But, while
that makes things a bit worse for these old depositors,
they are getting £9OOO a half-year, which is twice as
much as they formerly got, so the newest arrangement
has been accepted without any kicking. Also the State is
getting £86,097 per half-year instead of £3OOO. No
doubt, with these payments to make, the bank will
have to maintain a heavy reserve of liquid assets, to
draw on when it can’t conveniently realise securities ;
at the same time this idea of paying a tidy sum off year
by year, instead of postponing the process fora number
of years and then allowing the whole trouble to drop in
at once, is to be commended. The old crowd was a whale
for postponing trouble ; the new arrangement represents
the new idea of humping some of the trouble ourselves,
and not passing the whole of it on to the infant to wrestle
with by-and-bve.

On account of its up-north agricultural connection, this
bank has an “off season,” when the gentleman who is
alleged to want the Kanaka is living on his last year’s
crop, and doesn’t come along for advances to any great
extent; and, for an “ off season,” the past half-year has
been a bit better than the recent average. But the
figures, like those of most other banks, show generally
that the country has for once been having a rather better
time than the banks. Compared with the corresponding
part of 1904, advances have jn this case certainly moved
up a trifle, but deposits have moved up still more, with
the result that the bank has £21,500 more liquid assets
waiting for somebody to borrow. The position compares
with that of 12 months back in this way :
June, 1904. June, 1905. Increases.
Deposits .. .. £270,193 £300,056 £29,863
Bills payable, &c. .. 58,666 61,423 2,757
Liquid assets .. .. 110,155 131,655 21,500
Advances .. .. 312,829 325,486 12,657
Premises .. .. 25,243 25,763 520
The liquid assets are equal to nearly 37 per cent, of all
liabilities to the public—probably a good deal larger pro-
Sortion than safety demands. Of the entire assets share-
olders’ funds represent 25 per cent. Securities against
advances would have to depreciate 37 per cent, before
shareholders’ funds would disappear and the public’s
deposits, &c., would be affected, which is an unusually
strong position from a depositor’s point of view. Net
profits are, however, comparatively low ; on shareholders’
funds the rate per annum has recently been :
June, 1902 ..£3 12 1 p.c. June, 1904 ..£3 16 11 p.c.
Dec., 1902 .. 313 7 „ Dec., 1904 .. 4 2 0 „
June, 1903 .. 314 8 „ June, 1905 .. 316 0 „
Dec., 1903 .. 4 3 0 „
The expenses of running a small business with numerous
branches must be relatively high ; and this, and the large
proportion of liquid assets maintained, account for the
smallness of the figures. It earns good money on the
business which it does, but it pays an extra large propor-
tion of that money away in pursuing the small account
in scattered places where the expenses are necessarily
high. The dividend is among the smallest paid by Aus-
tralian banks ; on the other hand, the fact that it
absorbs but 60 per cent, of the profits, the other 40 per
cent, being laid by, is evidence of prudent management.

A very desirable result ot some recent proceedings is seen
in the directors’ statement that a record number of
estates and trusts have been placed under the co.’s
control during the half-year. This co. has a paid capital
of £90,000, and shareholders are liable for a further
£210,000; and that is a tolerably solid guarantee of
honesty—anyway, that clients will not suffer through
anybody’s dishonesty. Further, the Trustee Co. isn’t
formed for a day or a year ; it proposes to go on being a
trustee indefinitely, and that is a further guarantee of
straight-dealing. Also it publishes its balance-sheets,
which is some additional g uarantee. And on top of all this
is the fact that its business is to be a trustee ; and
that means, or ought to mean, some skill in a
business which, for some extraordinary reason,
had fallen into the hands of amateurs. When
it hadn’t fallen into the hands of a friend of the
family, it had mostly fallen into the hands of the family
solicitor, and in his dual capacity the family solicitor often
made a mess of things. He was and is usually a very good
man to watch somebody else ; but for watching himself
he wasn’t and isn’t invariably to be trusted. The trustee
co. never goes to races, and doesn’t play nap, or take a
trip abroad ; and these are points in its favor. The pro-
gress of this old-established co. is therefore to be noted
with more satisfaction than usual. These further particu-
lars show how it is moving :
June, Dec., June,
1895. 1904. 1905.
Commission, etc £4383 £8,634 £8,909
Interest 2115 807 810
Expenses 5730 5,545 5,685
Profit on Funds, p.c. ..£3 910 £6 18 3 £7 111
Trust Estates .. .. 7,881,720 7,787,043
Expenses p.c. of Trust Es-
tates 2/10 2/11
The co.’s funds are represented mainly by freeholds
£65,000, Vic. Government stock £10,300, Melb. Board of
Works debentures £12,000, and mortgages and fixed
deposits. It has been remarked ■on previous occasions
that the return was small, and it looked as though
some writing-down was called for. Profit and
loss account now, however, shows the old item
“ Co.’s premises ” as “ Rents from offices in
co.’s building not used for co.’s business.” In other
words, co. does not, it seems,, charge itself with rent
for its own office space and pay this rent into the
interest account. This makes the interest return
look a little smaller than it really is. Also it makes the
expense rate look a little smaller than it really is.
But so far as persons interested in trust estates are
concerned, it wouldn’t be a good sign, in any case, if the
co. showed large earnings. They want absolute security
from their trustee ; and that can best be got through that
trustee putting its guarantee mainly in gilt-edged invest-
ments, which is the course this office is adopting with
regard to most of its savings.

The Co-operative Wool and Produce Co.
(Sydney)

The bonus to clients is at the rate of 20 p.c. on amounts
received from them aswool-selling commission, and is paid
to shareholders and non-shareholders alike. Even after
making this rebate and paying a reasonable 6 p.c. divid-
end, the profits allow a very substantial addition to re-
serves, which have already reached nearly 50 p.c. of the
capital (£22,292). The financial position shows generally
a solid improvement. Twelve months ago there were
net overdrafts of £17,181, which have now been reduced
to £9175, or £BOO6 less—partly through increased profits,
partly through reduced outstanding accounts, which
amount to only £5019. After payment of present div.
(half has already been distributed as an interim) and bonus,
the overdraft may show up larger ; still, even then, there
will be an excellent movement the right way. Against
shareholders’ funds and liabilities to the public the co.
holds premises valued at <641!,604. Practically nothing is,
however, written off this ; total charge for depreciation is
£llO, and that has to cover furniture, plant, and every-
thing. This item will need attention. Gross earnings
were £13,167, against £11,558 in 1904 ; yet general work-
ing expenses, which cover everything but directors’ fees
(a very moderate £406), insurance, and rents, amounted
this year to only £6973 against £8136 last year. This
looks like strict economy ; and the Co-op. is the sort of
concern in which part at least of the results come back
to the people whose business the co. looks after.

Broken Hill mines are at present employing,
apart from smelting and flux-raising and coke-making,
7343. men, which is the field’s record. In the early ’9o’s a
couple of years showed a larger total, but that included
the smelting works, which had not then been removed
from the mine-leases.
B.H. Junction North’s new manager is T. H.
Palmer, who has been for a brief while assistant to the
general manager of Block 10. It is a tough problem for a
neophyte to tackle. Palmer is Sydney University-
trained—a son of the broking firm which issues a monthly
circular about the imaginary horrors of Watson’s banking
proposals.
* * * *
“Reader” disputes that the B.H. Junction
North magnetic machines have proved a failure: —“The
Junction North plant is a combination of a wet jigging
and vanning plant and a dry magnetic re-treatment plant,
and is quite capable of treating a sufficient quantity to
make the mine pay well, and of making a higher re-
covery of metals than a wet plant alone could make.
Anyhow it could make good profits out of the middle
product, which other Broken Hill mines except the
Central (Sulphide Oorp.) are throwing away. There is
nothing wrong about the Junction North except the
management. The ore reserves are such as the adjoin-
ing North, which has lost through the underlie what the
Junction North has got, must be very jealous of. Very
likely the North would like to snap up this mine as it
did the Victoria, which adjoins, and which at present
promises to become its mainstay, according to the way its
northern shoot is dipping into the property.” But the
fact remains that the plant, even when run by the
engineer of the Australian Metal Co., who designed it,
was not able to show a profit.
* * * *
B.H. Block 14 opens an office in London, and
thus increases the opportunities for gambling in this
stock. Shareholders have also sanctioned the borrowing
of £20,000. With this, and the zinc concentrates, and the
rooting out of what little carbonate ore is left, and the
recent unimportant strike of a patch of rich ore, the co.
should again reach the profit-making stage without grub-
bing into shareholders’ pockets.

From Burraga (N.S.W.), where the Lloyd
Copper Co.’s mine is : —“ It is true that the Lloyd Co. had
an overdraft of £12,673 when it held its last meeting ; but
it also bad £29,177 of metal afloat. Don’t know if the
mine is much good, or if the shareholders are likely to get
anything out of it, or what had been drawn against the
metal in transit ; but, anyway, looking at the thing from
a local man’s point of view, I know it is paying away
between £7OOO and £BOOO in wages monthly, and that the
mine is supporting nearly 2000 people. So here’s better
luck to it ! ”
* * * *
Nuggetty Gully, on the old Turon (N.S.W.)
field, has been dug up again. A Sydney co. formerly put
up a good 15-head mill, and in a slap-dash way ran
through dirt whether it was gold-bearing or not; then
sat down dazed ac the natural result, and left some people
waiting to be paid. New concern doesn’t seem to have
enough capital to give the thing a fair trial; battery has
been mortgaged already, and the little that has been
raised on that pawning transaction is being put into very
necessary development work.
* * • •
Chief N.S.W. Inspector of Mines Jacquet’s un-
reserved report that Mt. Boppy auriferous deposit is an
“ inverted saddle reef ” opens up an interesting problem
for mining: men. Experts like Frecheville and Clemes,
both men who carried large salaries, as well as the repu-
tation of John Taylor and Sons, after Spending weeks
examining Mt. Boppy for the London holders, just as un-
reservedly designated it a fault. Frecheville was emphatic
on this ; Clemes allowed that it might be an elbow, or a
bend, or a fault. Also, past mine managers White and
Davey, and present boss Pascoe, all qualified men, and
with an experience of most of the world’s known reef-
formations, called it a fault. In face of this evidence,
which might be called weighty, Jacquet, after a brief
examination, confidently tells his Department that fault
is a misnomer, and it’s an inverted saddle reef. Problem
is certainly interesting, and calls for argument.
* * * *
Queen Bee Copper (N.S.W.), referred to last
week as suffering from dual management and a lack of
development in sulphide zone, has reconsidered its
policy and wisely decided to place control in mine mana-
ger’s hands. Almost simultaneously with this announce-
ment comes the news of the much-wanted sulphides being
struck in crosscut on the 370 ft. level; but so far values
are undetermined. Month’s return, just to hand, esti-
mated at 40 tons, only reached 38 tons, which, to say the
least, is suggestive that erection of No. 3 reverb, was
rushed. Sulphide strike may now, however, justify it;
but unless something better than 40 tons copper can be
got monthly, mine’s best effort will be threepenny divi-
dends.
< * #
C.S.A., an old-time and long-abandoned Cobar
copper venture which a few of the best-known Cobar
men lately resurrected, has given some encouragement
already. Old shaft has been re-timbered to bottom level
—something like 300 ft.—and thereabouts they have
struck lead carbonates, and indications are considered
promising. Syndicate has £3OOO to spend, and it’s in
good hands ; but the mine, from surface to present level,
has always given promising indications, and never any-
thing, more substantial, so that, in spite of the good
management, too much confidence would be still a mis-
take. Policy now is to get down where old-timers left
off, and chances favor a payable sulphide body. All the
same, C.S.A. is not only a gamble pure and simple, but it
hasn’t even the plums that most copper shows carry in
the upper levels.
» * * •
Young Australia, an old-time producer of much
gold, on tlie long line of Cobar’s auriferous helt, gets unde-
servedly forgotten. Area is considerable; further, there’s a
20-head stamp mill not yet obsolete, and a reservoir with a
couple of years’ supply. Deposits of mine, like those of
its neighbors, the Western and Occidental, are proved
through the oxidised ores right to the copper zone, and
both are known to contain stuff that should now be pay-
able. So far mine hasn’t had a chance to establish itself
on a fair basis, which is the only reason it isn’t estab-
lished ; but as soon as Cobar people find time to
give serious consideration to the things near home,
there won’t be any further slump in Young Australia.
It’s near at home, a good mine, and, like the Occidental,
should scatter its divs. locally instead of throughout the
land of J. Bull.
* * * «
Myall and Peak Hill United (N.S.W.), under
manager James Hocking, gradually moves into larger
production. It is not long since the Hill was secured by
the Myall people. It failed dismally under battery treat-
ment ; but being a porous, low-grade, gossany ore, it
yields itself to the rolls, and as total costs of treatment
under this method don’t exceed ss. per ton, there is
already a monthly profit coming out of the cyanide vats.
So far, co. has only 10 vats in position, with a total out-
put of 2500 tons monthly ; but directors (J. Bull and co.)
contemplate duplicating these, bringing output to 5000
tons. Myall, which is a dead-weight Peak Hill certainly
can’t carry, is to be abandoned, and machinery passed on
to Peak Hill. This move should have been made before.
Peak Hill has enough gold and is big enough to absorb
all the available energy—though, to satisfy all the share-
holders in a 250,000-share co., there isn’t likely to be any
energy to spare.
* » * •
For first half of 1905, W.A. mines have produced
965,3730 z. fine gold against 984,6150 z. for first half of 1904,
a shortage of 19,2420 z. For second half of 1904 output
was 998,6150 z., an average of 166,4000 z. monthly, figures
which are quite unlikely to be realised this year. It
seems safe to predict a shortage of quite 50,0000 z. on the
year.
* * * *
Great Boulder (W.A.), as result of further
development, is proving that its 1900 ft. level, which was
for nearly nine months under a cloud, is exceptionally
good. After driving 90ft. north, lode widened to 10ft.,
worth 36dwt. ; from 90ft. to 120 ft. it averaged 10ft., worth
47dwt. This puts a different complexion on mine and
field generally, as bears had been arguing that gold had
petered out. Had any energy been displayed, this dis-
covery should have been made last October, as driving
40ft. to 50ft. weekly can easily be done ; yet Boulder has
done 10ft. a month only. Boulder treatment costs con-
tinue ridiculously high, being 14s. Id. for 1904 against
Ivanhoe’s 9s. 10d., Oreat Fingall’s 7s. lid., and Sons of
Gwalia’s ss. 3d.
* » * *
Associated, Kalgoorlie, which has now been in
charge of G. M. Roberts for about three months, looks
much more hopeful than it has done for years. Roberts
was positive that Oroya shoot had never been found and
that it was east of No. 10 level. His theory has proved
correct, and a rich shoot has now been located 435 ft. south
of Oroya boundary, and about 40ft. below 10 level. For a
width of about Soft, ore is worth 2oz. This is best
development Associated has had for a long time, and quite
alters prospects of mine, as co. has possibility of 1600 ft.
in length of rich ore. Other developments have also
been satisfactory. Alterations to treatment should be
completed by end of year ; co. is now earning profits of
£2500 to £3900, besides spending £3OOO a month on
alterations to plant.
* * * *
Princess Royal Central, Norseman (W.A.),
which has hitherto been rather disappointing, has had a
good development. Some time ago driving north and
south at 950 ft. was commenced, and good values were
looked for in south drive, where chief work was concen-
trated. However, nothing better than 6dwt. was got,
although reef averaged Bft. But in north drive, ore worth
25 to 30dwt., and sft. wide, has been cut, and, if this con-
tinues, outlook for mine is fairly rosy. Manager Hender-
son has displayed great energy in pushing on develop-
ments.
* * • •
Princess Royal (W.A.) looks as if it still had a
slight chance. In driving south at 50ft. good ore has
been met, as all along anticipated ; but trend of lode is
carrying it near boundary of Central, and con-
sequently life of the mine is limited. Shareholders
made a fatal mistake when they vetoed the
purchase of Central blocks, and so lost an opportunity
never likely to recur. Unless costs given by ex-manager
Ridgway before Arbitration Court can be materially
reduced, there is no chance of any divs. June return
shows that 1900 short tons, worth 475., were treated, at
cost of 55s.—a loss of Bs. a ton, or about £750. Isolation
of Norseman, and erratic nature of its reefs, appear to
make cheap costs out of question ; and proposed railway
seems as far off consummation as ever, as experts allege
that outlook is not sufficiently encouraging. Judging
from current prospects, experts seem about right.
» * * *
North Kalgurli (W.A.) has at last closed down
its mill, and chances of a revival seem remote. A good
deal of vain prospecting has been done, and, as col is still
financial, furtherjjqgsicking is proceeding, but outlook is
pretty ln upper levels country is much dis-
turbed, and lode broken and contorted, and in more
settled country nothing payable has been met with. On
several occasions hopes of Kalgurli and Hainault shoots
entering the property have been held out ; but, un-
fortunately, these shoots have invariably petered out
before reaching the boundary. Recently accumulated
slimes have enabled co. to continue, but they are about
done.
* • * *
We&tralia Waihi doesn’t improve. Last three
months returns show 28s. 4d., 245. 5d., and 18s. sd. per
ton, with a resultant profit of £lOO, and losses of £lOO
and £450. Previous official estimates were thus worse
than valueless. Co.’s only visible hope is that eastern
reef discovered by present manager Watts may pan out
satisfactorily when cut in crosscut from 280 ft. level. If
that proves a frost, outlook seems hopeless.

SOCIETY
FROM THE NORTH.
[For The Bullet i n.]
“Japan will construct a new Chinese Navy.”—Cable-
gram.
Can you hear the distant throbbing- ? ’Tis the sound of
Asian drums.
’Tis the tramp of Manchu legions, and the war-songs of
Japan.
’Tis the eerie, far-off murmur, e’er the roaring tempest
comes,
Of the men who once went Westward—in the days of
Genghis Khan.
Do you see the flash and sparkle on the hills to northward
there ?
'Tis the glint of bit and bayonet—’tis the kiss of steel and
sun ;
'Tis the leaping of the lightning through the tense,
expectant air,
Just before the thunders reach us from the deep-
mouthed six-inch gun.
They have wrongs that they remember—they have debts
they long to pay :
Even now the swords are sharpening in the subtle, silent
East;
And they fear the White no longer, do those myriads of
Cathay.
Do you see the eagles wheeling as they gather to the
feast ?
There is talking in Tokio, there are whispers in Pekin ;
There are thoughts of lust and plunder, there are
thoughts of ancient scores.
They have portioned out already all the land they hope
to win,.
And our country lies in slumber—with the Mongol at her
doors.
Ji-L- Taiwa.

This night we celebrate the anniversary of the battle of
the Boyne, fought 215 years ago.
S.M. Herald next day -
Yesterday, being the 12th of July, the anniversary of
the battle of the Boyne.
These people should really look up their history
books and find out what battle they are celebrat-
ing, and when the Boyne scrimmage happened.
The great aggregate Constable Pecksniff,
of Melbourne, lately haled a bookseller before the
beaks for exposing “indecent pictures” in his
window. Said pictures were price-ticket cards,
showing damsels getting their shoes polished and
exposing portion of a neat calf the while, a couple
of babies dressed only in top boots, and so on.
He was discharged on undertaking to withdraw
the allegedly “offensive” cards. Melbourne is
getting fearfully moral, and even the spielers,
who decorate the pavement outside the Bourke-
street betting “clubs” and pour undressed dic-
tionary into the air, may yet find themselves
spoken to severely.
Three legal sharks had a great riot at
Camperdown (Vic.), lately, over a council em-
ployee whose wages they wanted to garnishee.
The victim’s monthly pay came to £B, and for an
hour two of the fish battled over that, the Bench
finally giving shark No. 1 £4 55., and grampus No.
2£3 14s. 3d. There being 9d. left, they were still
arguing about costs when whale No. 3 burst in
and put in a claim for his costs, saying he had
appeared for the employing council at some more
or less distant date. The Bench eyed the miser-
able ninepence for a space, then considered the
three learned fish, and, finally, in disgust, threw
the lot of them out.
* * * *
Vic. Premier Bent’s brutal directness of
speech has its points at times. At their annual pow-
wow over the battle of Aughrim, Melbourne
Orangemen, including Simian Phraser, Balfour,
M‘.Ketchup and Rev. Pearce Carey got excited as
usual. Rev. Carey in his anger stated that “the
man behind the Premier —the man who bent
Thomas Bent—-was the astute head of the R.C.
Church. The bricks of Mr. Bent’s policy were
not made in Bent’s brick works but in the Arch-
bishop’s kiln. It was the nod of the Archbishop
that determined the decisions of our Cabinet.”
Thomas duly noted this and rose to the occasion,
telling the reporters next day, “ A man who talks
like that is not entitled to the retort courteous,
but deserves the lie direct, and I give him the lie
direct. What he has stated is a lie ! ”
* * * *
Sydney’s bread supply is under discussion.
It is, like the supply of most other cities, “ pure ”
enough—so far as that goes. The wheat of which
it is made is first carefully treated, so that as
much as possible of the nutriment is extracted.
The balance is mostly starch, and is excellent for
making paste to stick up bills with. But it is not
used chiefly for that, but to make bread. If this
bread were properly baked, a good deal of the
starch would be converted into dextrine—a fairly
nutritious and digestible product. But that is
carefully avoided in most loaves, which are made
very thick and heavy and damp; so that the centre
is hardly cooked at all. Some bread is properly
cooked in long thin rolls, which are chiefly crust;
but that’s rare. Most bread is prepared in the
interests of the dentists and doctors. It is their
staff of life. They ought to subsidise the bakers,
but they don’t—partly out of ingratitude, and
partly from a desire not to give away the show.
Explorer Gregory was buried with the
greatest display of man millinery ever seen in
e. The deceased was Grand Autocrat of
the Most Gorgeous and Ancient Confusion of
Cheerful Giraffes that the State can produce, and
his body was preceded and followed by a host of
well-meaning persons, tricked out in sashes,
scarves, aprons, and stomach-bands, like unto the
King of the Cannibal Islands in all his glory ;
whilst little round pinnies were as common as flies
an d bad debts.

HOSTESS: “ Why, what's the matter with you, Ray? You don’t seem to be dancing."
RAY (very grown up): “ Can't stand these kid affairs —got to be so careful how you talk."
The farcical nature of the pretence of
administering the anti-gambling laws in N.S.W.
was never more clearly shown than in the State
Parliament last week, when the Chief Secretary, in
reply to a series of questions, was able to state that
there are 25 known betting shops and four “ two-
up” schools in Sydney. These places must be
known thoroughly to the law, since the law can
thus accurately census them, and they are illegal.
But nothing happens to them. The law seems to
be very blind or very sharp at seeing a hole, just
as wealthy interests dictate; and the betting-
shop and bookmaking interest in Sydney repre-
sents an annual turn-over of about £3,000,000.
* * * *
Victorian law seems to get more cursory
and accidental and honorary every day. One of
the judges recently suspended, pending good
behavior, a nine-months’ sentence on a youth,
who straightway went astray again. The prosecu-
ting inspector wanted the bonds estreated and the
delinquent sent to serve his nine months, but
somebody discovered that the sureties had never
been entered into. Then the inspector wanted
the accused committed for being illegally at large,
but the Law, after considering the matter side-
ways and endways and upside down, finally said
“ Dunno,” and discharged him. There is a fright-
ful lot of “ Dunno ” about the Law.
* * * *
The Sydney suburb of St. Peters proposes
to change its name to “ Brompton.” Brompton
is strictly Hinglish. It is the name of a region of
horrible “ gentility” which lies south of Padding-
ton (London), and north of Clapham and Bat-
tersea ; and the Halbert ’All is thereabouts, like-
wise ’Ammersmith; and the best thing that can
be said for it is that it is some distance from Toot-
ing. The ingenuity of a municipal council which
proposes to call its town Brompton is beyond all
praise ; a council like that deserves to be pre-
sented with an oleograph or taken to see “ East
Lynne.” St. Peters is a smellful suburb, and has
consequently got a bad name, and the council pro-
poses to change the name instead of changing the
smells. The fact doesn’t seem to have dawned on
it yet that people will soon notice that Brompton
smells just as St. Peters did. Then it will be
time to change the name again and call the place
Asafcetida.
* * * w
Brisbane Water Board, an interesting
mixture of fossildom and up-to-dateness, has
turned again to Stradbroke Island as a possible
source of water supply for the city. In the centre
of this island there is an extensive lake, yielding
a bountiful supply of pure water. Some years ago
the matter was mooted, but after some languid
inquiry it dropped. The drawback is that the
water will require to be carried across the bay,
and, in the case of an invasion, the service pipes
would not easily be defended. But, on the other
hand, there is too much present and late animal
on the catchment area between Brisbane and Ips-
wich, and out at the back of Ipswich ; and some
improvement is urgently required.
Maoriland is talking about dealing roughly
with its police force. It says it wants a new force :
the present one having become hopelessly de-
moralised. The debacle commenced with the con-
viction of three policemen in Dunedin for whole-
sale burglary. After that the luck of the force
seemed quite gone, for within a week it was
bowled out in half-a-dozen very bad cases. One
of the most glaring was that of a man whom it
found in an epileptic fit in the street, and charged
with being just soaked with liquor. At the court
next morning the whole watchhouse force turned
up to swear that it was a case of drink and not
epilepsy ; but it hadn’t quite done swearing to
this effect when the man corroborated his own
story by taking: another fit on the floor of the
court, and the publicity brought along evidence
which cleared him of the charge of beer. Next,
in Auckland, two young men looking for a cab
late at night, were ordered to “move on and not
obsthruct the thraffie.” But for themselves and
the copper the street was empty, so they took it
for Robert’s wit, and laughed out of a sense of
politeness. Then a cab came along and they
hailed it. Robert got in last, without any invita-
tation, and said “Police Station,” There he
charged them with being drunk and disorderly,
and in the morning a further charge of violent
conduct in the cells was brought against them. A
squad of police backed up the constable’s story,
and things looked bad. But they were able to
hire expensive counsel, and proved that if they
were drunk they must have got that way in about
two and a half minutes. The police position be-
came a rout. Later, another amazing constable
found a man lying on the pavement, and without
warning started to drag him to the station. The
man pleaded that his leg was broken, arid the
constable noted the Remark with a view to a
further charge of insulting behavior. He was
dragged to the lockup, and left in agony all night.
In the morning a doctor ordered the accused into
hospital instantly. What with one thing and
another, an awful row has started; and when you
mention perjury now such a shiver runs through
the force that some people mistake the commo-
tion for another earthquake.

The Victorian Education Department at-
tributes to bad accommodation the difficulty it
experiences in getting teachers to go into the
country, and a lady officer has been appointed to
visit each country district and stipulate that the
lady teacher shall get proper accommodation and
fruit and vegetables in her dietary. But as the
girl teacher gets only £7 to £8 per month, it will
cost her, in these circumstances, just about her
whole pay to live. Fancy a Mallee farmer in a
dry season supplying fruit and vegetables for 10s.
to 15s. per week—the usual board charge, If the
Department paid the backblocks teacher a bit
extra for facing the deadly dulness, the difficulty
might be overcome. Teachers mostly begin in
cities, but at about 23 they are sent to the bush—
after a. short spell of which they either get back to
town by hook or crook ; or else quit kicking and
mentally go to sleep.

The voice of “ Old Chum ”
An ancient North Shore (Sydney) house, historic as an
inn and as the resort of operatic and dramatic people half
a century ago, was the subject of inquiry, at the North
Sydney police court, last week. In 1843 Mr. William Dind
kept the Clown Tavern, in Pitt-street, afterwards known
as the Shakspeare Hotel. He sold out to George Coppin,
and took the Star and Garter, nearly opposite. George
Coppin in a short time gave up the Clown and went to
Van Diemen’s Land, but Dind hung on to Sydney, and in
1850 was keeping the Lily of St. Leonards, at Milson’s
Point, the “ lily ” being a black gin. The Campbells, of the
Wharf, owned a big slice of land close to Milson’s Point,
and Dind was entrusted with the cutting up and sale of
the estate. He had the privilege of selecting an allotment
for himself, and chose the N.E. corner of Fitzroy-street
and the main road, known as Lane Cove-road (“ Alfred-
street” was an invention in the Duke of Edinburgh’s
time). Here he built an inn, still known by his name.
From his connection with theatres as lessee, treasurer, &c.,
his house was the resort of theatrical and operatic people.
There is a large gallery still there of pictured
celebrities of-a former age. Recently the house was con-
demned and the license was renewed only on condition
that new premises should be erected. This new house
was built, but not on the site of the old one, and the
police summoned the lessee for selling in the new pre-
mises without a license, holding that it was not a substi-
tute for the ancient structure. To get over the difficulty
the present Dind connected the two houses with a covered
way and made them one. Still the police are not satisfied
and the S.M. is undecided. The original Dind died ten
years ago, aged 82 years.

Sydney mint passed its 50th anniversary
on May 14th. It was established in 1855, largely
owing to the advocacy of James Martin and
J. B. Darvall, in its present location, Macquarie-
street. The building is an early Sydney relic,
the mint offices having been the military hospital
of the old days. The father of W. J. Trickett,
M.L.C., was technical expert in charge, under
first deputy-master Captain Ward. Sydney mint
ante-dated that of Melbourne by 17 years, and
that of Perth by 36 years. It turned out the first
regular Australian coinage, although South
Australia issued some sovereigns in the early
60’s. They bore the royal crown on the obverse,
but had no Imperial authorisation. Prior to 1870
locally-coined gold was intended only for Austral-
asian circulation, and was stamped with the word
“Australia.” It was alloyed with silver, and in
consequence was greater in value than the present
coinage, which contains a copper alloy.
A letter from a disrespectful correspon-
dent :
There’s a good deal of the spirit of Bill Adams in N.S.W.
Teachers’ Association.
When Mr. Perry and Mr. Wise raised the small assis-
tant’s salary to a figure at which body and soul could be
partly kept together, the Association took the credit So
did Mr. Osborne, of the Public Service Association. Mr.
Osborne said if the teachers didn’t join the P.S.A. their
salaries would be lowered. Just after that Mr. Perry and
Mr. Wise further raised the salaries of the small men.
Then the Public Service Association and the Teachers’
Association bumped one another in the doorway in their
hurry to show that each had brought about that result.
Some of the members high up in the Teachers’ Associa-
tion say they have great influence in politics. I don’t
know whether this is true, but after the last election Mr.
Carruthers became Prime Minister, and his Attorney-
General has since chopped down the incomes of the
smaller teacher. The Teachers’ Association approved
this, and it and the P.S.A. didn’t bump each other in the
doorway this time.
I’ve been a member of the Association for some years,
but have lost my card of membership and so have great
difficulty in gaining admission to the meetings. There-
fore I make the following suggestions to the Association
here:—
(«) Don’t worry about Mr. Knibbs. Let him startle the
world with a great scientific discovery, or experiment
in acclimatising seagulls in Bourke, as he thinks fit.
( b ) Pass a resolution (you’re great at passing reso-
lutions) that either Judge Heydon be compelled to pay
for the cleaning of his own courthouse, or that the mean
tax imposed on teachers for the cleaning of school-rooms
be abolished.
* * * *
The other day, in a fashionable Melbourne
suburb, the principals of a “ladies’ school” with
a pretty pink name, sued to recover £8 Bs. for a
quarter’s “ board and education of two children.”
The row was whether the fees were £4 or £4 4s.
Four pounds for, say, 10 weeks feeding and train-
ing, and for the purchase of the large quantity of
frill which ladies’ school with the pretty pink
names have to wear on the outside ! One hardly
knows whether to pity most the school marm or
the kid, or what size of rock to throw at the kid’s
parent.
* » * •
Sydney D.T., last Monday, in a shocked
shriek, deprecated the idea that Australia could
possibly know as much about what is needed for
Australian naval defence as does the British
Admiralty. And a few years ago how absurd it
would have been to suggest that Australian
soldiers knew more about open-order fighting than
British Tommies !
