A.M.P. manager Teece, who has been
again delving into infant-mortality figures for the
benefit of Sydney Society
of Actuaries, might delve
a bit further for the benefit
of the Black Labor crowd
who picture Queensland as
a place where the white
man gets too limp to work,
and where the white infant
comes puling into the world
only to leave it promptly.
Australasian figures, lately
made up to the end of 1903,
show that Westralia has
absolutely the worst record;
of every 100 infants born,
14'12 die there within
12 months (1903 figures),
against 11‘99 in Queensland.
The total death rate of
Queensland is less than
that for either N.S. Wales
or Victoria, while the per-
centage of deaths under
five years to total deaths
was less in Queensland in
1902 than it was even in
Tasmania, where the Dob-
sons live, and in 1903 was
justasliadegreater. Taking
them all round, far and
away the best figures for
all Australasia are those of
S. Australia ; and S. Aus-
tralia includes the N. Terri-
tory where the European is
supposed to go only to
shrivel up.

Melbourne burglars
grow more enterprising and
successful. Some of them
broke open a suburban
butcher’s shop on a recent
Friday night and carted
away the whole stock of
meat (about £6O worth), in-
cluding the sausages, sheep’s
head, trotters, liver and
kidneys, cow-heel, ox-tail,
calf’s brains, dripping, suet, etc. They didn’t
even overlook the meat hooks and price tickets
necessary to do a thriving retail trade at “ cut
prices.” Also, they took the cleaver—in fact,
they left nothing but the block and a scrap of
cat’s meat.
Australia has not, so far, had any nigger-
roasting incidents as the price of admitting the
black and yellow man to its shores, but almost as
squalid horrors now and again spring up to show
the inevitable penalty of race-mixture. the
Maclean (N.S.W.) poisoning case is an example.
In April last a white woman, Jane Davis, was
charged with poisoning a Kanaka named Williams
at Maclean (N.S.W.). She was living with another
Kanaka named Tanna, and Williams lodged with
them. On the evidence, which was revolting, the
woman was convicted. Now, after the lapse
of some months, a crowning horror conies
in the discovery that both Kanakas, the white
woman’s paramour and her alleged victim, are
lepers, and in an advanced stage of the disease.
For years they had escaped observation, and had
even come under the fierce light of a notorious
criminal trial without being detected ; and one
had been cohabiting with a white woman. Two
choice samples of the harmless, gentle, unobtrusive
Kanaka of Senator Dobson’s affection ! Probably
they, too, were especially gentle and unobtrusive;
leprosy takes the craving for public advertisement
out of a man.

N.S.W. Public Service Association has
finally decided not to register under the N.S.W.
Arbitration Act. The reason of its hanging back
is the known antagonistic attitude ot the Lar-
ruthers Government to the arbitration principle.
The Association has 17 branches, and a member-
ship of nearly 5000, with a balance m hand ot

“ Hamer ” : The way passen-
gers have of disappearing off Maori-
land steamships creates anxiety
amongst the ships’ officers, and
when they see anyone roaming
about the deck in the mid-
night hours they are a
haggard and anxious lot.
I used to haunt
the binnacle in the
silent hours, and
was amused by
the watch set
upon me and the
way, whenever I
walked rapidly
towards the bul-
warks, somebody
edged up close.
After a whi 1 e,
when the strain of
uncertainty got
too strong, one
of the o ffi ce r s
would come up
and get into con-
versation with
me, apparently to
ascertain the mood
I was in. Once I
happened to be
in a gloomy frame
of mind, and told
the tenth mate
that life was a blank.
In his anxiety to per-
suade me to postpone
suicide, he asked me to
partake of a whisky.
After that I used to come
on deck late every night,
and say that existence
was a mistake, but after
two more whiskies the
tenth mate decided that
it would be cheaper to let
me drown.

When I get to Parliament my first effort
will be to make it a criminal offence for racing,

other clubs to make any man in an official
position president, vice-president, or anything else,
of tiieir tin-pot shivoos. My town beat the record
when it tried to -appoint the State M.E.A., the State
M.L.C., the Federal Rep., the most adjacent Senator,
the shire president, and the mayor of the nearest town all
“ patrons ” of a sheep-dog trial—two events worth £l6 in
all.
* * * *
A Melbourne scribe :
Encountered old King Cole, the barracker for a piebald
Australia, in his private lair the other night. I found him
south-east of the monkey-department, and he had just
finished the 175th stanza of a poem, extolling the virtues
of the Chow, or words to that effect. King Cole writes
poetry the same as a carpenter saws timber, sawing each
verse out with laborious earnestness. But he is an im-
pressive old man to gaze upon. He suggests Paul Kruger
to a slight extent; and an avalanche of hair descends
from his brow to his ear. His stock of information
impressed me most, however. He’s a regular vat of
figures.
He reckons that inasmuch as the human race all come
from the same original stock, therefore every white man has
Asiatic or African blood in his veins, and vice versa. It is
his opinion that in time the breeds will all become mixed
together, and the Chow will intermarry with the Saxon.,
and the Jap with the German, and the Javanese with the
American, and the Malay with the Frenchman, and that
the prevailing tint will be a sort of yellowish black, and
that it will be impossible to prevent it.
If I hadn’t had to catch a tram I would have asked Cole
where on earth he got the extraordinary information that
men all came from one original stock. I believe there is
some superstition to that effect, but no Scripture that I
can remember backs it up. I presume the people who
hold this idea reckon that Cain married his sister—this at
a time when the Scripture doesn’t even mention that lie
had a sister. And why the complaint in early Genesis
that the “ sons of God” began to marry the “ daughters
of men ” if they were all of one descent ? Even as far
back as Genesis VI., 2 and 3, the intermarriage of races
seems to have been a curse and a failure.
« • * •
A stiff-collared Scotclibyterian parson of
the Tasmanian West Coast got between the Devil
and a cold blast the other evening, and after con-
sideration decided to put up with the Devil. He
had been away touring, and his parishioners
decided to give him something extra in the way
of a welcome back; so they hired the local hall,
and put him up to make a speech and tell them
all about it. He had got quarter-way through,
and had already mentioned the shocking evils of
derrink that he had observed, when, turning, he
saw' a huge beer barrel painted on the “drop”
behind him. Also, he found that he was partially
blocking an invitation to the crowd to dunk
Eliss’s Beer, while around his head circled a
pressing command to stick to square gin. He
adjourned his further remarks till the awful
barrel had been rolled up. And then the coldest
blasts that the bleak West Coast knows how to
brew struck the prodigal on the neck. He
shrieked heroically for 10 minutes; then sadly he
stepped aside, and unrolled the barrel of beer, and
concluded his remarks with the old command to
stick to gin again glaring behind his head.
* * * *
Adelaide Register vouches for the story
of a lady who complained that “Federation has
not done much for us ; I can’t even get my wood
to burn.” On further inquiry, it was found that
the inferior fuel came from a local dealer, who,
for advertising purposes, called his allotment the
“Commonwealth Woodvard ” !
A Maoriland citizen named Sewell, who
was defendant in a little court case about two
cows, at Murchison (Nelson) last week, wakened
up the sleepiest province in Maoriland with a
new sensation. He blew himself up with dyna-
mite in the doorway of the court, and sent his
head and legs flying separately across the ball of
justice. The other shatterage consisted of a
badly-damaged magistrate, a wrecked police
inspector, a harmless citizen named Bunn terribly
injured, the front of the court blown out, and an
immense number of windows destroyed.

The result of the inquiry concerning the
death of Mrs. Tierney at Gymbowen (Vic.) is
reported in Sydney D.T. thus: —
At midnight the jury returned a verdict of murder by
some person or persons unknown. The verdict was
received with loud cheering-.
There is a legend that in the days of public
hanging in Sydney several persons went through
the trap-door to ail accompaniment of loud
cheering. There was also a reporter on an even-
ing paper who closed his account of an execution
at Darlinghurst Gaol with “loud cheers,” he
having inadvertently supposed that he was
handing in a report of a speech by Henry Parkes.

MUNIFICENCE.
“Sir, when you gave me your fare yesterday you
gave a penny too much.”
“ Did I ? Then keep it, keep it. Honesty should
always be rewarded.”
Under new Melbourne City Council by-
laws, licenses for tram cars, gripmen and conduc-
tors will run the Tramway Co. into £3 11s. per
annum for each car employed. Regulations as to
number of passengers, etc., are very stringent.
Melb. Argus, however, after interviewing the
Town Clerk, says, “it is hardly likely the bylaw
will be literally and rigidly enforced.” And
why ? Presumably the bylaw enacts what is right
and necessary.
A YEAR AGO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The time was summer, the sun was high
As I stood and watched the team go by ;
Wending slow o’er the dust-swept hill
([ almost think I can see it still),
For you were walking beside tbe load ;
And your eyes met mine across the road
But that was a year ago.
The night had fallen, the world was still;
The sun’s last gleams had kissed the hill ;
The soft breeze whispered the rustling wheat,
And stirred the grasses beside our feet ;
A white moon flooded the white bush land ;
The curlew called as you took my hand,
Vowing you’d love me till—who shall say ?
I think you said until Judgment Day—
But that was a year ago.
The wheat is reaped, and the grass is brown ;
The moon has vanished behind the town ;
Clouds sweep the sunshine from the sky ;
Vows are forgotten, and so am J.
No more the team comes over the hill;
I couldn’t expect you’d love me still —
Since that was a year ago.
TAS.

THE VARNISHED BOARD
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, July 18, 1905.
My Deab Arini.—
Whenever two or twenty women are gathered
together these days, there will surely spring up
the topic of “how to make a little money,” or “a
little more money.” This desire to make “a
little more money ” has become vehement of late.
And it may be heard from women who are of
Sydney’s bon ton (such bon ton as we’ve got, which
mostly isn’t up to the cwt. mark). Brows are
knitted and faces become tense as the speakers
argue that tea-rooms and florists’ shops are over-
done. Typing and shorthand, metal work, etc.
—well, not very lucrative, and, at the best, calling
for a sacrifice of all one’s days to make them pay-
able jobs. “Oh, if I could onljr write short
stories,” moans one She; “there is, I hear, an
almost unlimited market for them. I would write
one every night when I came home from bridge or
a dance ! I could easily malce ten guineas a week.”
All agree that music-teaching, or any teaching,
is hateful. Besides they don’t w'ant work that
will demand all their time. Not unless it were
immensely remunerative, like successful crystal-
gazing, or clairvoyancy, or some other form of
fortune-telling. At mention of these three crafts
you may almost see the listening ears waggle with
eagerness, for these are splendid money-making
concerns if you are very good at them.
But I think the prophetess of to-day needs to
devise a new form of prophecy to be an Enormous
Success. I am thinking out a new branch of the
business. It will have nothing to do with crystals
or cards. I will buy an old graveyard, and a tall
prophetess in evening dress will read the future
there on moonless nights w T itli the aid of a faint
blue light and a cypress tree and a black kitten.
The charge will be very high, and fainting patrons
will be removed in a spring-lieeled cab.
The triumphs of mechanical surgery ! You may
remember some two years ago that one of the
staff of a Sydney firm of solicitors had to have
both his legs amputated at the knees through a
tram accident at North Sydney. The tram
guard’s ability to render “first aid” saved the
sufferer from bleeding to death in the first in-
stance, and afterwards the young athlete’s own
indomitable courage and fine constitution pulled
him through. No one w r ould guess from
this “patient’s” locomotion now that he was in-
debted to any artificial support. He walks quite
erect, only a very slight stiffness is apparent when
he is going down hill, and he does not seem to
lean other than nominal weight on the cane he
carries. And the young man takes the position
quite cheerfully. “I’m going out to get my
corns cut,” he tells his friends at the office when
the spirit moves him to sally forth and see a man
about a kitten. Also he jeers at them for their
“meat feet.” The courage which kept his heart
beating on the operation table keeps it now quite
cheerful.
From a recent book: “Lady Wick stood re-
ceiving her guests in a merciless white satin gown
which set off with uncalled-for vividness the hues
of a visage dyed with wine and wind.”
France in Sydney celebrated its national fete in
the usual innocent, pastoral way—with a picnic to
Cabarita. The “ entente ” was a special feature of
this year’s frisk, and it was petted and patted and
glorified like a first baby. All the speakers
pulled it into their discourse and dandled it and
called upon the Cornstalk citizens (who went on
from one till three eating their lunch—they had
paid 15s. for their tickets) to note what a fine
thing it was and only two months old ! The band
was the German band disguised in other clothes !
When it brayed out “God Save the King,”
Mr. George Reid rolled into the picture, like the
Judge in “Pickwick,” on two little quaint legs,
and immediately fell asleep. He was awakened,
and proceeded to frolic with the entente. None of
the speakers, save Consul-General Pinard, used
French. The gap left by Mr. B. R. Wise showed
yawningly. He always made an oration in French
on these occasions. A visiting artist (Victorian)
lost his eyes to “a beautiful dark French girl,”
found an introducer, and discovered her to be a
pure Potts Pointer, who wouldn’t have known the
entente if she had found it floating in her after-
noon tea.
A seventeen-year-old boy went to a fancy-dress
ball, lately (in no matter which State), dressed as
a girl. He looked a beautiful fraud, and he has a
pretty wit which he didn’t hide behind a fan, or,
for the matter of that, behind anything else. If
that boy wanted money for a trip to Europe or the
Kermadecs, or for a rope wherewith to hang him-
self, he’d not have to look for it. Two of the
sparklets who made love to him, and ap-
pointments with him for next night, gave
him their cards with the cooing time pencilled
thereon. Their wives are great friends of his
mother. A third love-struck-at-first-sight Bertie
is engaged to be married to a sister of the mas-
querader’s dearest friend. He wrote to the saucy
youth a note beginning “Angel Face,” which the
frivolous monster showed to his sister and to “the
other fellows” at the Grammar School. Also, he
has retailed some of the compliments paid him
by those other two men. The three guilty ones
are trying to laugh it off by protesting they knew
all the time, and all the she-friends of the engaged
girl declare that “she really ought to be told.
Might save her so much misery in the future, you
know.”
When Paderewski was in Chicago recently a
certain big laundry distrained on one of his grand
pianos for a washing bill. The Master and his
Madam and his manager and the talking parrots
vowed the wash had been liquidated, but the Pole
paid again, under protest. People have so little
reverence in Chicago that they would distrain on
Paderewski’s hair if they felt that way, and only
let him have it back when he had settled up.
Professor and Mrs. David’s large Shakspeare
entertainment was one of last week’s social
events. Any guest who did not appear masque-
rading in one of the bard’s characters was to be
cast into eternal gloom, which intimation caused
dismay in the ranks of the alumni (no’Varsity
function can proceed without a reference to the
alumni). The said bard mostly chronicled the
doings of slim persons in doublet and hose, and
the host’s professorial colleagues refused to appear
in tights and arouse the ribald delight of the
undergrads, present. A deadlock was only saved
by someone remembering Friar Lawrence. Con-
sequently, monks abounded at the affair, and it
wasn’t necessary to appeal to the Arbitration
Court on account of a strike of professors, and to
hire a lawyer to ask that Judge Heydon would
order them to cavort in the Forest of Arden.
Shakspeare's songs were sung ; and the Romeos,
Juliets, Violas, Hamlets, Rosalinds, and Ophelias
danced merrily.
A comparative new-chum daughter of Mr.
James Nesbit, of Glasgow, was married on June 7
to the descendant of a very old Australia family,
Mr. Reginald Rudd, sixth son of Mr. Wm. Rudd,
of Harrington Park, Narellan. Away in the early
thirties, T. C. Harrington, Assistant Colonial
Secretary, owned the park and another property
known as Elderslie. Mr. Wm. Rudd subse-
quently came into possession, and the name has
flourished in and around Carnpbelltown ever since.
Time is good to Professor Tucker, the man
who knows his way so well about Greek life and
literature. It writes no wrinkle in adipose on his
sapling-like figure, and shows no sign of fore-
closing its mortgage on his hair. The Prof, gave
the first of a course of six lectures on Ancient
Athenian Life last night, and was listened
to with a silence that almost spoke. There
were graduates and under - graduates and
boys and girls who fairly bulged with
matriculation stuffing, and a leavening of
literary and butterfly circles. The period chosen
for the initial lecture was 440-330 8.C., when the
Athenian head was greatly swollen with conquest
and prosperity. The fortifications—shown by
lantern slides—round Athens had then just been
completed ; you could almost hear the last
Athenian stonemason dropping his tools as the
lecture cleared its throat and started. When the
Prof, gets to lecture IV. and deals with “The
Girl—The Woman—Her Ignorance—Love of
Dress—Marriage,” proceedings ought to brighten
up considerably.
Amateurs will play “The Schoolmistress” at
the Victoria Barracks presently in aid of a charity.
Some years ago this play was done by local persons
of quality. Some of them told their intimate
friends as a “dead secret” that the piece was so
difficult that it would certainly be a fearsome
failure in unskilled hands. Consequently there
was a rush for seats. A club waiter, who was
sent to procure accommodation, had his coat torn
to pieces by the dear women who were climbing
over one another to get space in which to see their
dear friends’ downfall. Naturally there was a
large audience. The play, by the way, was a
success. It is said you can see claw marks on the
wall yet where disappointed cats scratched as they
came out, and that there is a piece bitten out of
the door of the building.
There never was a better-tempered or more
philosophical concert company than that of Mr.
Watlcin Mills. Spite of the sparsely-populated
house on their final night, they sang as if every
seat were taken. “Flora’s Holiday ” was made as
jocund as lambs and children playing in spring-
time. Then the melodists went back to the Aus-
tralia and sang to each other till one in the
morning.
Mrs. Heermann and Miss Pickersgill, who
always come to great violinist Heermann’s con-
certs, are two very well-dressed and well-jewelled
women. They are so enchanted with the spring song
sung by July in Sydney that they are remaining
here whilst the great and the little masters, Hugo
and Emil, are touring elsewhere., Heermann’s
playing of the 40-minutes-long Beethoven violin
concerto at his last concert was hearkened to by
a full house that, at the close of the performance,
had arrived at just such enthusiasm as was
poured forth on Paderewski. All adjectives
that signified homage went up like hosannas.
It took Sydney some time to find out that Hugo
was great, genuine, and simple, and that his
violin sang like a singer by the grace of God.
Last Saturday Sydney tried to make up for lost
opportunities, and now awaits with ardor the
player’s return in mid-August.
Performance of the scratch professional orches-
tra on Saturday was so excellent, and got such
eulogy from Heermann, that it is a wonder these
men do not combine to give afternoon orchestral
concerts such as Melbourne enjoys under Prof.
Marshall Hall.
~Mrs. Fitzwilliam Curtis, the handsome Austra-
lian who was a Miss Genevieve Fattorini, of this
State, has secured a divorce from her officer-
husband in London. Evidence was so much in
her favor that the Judge said severe things about
the respondent, and the decree was secured on
three counts, any one of which would have been
sufficient in this State.
A delightful French pair, quite young and
decorative, have been in Sydney, at the Metro-
pole, for some weeks. Monsieur and Madame
Negaro. He has a chair —something to do with
sociology—at the Paris University, and is touring
to collect industrial knowledge. Miss Rose Scott
has provided a number of well-informed intellects
to sharpen and be sharpened on those of the
Parisians.
The collection of posters, or color impressions,
that were hung around the gallery of the Town
Hall on the night of the Artists’ Ball, was sold by
auction a few days later. Some of the pictures—
there were 28 of tliem—were capital. “ The Federal
House that Jack Built” (Gordon), “Queen of
Hearts” (Lindsay), and several of Souter’s, Leist’s
and Taylor’s were highly comic. A few artists
and Dr. Le Brun straggled in to the auction.
Then one of the artists, in despair at the thought
of the pictures going for tuppence each, telephoned
to Mr Sam. Hordern an entreaty to come and
save the situation. He came swiftly, and the
brown paper and calico masterpieces realised
for the Children’s Hospital charity.
The death in London of millionaire Peter
Russell in the manyth year of his life reminds
that he was Miss Thea Proctor’s grand-uncle, and
looked upon that pretty poster artist and her
mother (his niece) as his especial care. Ldce so
many very rich men Mr. Russell was childless.
Miss Proctor is still working at the Kensington
School of Design.
Committee of Boys’ Brigade Ball was quite
resigned to the dance not realising much lor the
charity this year. All the other chanties are m
shadow (list now whilst work for the Children s
Hospital looms up and obscures the horizon of
benevolence. Nathless the 8.8.8. was a very jolly
little one, and well-managed financially. An

14-year-old son of Sydney’s Mayor, who is usually

Some “ somebody ” Maorilanders are preparing
to stand under the usual floral wedding bell and
be Breathed upon by the Voice. Miss Hardy-
Johnston, a member of one of the First Families
of Christchurch, is engaged to Mr. T. Ogilvie
Grant, a brother of the Earl of Seafield; Miss
Helen Barnicoat, a daughter of the leading lawyer
of Wanganui, is to marry a Dr. Wall, of the same
pastoral region. Bride-elect is a cousin of the
noted Constance Barnicoat, magazine contributor,
mountain climber, and for many years W. T.
Stead’s secretary.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent :
“ News comes from Bananaland that Captain
Fitzpatrick is improving his wool knowledge there
up on a sheep run, and making preparations for
his early marriage with Miss Armytage.
“Mr. G. H. Reid kept himself awake more
than once after dinner to tell people that he didn’t
really sympathise with that Purity-of-the-Home-
and-Something-for-the-Cradle cry against the
Labor partv. But I have had a peep at the anti-
Socialism literature that George was preparing to
distribute when he came down crash, and it’s
pretty warm in its appeals to the women on the
Purity of the Home. It alleges that the Labor
party would give equal reward to the vicious and
the virtuous and 1 would make virtue and religion
impossible. By the way, I haven t noticed, since
Reid went out, any extraordinary number of
men running off with other people’s wives.
“ A story goes of a sweet young thing declaring
to a particularly nice and good-hearted johnnie
(whom she had, not succeeded in bringing up to
proposing point) that she was about to enter an
Anglican Sisterhood or something. That brought
him to the point, and he prevailed on her to try
matrimony instead, crowing as he did so over
winning a dear little retiring mate, who wouldn’t
ruin him with dress bills, and who would allow
him to say triumphantly, with a bang on his chest,
that he had snatched her from no earthly rival.
But the sequel goes to show that the retiring Eve
is a deceiver. That little wife wants quite the
ordinary number of Romney hats and Tommy
Atkins toques, and doesn’t retire any. That man
—if he ever gets another chance —will beware of
the angel-lamb who bleats about nunneries with a
downcast eye.
“ A continent-gadder has been seeing Carrie
Moore in England, and writes enthusiastically of
her improvement. ‘ There are new little tricks
the flutter of fingers and the play of the wrist.
They caught my attention, and are quite pretty.
She is a shade fuller in face and figure, and has
better taste in clothes. She will drift back to
Australia one of these days, and will please the
crowd out there. But another spoiling will never
do. Carrie hasn’t been spoilt since she left her
own country. Hard work has been a good thing
for her.’
“Before Hugo Heermann left Melbourne he
heard little Leila Doubleday play the violin, and
predicted a great future for the child if she is
started on the inevitable European course of
training within the next two years. Leila
Doubleday undoubtedly has the makings of a fine
violinist, and one likely to appeal to the crowd
as well as to the musical few. Already there is
warmth of tone in her playing. Talking of Mel-
bourne girl-musicians, it seems that Una Bourne
will be able to get away to Germany in a month
or two. Thus far there is no advertisement of a
farewell concert for the tall and wistful Una, and
if it is to be a big success the date had better be
fixed for as late as possible. Melbourne Town
Hall just now is a cold place, where the spirit of
lumbago flaps her snowy pinions, and a seat near
a doorway spells stiff neck. But the temperature
cuts both ways, so to speak. If it keeps some
people away from a concert it also moves
assembled audience to great enthusiasm. The
‘demand for an encore’ is an opportunity for
slapping one’s frozen hands together, and banging
the floor with both cold feet. The ‘ turn ’ that
gets no encore in the middle of July has probably
heated the public with indignation.
“ Mrs. Brough has no intention of deserting the
grease pots. She blithely expresses herself willing
to go on mumming, with the help of Providence
and Robert, for the next 20 years. She is exceed-
ingly astonished that Australia expected anything
else/ But did Australia really expect anything
else ?
“ A swagger Melbourne She-Club has contend-
ing elements. The cats on the hob do not agree.
Every fresh nominee is set upon by some of the
scratchers, and a dame submits her application for
membership with misgivings. ‘ The worst of the
club,’ said a jolly woman the other day, ‘is the
secret coterie element.’ ‘ Catterie,’ exploded her
companion in a purple rage. She had been
scratched out a few days before.
“Mr. and Mrs. Harry Payne struck out with a
vast number of side-shows to add to the joy of
their July spree. The Professor who boils the
billy with liquid air was one of them, an electric
bioscope was another, a programme of song and a
trio picking coon strains from the banjo, and cosy
corners (warmed to sitting-out needs) were some
more. Madame Sutherland raised her warm con-
tralto voice in song to the best effect of all the
artists. Mrs. Payne stood at the head of affairs in
a blaze of gems and in milky raiment of satin
duchesse with gaudy illuminations of silver and
steel. And all Society’s diamonds were out of
their safes that night.

RHEUMATI
Purely a Blood Disease.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
The Only Sure Cure.
RHEUMATISM and good blood can’t live
together. You put an end to your
Rheumatism the moment you make your
blood good with Dr. Wilburns’ Pink Pills.
They actually make new blood—blood that is pure
and rich and red and strong. That is the simple
scientific reason why nothing but Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills can cure Rheumatism.
Rheumatism is a disease of the blood, simply
and solely. Any doctor will tell you that. All
the cold and exposure in the world can never
really give you Rheumatism. Cold only sets the
pains going. The real cause of those pains is
Rheumatic acid in the blood. This crippling acid
stiffens the muscles, eats into the joints, and
inflames the nerves. Every day it will cripple
and torture you till you drive the aching poison
out of vour blood—and nothing can possibly drive
that poison out except Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
You can never get this rheumatic acid out of
your system with hot cloths or liniments or with
anything else that doesn’t touch the blood. These
things only make the poison circulate better. They
may seem to ease your pain for the moment —but
they are really doing you more harm than good.
They cannot possibly cure you. Nothing can
cure you till you fill your veins with good rich
blood—and Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills alone contain
the very elements that go to make new, pure,
rich, red blood. This new blood sweeps out the
rheumatic acid, loosens your muscles, soothes your
nerves, and gives you back the use of your stiff and
burning joints. Rheumatism and Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills simply can not live together.
“ I don’t know anyone who ever had Rheuma-
tism worse,” said Mrs. Burrowes, Holly Cottage,
31 Arcadia Road, Glebe Point, Sydney. “My
whole system was filled with painful poison. The
agony I suffered was intense. But, thanks to
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, I don’t know what
Rheumatism is now. They drove all the aching
acid out of my blood, and made me a strong and
active woman when I thought I was crippled for
life. .
“ For two years I was never free from Rheuma-
tism,” Mrs. Burrowes went on. “ Some muscles
were stiff as if they had been bruised —in others I
had cramp-like pains that almost made me cry with
agony. Sometimes the pain was more than I
could bear. For months I didn’t know what it
was to get a night’s sleep. Even if I did doze off,
I woke in the middle of the night suffering pain
that can never be described. My legs and knees
were swollen to twice their size. And my hands
were so bent and twisted that I could not hold a
needle.
“ The doctor said the Rheumatism was brought
on through overwork. I took his medicine and
rubbed myself sore with liniments—but they didn’t
give me any ease from my pain. Nothing did me
the least good till I took Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills.
“ A few days after starting Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills I felt wonderfully better in some strange
way. I could not explain j ust what it was —but
I was better,” continued Mrs. Burrowes. “ Then
I got a grand appetite. Soon I noticed that the
rheumatic pains and stiffness were not nearly so
bad. Before long I was able to get a good sleep.
At last every trace of Rheumatism was driven out
of my blood. To-day I am in perfect health.
When Dr. William’s Pink Pills cured me I don’t
believe there ever was a case of Rheumatism that
they could not cure. They saved me from being
crippled for life.”
By their direct action on the blood, Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills cure all blood diseases like
anaemia, backache, biliousness, consumption (when
not too far gone), debility, eczema, failing powers,
indigestion, kidney disease, liver complaint,
nervousness, neuralgia, partial paralysis, rheuma-
tism, sciatica, and the secret suffering that many
an honorable woman bears in silence. But you
must get the genuine Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Pale People. If anyone tries to-force some substi-
tute upon you send for the genuine kind direct to
Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co., Sydney, 2s. 9d. a
box, six boxes 15s. 3d., post free.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
A Brisbane comp, set it up thusly : “He
was also at this time infested with the order of
C.M.G.”
The Princess of Wales has given birth to
another child, the sixth. They patriotically guard
the succession to the throne, these Royal folk.
Last day of June was a busy one for Prime
Minister Deakin. He first ejected G. H. Reid
from office, and then rushed off to give away his
daughter in marriage.
In all probability Queensland Senator
Drake will not face the Northern Democrats next
election. His recent appearance around his old
haunts with the shandygaff Premier was the last
straw.
Ex-solicitor Horwitz, now in Pentridge
gaol, is reading up theology, with a view, he says,
to starting a new religion when he gets out.
“There is nothing like a new religion to make
money at for a smart man,” he observes.
Died a few days ago, Edward Latham,
founder (40 years ago) of the Carlton (Melb.)
Brewery, aged 66 years. Mr. Latham was a strong
Church of England man, and made himself notable
once by a huge subscription to a home for indi-
gent parsons.
There are but two lawyers in the new
Commonwealth Government —Alfred Deakin and
Isaac Alfred Isaacs. Deakin is the son of a
Fitzroy (Melb.) coach proprietor, and was born in
that suburb. Isaacs was born at Beechworth, and
is the son of a tailor.
“ O’Fipp ” : To save further mistakes, I
wish to inform The Bulletin that the president
of the Women’s Gnash-’em-all League is not
Mrs. Janet Clarke, but Janet Mrs. Clarke. Also,
the leader of the Victorian Woman-Suffragists is
Vida Miss Goldstein.
Wife of H. Minmah, of Murwillumbah
(N.S.W.), presented her husband, t’other day,
with the 20th child. There are still some fine
families in Australia. Ex-M.L. A. “ Baldy ” Davis
(N.S.W.) is said to have 24 children, most of them
big strapping adults now.
Recent decision of the High Court in the
case of T. M. Slattery, in which the Chief Justice
said that there were gaps in the law which it was
not the duty of the Judges to fill, recalls that two
members of the Court were for some time Law
Officers in N.S.W. and yet didn’t see to it that
those “gaps” were filled.
Mr. W. N. Willis, the land agent whom
some people are desperately eager to see the last
of, is still at Perth (W.A.), fighting hard against
a return to N.S.W. in custody of the police.
He states that he is anxious to come back to
N.S.W. to clear up everything, but he resents
being dragged back.
GASTON MERVALE, Actor.
Mr. Harry Stockdale, one of the few re-
maining members of the band of Australian
pioneer explorers, has for some time past been
seriously ill at his residence, Bunnerong-road,
Lilyville. Mr. Stockdale’s fortunes are not flourish-
ing, and a recognition of his past work by old
friends would not be now amiss.
Mrs. D. French, a resident of the quiet,
old-fashioned town of Montefiore, on the Mac-
quarie River (N.S.W.) died last week aged, on
pretty good evidence, 105 years. It is only in
very quiet towns, dead towns —in fact in the very
deadest towns —that people seem to drift along to
these unfathomable wastes of years.
Died last week at Brisbane, “Sam,” or
“Puttingga,” the last of the Brisbane tribe of
aborigines. For some years he was a member of
the native mounted police in N.Q. He is men-
tioned in Tom Petrie’s “Reminiscences of Queens-
land.” He and Petrie played as boys together in
the bad old days of early Brisbane.
This paper mentioned lately, re the need of
an automatic marine stoker, that some eight years
ago Geo. Clavdon assured Sydney shipping co’s
that he had solved the riddle, but that since then
he had faded away. The long-lost inventor writes
from Christchurch (M.L.) that lie has been silently
making improvements, which he has patented
everywhere, and that he intends to soon strike
Sydney with them.
The Bulletin is informed by Mr. H. A.
Brandt, solicitor, Melbourne, that the par re
artist Nerli, once a resident of Sydney (6/7/’O5), is
not correct, and that the Marquis Pieri Nerli is
still alive, and residing in Italy. This paper is
glad to hear it and sorry for the error. Neither
the Marquis Nerli, it is stated, nor any of his
family has ever been afflicted with any mental
derangement.
From a Perth man :
Maitland Brown, who died in Westralia the other
day, was not, as often printed, a brother-in-law of Bigjohn
Forrest’s. Uigjohn married a Miss Hamersley. Brown
was a good, kind-hearted soul, and was \*ery hospitable
to t’othersib jrs passing through Geraldton in the early
days of the Murchison goldfields. Also he had a fine col-
lection of old coins, bottles, glasses, blunderbusses, and
other relics from Dutch vessels wrecked on Houtman’s
Abrolhos Islands, some 30 miles from Geraldton, nearly
300 years ago.
Re depriving a Supreme Court Judge
of his office. In November, 1837, J. Wal-
pole Willis, a man reputed to be of some
ability in England, and with influence
there, was commissioned to N.S.W. with
the rank of Second Puisine Judge, and
was, on arrival, sent to Melbourne (then
Port Phillip), where he officiated as first
Judge. He was something of an eccentric,
and prided himself, on saying just what he
thought. As he thought at right angles to
most people’s thoughts, hot water was his
regular element, but the Judge liked hot
water. He was reported to the N.S.W.
Government, not for neglect of duty or in-
competence, but for bad temper, vulgar
expressions and guffawing in the faces of
the leaders of Port Phillip society, and was
removed from office in November, 1842.
Philp, Queensland’s almost forgotten
Premier, was a hold vote-canvasser in early
days. He and E. J. Bamfield, then a press-
man, came to the Barron River on one occa-
sion. They knew there were some votes
for the snatching at the other side, but the
river was a banker fully half-a-mile across,
and timber all sizes tumbled along. Still
those votes fascinated Philp, and he deter-
mined to get them, though he went for them
naked. He peeled off at the foot of a gum.
Bamfield, being a pressman, was a bit shy,
but the barbaric Philp yelled him into sub-
jection, and in a few minutes, with duds
strapped to their heads, the pair took to
the river. Philp got the votes—the voters
felt they had to give them—and thus he
won his first election.
“ Truthibus ” : Saw in a recent issue
a paragraph that even a laugh like Pea-
cock’s has its uses. It also has its draw-
backs. Was working in a house in Flin-
ders-lane. One day the boss, transacting
business with the Peacock, showed him over
the place with a proud air, got him to the
first floor and made a joke. Peacock had
just finished his last “ha, ha,” when up
the lift bole from the packing room came a
mad echo—stronger, heartier and with more
of the devil in it than the original. It
floated up that well and filled the whole
house. Result, half a fit of apoplexy for
the boss, a cold and dignified silence on
the part of the Peacock, and the “boot”
on the spot for the junior packer.
Judge G. C. Heydon, the new President
of N.S.W. Arbitration Court, is a native of
Sydney, born at 78 King-street (Hoadley’s Build-
ings), where his father, Jabez Heydon, carried on
the business of an auctioneer. Subsequently
Jabez Heydon became a pawnbroker and Aus-
tralian agent for Holloway’s pills and ointment,
and later he was proprietor of the Freeman’s
Journal, and owned much city property.
Dr. Dunne, R.C. Bishop of Wilcannia
(N.S.W.), should acquire merit as a much-travelled
ecclesiastic. He resides at Broken Hill, and his
diocese includes the town of Bourke, 300 miles
north. It is his custom to refresh himself
quarterly by making a journey of 2000 miles—
from Broken Hill to Adelaide, thence to Mel-
bourne, Sydney, Bourke, and back.

A letter from Melbourne :
With an election among the early possibilities, the
trimmers and ratters of the Reps, are having a most un-
comfortable time attending district social and religious
functions under all the inconveniences of half-eaten meals,
bad digestion, colds, croup, &c., in order to preserve their
seats. Rail-sitter , of Victoria, finds himself com-
pelled to hustle in spite of girth and wind. He went to a
banquet, the other evening, but hearing a whisper that a
late opponent would take the chair at a religious meeting
“in the unavoidable absence of our member,” he left
before dessert, and the religious gathering rubbed its
eyes at the sight of an over-grown collection of grease and
want of breath, in expansive dress suit, apparently chew-
ing a piece of half-eaten turkey, pushing for the platform
in the very nick of time. Once more the seat was saved !
Deceased P. N. Russell was an exacting
boss, and treated business as a religion. As his
father’s right-hand man in the foundries in Queen’s
Place, Sydney, he began at a time when most of
the workers were a sorry, and some a criminal lot,
and Russell’s notion was that, by giving them law-
ful employment, he commanded them mind and
body. In after years, when the business attained
vast proportions, he tried to live up to the same
principles, but met snags in the form of better
men and union men. In a fit of dudgeon he shut
up shop, and told the workers to starve. Withal,
old Peter had good features. His main fault was
that he was incapable of realising that the man
who worked for another could think for himself.

Statue to commemorate the New South Wales
Lands Administration.
Rev. James Cheong, the anti-opium crusa-
der in Australia, is a Church of Englander, and
son of a Church of England Chinese. He showed
himself a smart youth while attending classes in
Melbourne University, and managed to take
honors with his degrees. Then he went to Oxford
to learn the one and only true , bleat, and was
ordained with a great flapping of garments by the
Bishop of Rochester. There is no more gentle-
manly or gentlemanly-looking parson in Aus-
tralasia than Mr. Cheong.
“F.” : Apropos military titles (B. 15/6/’O5).
This is the way the military business was run in
Sydney in 1844. “ Military staff stationed in
Sydney, N.S.W. His Excellency, Lieutenant-
General Sir M. C. O'Connell, Commanding the
Forces ; Captain M. C. O’Connell, 28th Regiment,
Assistant Military Secretary; Captain E. M.
O’Connell, 99th Regiment, Major of Brigade ;
Lieutenant W. B. J. O’Connell, 73rd Regiment,
Aide-de-Camp : Ensign C. P. 0 Connell, 51st
Regiment, Extra Aide-de-camp.” Capt. M. C.
O’Connell was born in Sydney Barracks, George-
street, in 1812. When he retired from the army
he took to politics and was an unsuccessful candi-
date at the first election (1843) for his native city.
Afterwards O’Connell was returned for Port
Phillip without a canvass. He pitched his tent in
Queensland, and dabbled in politics there, being a
Minister without portfolio for three months in
1860, and President of the Legislative Council for
19 years from 1860 to 1879.
Alleged that Warton, whom the Queens-
land Executive didn’t save, from the gallows, to
which he was condemned in connection with the
Toowong murder, is identical with Butler, who
was about the keenest and most notorious criminal
the Maoriland police ever struggled with. If he
hadn’t had a mental kink of some sort his remark-
able abilities would have made him a land
agent. Butler’s first “big” affair was in Vic-
toria ; in his first 16 years there, and before
he was 25, he had got through sentences amount-
ing altogether to 13 years. In Maoriland (where
he had sentences of over 30 years) he once made
a six-hours’ address to a jury (when accused of the
murder of a woman and her two children) and got
himself off. Between times he was school-teacher,
reporter, and lecturer ; and once he combined
Sunday-school teaching and burgling. But his
vanity was his weak point; he told the police far
too much, having an idea that if he couldn’t get
round the jury he could beat the Judge. As to
the latter, the effort he was proudest of was when
he satisfied Justice Williams, of Maoriland, that
he was a first offender, and this after his Vic-
torian career. He must have received sentences
amounting, on paper, to well over 60 years.
Journalist J. D. Woods, dead at Adelaide,
aged 71, began his career as a reporter in the
House of Commons. His-father and eldest
brother were both London Times men ; latter
afterwards came to Melbourne and became leader
of the Argus Parliamentary staff. Among other
journalistic work Woods compiled the official
account of Edinburgh’s Adelaide visit, and an
historical account, under Government authority,
of South Australia from its discovery up to 1892.
He was for many years electoral returning officer
for East Torrens.
John Medway Hay, who lately died at
Hobart, aged 67, held the sad office of editor of
the Tasmanian Mail in his latter days. Day
came to South. Australia in 1866 and for some
years tried ministering to a Baptist flock. He
wearied of the narrowness of the Dippers and
joined the staff of the Register, where he
remained for 17 years. In ’93 he stood for
Gumeracha in the State Parliament elections, but
was defeated. Gumeracha wanted something
heavy that would make a good paper-weight, and
it put the stolid Ilomburg at the top of the poll.
Medway Day left S.A. after that, and trod the
inky way in the Eastern States for the balance of
his days. That a man of his ability should have
carried the Tasmanian Mail on his shoulders was
a tragedy suggestive of Shelley lumping on a
wharf.
“ Synic ” writes :
Reading your disrespectful comments upon Pursy
Chirnside and his C.M.G. degree, 1 shuddered as one
shudders at an uncalled-for blasphemy or a serpent.
The true meaning of the C.M.G. (with or without the
titulary K) has been somewhat obscured by its application
to politicians and mere flunkeys. Nevertheless it con-
tinues to signify “ the reward of money.” The man who,
being possessed of wealth, chooses to splash a little of it
around in what is called a public-spirited manner, earns
the right to be C.M.G.’d. Pursy Chirnside is such a man.
He has provided the whole or part of the cash required
for building churches of various conflicting Christian
denominations. His cheque-book is non-sectarian. He
has built a school in the Wyndham (Vied district; the
Melbourne Hospital got £5OO from him ; a half-battery of
artillery cost him £i5,000, and his contributions towards
the encouragement of militarism for national defence
have been not unworthy of a large proputtv owner. In
that respect one may think of Pursy as a staunch self
protectionist. He deserves no actual credit for
possessing the means to do these financial deeds,
but he shines by comparison with those equally
fortunate mortals who refrain from doing like-
wise. Rupert Clarke, with an inherited baronetcy, is
a supporter of little else than the turf. The Manifolds,
whose sheep runs block the way to closer settlement in
the bounteous Western District of Vic., are niggardly
where Pursy Chirnside is open-handed. The Werribee
land monopolist can afford to do it, and he does it. Some
of the others can better afford to do it, but they don’t.
Let us wish citizen Pursy joy of his bauble, for just what
it is worth, nothing exaggerating nor setting down aught
in malice. We may deplore the fact that baubles are per-
mitted in our should-be democratic country, but as long
as such things are possible to the Pursy class, we can
very well affect to treat them as rewards of negative merit
in the men who pay a fair price for them. In the king-
dom of close-fisted capitalists a moderately 7 generous one
deserves any distinction he can get. Unfortunately we
cannot cast ridicule on the poor little bauble without
giving the stingy men an excuse for doing nothing to
attract attention to their wealth. They pretend to be
mean from motives of modesty.

A victim of the testimonial craze con-
sulted a solicitor the other day about proceeding
at law against admirers who put down their
names for substantial sums and failed to pay
up. The victim says he stood them innumerable
drinks and several hotel dinners while the testi-
monial list was going round, and even if they paid
up he would be nothing in pocket by their friend-
ship. What specially angers him is that some of
those admirers have now started out to raise
a testimonial for a bitter political rival.

Aboriginalities.
A word from a Darling River selector
about a man who hasn’t been having too good a
time lately:—
Edward MacFarlane, now Under Sec. for Lands (N.S. W.),
and a frequent witness before the Lauds Commission,
was for some years recently Chairman of Land Boards out
here. He used to put in some good work for us small
men (t.e., homestead lessees). The squatters invariably
had a lawyer to appear for them. This was a luxury we
could not afford. But the moment the lawyer bullied or
bounced, “old Mac,” who had the Lands Act and prece-
dents off pat, would come to the rescue, and the lawyer
would have to climb down. By the way, the really best
clause fop the small man in Crick's Western Lands Act
is the one doing away with lawyers and red-tapeism.
“Old Mac” was a stickler for etiquette. His dignity
many a time came near getting him down and worrying
him.
Call to mind (as a side issue) Mac. questioning an old
fellow re the carrying capacity of his lease. “ And what
kinds of grass grow on your lease ? ” “Eh ? ” queried the
old fellow—he was a bit deaf. Mae. repeated the ques-
tion. “Ay, dioh, what kind of grasses'? Well, there’s
porkypine, and mallee, and turpentine, and Darling pea,
and various ither grasses.” Of course the old fellow was
going in for a reduction of rent.
Again, the Board wanted to know how one applicant
proposed working the lease that he was applying for.
Applicant, a (German : “ I indend to grow heabs and
heabs of lucerne an’ stuff to feed mein horses.” “But
what are you going to use your horses for? ” questioned
MacFarlane. “Mein Gott, kandt you see I geep mein
horses to eadt up mein lucerne? ”

“ Dico ” : lie proceshing caterpillars men-
tioned by “Cronje” (B. 15/(i/’O5). Have often
come across them in the Mallee, out west of
Hopetoun (Vie.); but the length of procession
was invariably much greater than “ Cronje’s.”
They were of a light slate-color, and travelled in
such an unbroken line that a casual observer would
easily mistake them for one long gray worm.
Never took any measurements, but have seen a
file crossing a road, leader on one side of wheel-
tracks and last man on the other ; so length must
have been about 7ft. The strangest thing about
them was a fine, silky thread, running the whole
length of the line, and left behind as they travelled,
like a wake. This was only visible in strong sun-
light, or when the line was broken, as its silkiness
quickly faded. If a couple of caterpillars were
removed from the middle, the others never
stopped to make inquiries. Hind part just moved
up till line of communication was restored ; then
off they went. Never once saw them halt for a
spell, though they moved fairly fast —for cater-
pillars.
“Xerxes” : Have measured heaps of ’roo
jumps, and have kept lots of tame ’roos. The usual
jump while going to feed or water is only from
3ft. to sft Medium-sized animals at full speed
average 21ft. I verified this by repeated trials
till I got tired. On Arajol Station saw a,
very large ’roo clear the head of a fallen tree by a
jump 39ft. in length and over Bft. in height. Poor
beggar was caught in about ten more jumps—
please don’t let your writers call them “hops.”

“ W.P.8.”: Before aboriginaliars are
allowed to become rude to one another, please
allow me to have a say on sleeping out at night.
Once I accidentally got drunk on whisky, and
slept in a park with the slip-rails down. The
thermometer was four or five yards below zero at
the time. I don’t know how long I was there,
but I woke up on account of the draught, and
when I collected a few of my scattered senses
shuffled home to my bed, which was touring round
the room. When it came round to me I boarded.
I stayed there for three weeks, and there was
nearly one ass less in the world. Perhaps some
rhinoceros or other commercial traveller will now
write to say that I didn’t sleep out long enough,
and if T had stayed in the park all night all would
have been well. If so, please tear his letter in
two pieces.

“ Quidam’s ” complaint :
“ Dave H.R.” (B. 6/7/’O5) rails at “Cronje”(B. 15/6/’O5)
for killing a caterpillar instead of treating him kindly,
and then goes on to say “ Cronje’s ” species of caterpillar
strips all bark and leaves from a tree, and makes large,
itching, red lumps on your neck if he falls down it.
Where's the sense in chancing big, red lumps in order to
treat caterpillars kindly ? If you want to be kind, why
not start by providing afternoon tea for the Devil?

“ Erip ” : Another danger threatens
Northern Victoria. During the period of the
mice plague thousands of cats flocked from the
South. Every day, hordes of cats could be seen
dropping their swags and getting to work on the
mic3. While the mouse-food was plentiful they
grew to an enormous size and became bloated
cats. The mice have suddenly evaporated, and
the cats are starving. They are just commencing
to move about in bands, and farmers are losing
large numbers of sheep and cattle. They have not
yet been known to attack human beings, but that
may come. In fact, the time may arrive when
the beleaguered householder will look out despair-
ingly on a moving landscape of hostile cats come
to tear down his premises and eat all the
inhabitants and the bedstead.
* * * *
Up Ingham (Q.) way, where the “ peaceful
and harmless Kanaka” (ride Senator Dobson)
pervades the land, a selector’s daughter, aged 14,
went down the main road, the other day, for her
father’s horse. About 400yds. from her home two
Kanakas came out from behind a bush, and one
seized the girl while the other kept watch. The
plucky child fought for life and honor, screamed
for help, and hit the nigger a swinging blow across
his mouth with the horse-harnes. Her cries
brought white men from the fields (fortunately
there are still white men in the fields) and the two
niggers crept into the scrub. Next day the girl
identified one by the mark of the hames. The
other got off for want of identification. As the child
could prove only the laying on of hands, the Kanaka
could be sentenced for nothing nfore than common
assault, and got two months in gaol. All this
happened in hroad daylight. Not long since, in
the same district, a young girl school teacher was
waylaid going home in the afternoon, ravished,
murdered, and mutilated in a truly dreadful way,
and notwithstanding a reward of £*soo, her mur-
derers walk the land unknown. Now the women
up that way are arming themselves. Senator
Dobson should engage a few of these gentle inno-
cents to keep watch and ward over his female
relatives and friends when he leaves them at
home as he goes forth to preach the virtues of a
black Australia.
# * *
“Burrowong” : Re old Sofala, mentioned
in a recent issue of Bulletin. The old town in
its palmy days was a wild and hairy place, with
29 pubs, 26 stores (including nine run bv Chows),
and other trades in like profusion. Thisiswlieie
Larry Foley first made a name as a lad. Old
Steve Swain, uncle of Emily Soldene, backed
Larry in the backyard of his pub. to beat three
other lads, one after the other. The three were
not “on,” however. There Bill Day, the bush-
ranger, carried an anvil weighing 3cwt. 91b. from
his blacksmith’s shop to Steve Swain’s for a wager
of £lO, the distance being nearly three-quarters of
a mile. The late A. G. Taylor’s mother was a
native of Sofala. R. D. Meagher’s father was
sergeant in charge of the gold escort. Walter
Hickenbotham, the veteran trainer, first saw the
light there. Poor old Quong Tart learned a great
deal at Sofala ; and James Rutherford (Cobh and
Co.) made thousands with his coaches up that
way. Tom Coyle, one of the pioneer drivers over
the Wyagdon and Monkey hills, afterwards drove
on the Towers (Q.), and is, I believe, now a
wealthy man. Also, Fitzgerald brothers (of circus
fame) were born at Wattle Flat, four miles away
from the once-great gold camp which is now a
pauper.
* * * ■*
“L.C.”: “ Hamer’s ” cynical remarks on
the fidelity of the dog (B. 8/6/’O5) are right as far
as they go—which is about half-way. A man’s
dog differs from his master only in degree. If the
master is a cad, the dog will be a cad. If the
master is a gentleman, he won’t own a dog that
isn’t a gentleman—at least a gentledog—likewise.
Given a good breed, you can impart every manly
quality to the canine nature; and you can utterly
spoil the best-pedigreed tyke. R. L. Stevenson
observed a dog that enjoyed the patting of an ape
equally with that of human beings; but that dog
surely was of the low-down, uneducated order,
and in favor of colored immigration.

“ Pelican ” : Where did the wild duck,
swan, pelican, etc., get to during the big drought ?
Recently out west (N.S.W.). particularly round
Nyngan and Mullengudgery, there have been
tens of thousands of the birds. And their appear-
ance was made immediately after the second good
fall of rain. They couldn’t have been hatched
out in the time.

“Ran” writes: —One of Queensland’s
richest assets is the Atherton scrub country,
which extends from a little to the north of Ingham
to a point some distance north of Cairns. This
belt of rich, volcanic soil, reaching an alti-
tude of 2(>ooft. above sea level, and covered
with a dense jungle of valuable timbers, is,
perhaps, the richest unalienated area in
Australia. It is estimated to contain over
2,000,000 acres of valuable land, capable of
supporting over 200,000 people engaged in
primary industries, who would, in turn, sup-
port tens of thousands more. The Tory
advocate, in his desire to plead for the
Kanaka, has in the past unhesitatingly de-
clared N.Q. to be a place unlit for wh.te
men because of the climate. He made no
exceptions, not even this delightful table-
land country which possesses a climate equal
to that of Toowoomba and Warwick, and
nearly as cool as Katoomba’s. Even past
Queensland Governments did not realise the
enormous wealth which lies latent in tins
northern patch. The cedar-getter started to
exploit the rich timber years ago, and, to
serve his ends, got some of the country
thrown open as selections. After he had
skinned the timber, he generally leased his
holding to the Chows. Some attention is
now being directed to the place because
of its enormous dairying possibilities. But
the Government should turn the key in the
padlock until it can get the right class of
settlers, and it should see that a condition
of future sales (if there must be sales) is that
the mongrel races must get no more foot-
ing than they enjoy at present.
Had a boy working for me
who had a lot of warts on his hands. I set him to
pulling the plant known as deadly night shade
from among some potatoes. In three days the
warts had disappeared.
“ J. W.” :
The crow’s intel-
ligence is pro-
verbial, and his
mental develop-
ment seems to
have reached
that stage that
enables him to
count som e—
probably as
many as the
blackfellow. We
were a party of
four, dugongfish-
ing, in the early
sixties at the
southern end of
Wide Bay. The
place on the
beach where we
landed the du-
gong prepara-
tory to “ trying
ut ” the oil lay
just within gun
shot of our
i.umpy. On e
day, by a lucky
shot through a
loophole, we
knocked over a
crow busy feed-
ing on a carcase.
To encou rage
others, we hung
the body in a tree
some 30 yards
further along the
beach. The serio-
comic funeral
service that took
place soon after-
wardsbaffles des-
cription t h e
mournful caw-
comical
twistings of the
head, the hop-
ping of the
mourners from
branch to branch
round the body.
We tried careful
stalking, but the crows
in the tree could apparent-
ly judge distance as well as our-
selves, and we failed to get within gunshot.
O '.e day we happened to go in the opposite
direction, and on our return the crows were busy
at the carcase. As we approached they took up
their usual station in the tree. A happy thought
occurred to us. First one quitted the humpy and
went off in an opposite direction from the tree ;
presently another, a little later a third, leaving out*
with the gun at the loophole. Plenty of cawing in
the tree, but no descent upon the carcase. Then
the one with the pun left and down came the
crows. We tried another dodge. Four would go
out, three return, and so on, ringing the changes
quickly, until at last only one remained. There
was a fierce cawing and discussion in the tree, and
I could see the crows calculating on their claws
how many had gone and how many had returned,
and shaking their heads suspiciously. Only one
foolish crow figured it out that we had all gone,
and backed his"opinion by settling on the dugong.
1 Lis death happened soon after, and we never got
a chance to shoot another crow.

The minali, introduced from Tasmania
into North Queensland in the hope that it would
war against the cattle-tick, has done good work.
It follows the cattle to their camping-places, and,
walking over the animals, gets to business. The
same fill'd is proving* useful on the farms, where
it joins forces with the ibis to keep the cockchafer
grub in check.

BIG BLOCK, THE PIONEER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Melbourne Argus has coined a fine impressive name for
its friend, the bloated pastoralist. He is “ the Pioneer
of Settlement.”
The Pioneer of Settlement,
He gets his acres cheap :
He dedicates the grassy land
To cattle and to sheep ;
And fondly, from his homestead snug,
He views the prospect drear,
For he’s the settler thereabouts,
He wants no others near.
The flocks and herds that roam the earth
Bear witness to his solid worth ;
Dame Nature smiles in bitter mirth
At Block, the Pioneer.
The Pioneer of Settlement,
lie squats upon the soil,
And draws his profit from the needs
Of them who daily toil,
Whilst tens of thousands fight for food,
For food that is so dear.
The value of his vast estate
Is growing year by year.
He shows no light, he makes no track.
For toiler Tim and toiler Jack ;
His station fences keep them ha ik
lie s Block, the Pioneer.
VIC.
E.F.

“ Gip ” : Talking about what bushmen
will eat. Not long ago old Jim Evans returned
home after a big spree to find his hut in darkness
and the cupboard empty. He was very hungry,
likewise very drunk. He might have shot a
rabbit, but it was too dark ; also he was too drunk.
After some consideration he took the butchering
knife and went over to where Bawly, one of the
working bullocks, fed quietly, and applied the
knife to the root of Bawly's tail. By the time he’d
cut in a bit, Bawly was indignant, and started off
at a gallop. Jim hung on for three times round
the paddock, getting a cut in every now and then.
At the fourth lap the tail came away, and Jim lit
a fire and cooked and ate it. He talks about the
feat as if it were something to be proud of.
You may talk about Niagara,
And rave of quaint Japan ;
Quote the Trans-Siberian railway
As the greatest work of man.
Not even the greatest Colossus
Can such pride of place secure,
As the famous cough reliever
Known as Woods’ Great Peppermint Cure.*

“ Rei ” : Vic. law for the protection of
harmless native fauna is a dead letter in many
parts of the State, with the result that in some
places native bears and lyre birds have entirely
disappeared. One Melbourne skin and hide mer-
chant sets forth on his published price-list a warn-
ing that bears are protected all the year round,
and on the same page offers a price for their skins,
being thus a law-breaker—as a receiver of illicit
goods—as much as the shooter, whom he warns to
sneak behind a tree should a bobby heave in sight.
The law, to be consistent, should deal with the
merchant who advertises prices for the skins of
protected animals, or on whose premises such
skins are found.

-“ Nym ” : As soon as the dead timber has
been removed from the Waranga (Vic.) Basin
storage area, it is intended to let the water into
the Basin from the Goulburn though the inlet
channel now completed. What a splendid refuge
this would make for the native wildfowl, now
battered ’twixt sky and earth on the opening of
each slaughter season ! Bent should see that this
spot is kept sacred to the feathered tribes of the
waterways ; likewise (for several years, at least) as
a breeding ground for our native fish.
“ Yarrum ” : Had rather exciting experi-
ence here (Newcastle way) t’other morning. Took
ray rifle down to the sea to have a shot at a por-
poise. Couldn’t see one anywhere, so decided to
have a go at dark object on rocks along the beach,
500 yd. off. Thought it was an old cask, or a
fish basket. Had two shots, and missed badly, in
consequence of strong wind blowing at the time,
Was annoyed, as I rarely miss anything, and was
preparing for another try, when the object got up
and moved towards me. It was a newly-married
couple under an umbrella. I fainted, and am still
insensible.

“ Harry S.” writes :
Re the length of Australian snakes. I herewith submit
for your inspection a snake skin which, before being de-
prived of head and portion of tail, measured over 20ft.
[Skin, as submitted, measured 15ft. long by 9£in. greatest
girth.—Ed. B.) “ Werrimee ” quotes Krefft as saying that
“ An Australian rock snake of 10 or 11 feet must be con-
sidered a monster,” but 1 have several times seen northern
pythons of even double that length, fairly measured by
tape, neither “ stock-whip nor Bill’s feet ” being handy at
the time. In Queensland these monster snakes are often
found coiled up on the top of a sapling, where the leaves
are close enough to support the weight. A native climbs
up and either kills the reptile or makes it descend to the
ground, where others quickly despatch it. These snakes
are of “ constrictor ” habit, and are, therefore, not veno-
mous, but they can give a nasty bite. The longest Aus-
tralian snake in the Australian Museum, Sydney, measures
about 15ft. At Melville Island, North Australia, there is
a snake that measures 10ft. in length. It has very long-,
sharp teeth, and is a dark mottled brown with white
belly. It has a very broad, flat head, with a small neck.
Not known if it is venomous. In Kimberley, Nor’-West
Australia, I twice saw a very active snake about 6ft. long
and of the color and gleam of bright copper.

Harry Stockdale writes :
“ C.X.G.” (B. 15 6’05) re the Burke and Wills exploring
party errs in stating that “King was found in 1866.”
King was found at Innaminckaor Koolyamerra waterhole,
Cooper's Creek, in September, ’6l. A. W. Howitt, leader
of the “ Contingent Exploring Party,” writing from l’oria
Creek on Oct. lllth, ’6l, says :—“ On Sept. 15th, in lat.
27'44 found John King, the only survivor of Mr. Burke’s
party, living with the Cooper’s Creek natives. He was in
a very weak state, but is fast recruiting.” llowitt’s own
journal says : “At the lower end of
the large reach of water 1 met Sandy
and Frank looking for me, with the
intelligence that King, the only sur-
vivor of Mr. Burke’s party, had been
found. 1 found King sitting in a hut
that the natives had made for him.
He presented a melancholy appearance
—wasted to a shadow, and hardly to be
distinguished as a civilised being but
by the remnants of clothes upon him.
Tlie natives were all gathered round,
seated on the ground, looking on with
a most gratified and delighted expres-
sion.” King arrived in Melbourne in
Nov., ’6l, and had a great reception.
“ 0.X.G.” has probably confounded the
date of the finding of King with that
of the public funeral in Melbourne in
’63 of the remains of the lost explorers
—at which 1 was present.

of Chinamen, who were scrub-cut-

called Jacky, who had been chefs
assistant at a Melbourne hotel,
and could cook marvellously.
When he came up to the house, at
my request, he donned a white
shirt, white cap and apron, and
brought with him a large nonde-
script black and white dog. Jacky
liked exercising his authority over
his “lillie bup,” as he called it,
and when the dog misbehaved, his
master would tie a rope to his tail
and swing him over a tree-limb,
1 leaving him suspended, some-
times for hours. One day, the
dog appeared without his tail,
had cut the tail off because “ him
it, smilingly admitted that he
and Jacky, when asked about
waggee it too muchee.” He said he
was making soup of the appendage,
and, when I went to the camp to in-
vestigate, sure enough there was the tail
in a saucepan, cut up into sections, and
stewing with some Chinese condiments. Jacky
didn’t cook any for me after that. Which
reminds me that once, when a young sheep-
dog of mine, in jumping a fence, got his
hind legs caught in the wire-netting, and was
hanging there, howling piteously, our Chinese
vegetable-hawker passed calmly by, without
attempting to assist the animal. Coming to the
house shortly afterwards, the yellow scoundrel
blandly inquired, “Did em dog die ?”

“ San Remo ” writes :
In the interests of Australian bird life, I wish to start a
crusade against the slaughtering of many of our bird
species. Take the emu, our most distinctive type of bird
life. The emu is fast disappearing, and, if steps are not
taken, will soon be as extinct as the moa. Some States
(Queensland, for instance) protect (?) the emu by a £5
penalty'for destruction—which is its ineffective as the rest
of our tariff. The jackass is likewise protected ; also some
others (pelican, £2O). Protection of jacky is pretty effec-
tive. lie is hardly worth shooting, and only an idiot
would risk £5 for the sake of his carcase. With the emu
it is different. Every budding sport considers him the
blue ribbon of his bag, and an emu-skin mat is quite a
prize. If the fine were £5O, the emu might receive the
respect which its position in bird life demands—immunity
from wanton destruction. Perhaps it would be stiil
better if the fine were three months’ gaol. Our Federal
Defence authorities, by the way, eont.nue to utilise emu
plumes for our troops, when a hen’s tail would do just as
well. Tenders are continually being called for the supply
of emu plumes. If it is an offence against State law to
destroy' the emu, how does the militaiy johnny think
plumes are legally' procured ? You can’t overtake the
emu and steal his tail feather while he is running. Per-
haps the military idea is to stimulate emu farming, but
then no mere military man has brains enough to think of
such a thing. Still adequate defence is essential, and if
our soldier can’t fight without the proper bird's feather
in his hat, I suppose the emu must go. But in that case,
for heaven’s sake, let us get our big war over quickly
before the emu is extinct, and before our army is inca-
pacitated for want of the proper ben decoration on top of
its skull.
• * * *
“ Nulla ” : Aborigines are expert “ brace-
let slippers.” The average abo., probably on
account of his pronounced antipathy to hard work,
has remarkably small, symmetrical hands and
wrists, and although, in the eyes of the law, all men
are equal (police boots, for instance, are made on
the assumption that all men’s feet are 12in. long hy
Gin. broad), quite a number of troopers have dis-
covered a large-sized flaw in the theory as applied
to aboriginals and the regulation “darby.” The
notorious Jacky Jacky and the Queenslander
Tommy Ryan are cases in point.
* * * *
“ Walrick ” : Re animal hunger and thirst
endurance. At Euroka station, near Walgett
(N.S. W.), some strange sheep were found at a
general muster. No one turned up to claim them ;
and, as the absence of a “T” on their backs
signified that they were not “travellers,” they
were put into a yard pending advertisement. The
overseer who should have attended to the matter
was summoned to another station next day, and
the sheep were forgotten. Seven weeks afterwards
the sheep were found, all still alive. They were
let out and survived. The yard which had held
them contained no sign of herbage or of water.
Also, once in Clermont (Q.) I knew a horse to be
confined by mistake for eight days without food or
water, and he survived.
* * * *
“H.J.L.” : Re queer methods of catching
fish. Native women of Bartle Bay, near Good-
enough Bay, British Papua, wishing to catch fish,
enter the water with fish spear poised, and stand
very still. Then with a quick motion those who
can do it press milk from the breast. The fish
dart towards the fluid, and are impaled. Other
women simply chew yams and other vegetables,
and spit out the juice.

THE 12th OF JULY.—From Sketches by a Survivor.
July has been a very strenuous month with our artist (festively speaking). The Glorious Fourth, the Battle of the Boyne, the Battle of
Aughrim, the Fall of the Bastille-not forgetting “Hop’s.” own birthday—all occurred in such rapid succession that—well, that he lived
through it all the above; picture testifies. Another such victory, and he is ruined.

POLITICAL
POITNS
The Tory political view : Deakin-Reid
is patriotic. Deakin-Watson is unparliamentary
and unconstitutional. Watson-Deakin isSkeol.
1 ‘ Mr. Reid has proved himself true to the
Orange cause at every turn.” —Testimonial from
Dill Macky at Sydney Town Hall, July 12.
The White Australia policy can keep
this continent from being a black Australia ; but
only High Protection can keep it from being a
blank Australia.
M‘Lean, Turner and M‘Cay intend to
form a Fourth party in the House of Reps, in the
hope that such a “cave” will in time attract
away some of Deakin’s following. But on the
face of things it looks a hopeless project.
Thus S.A.’s returned Agent-General,
Grainger :
There is an awful lot of nonsense spoken about the
actions of the Labor party affecting the London money
market. Financiers there don’t care who is in or out of
power, as long as they are sure of getting their interest
regularly.
N.S.W. Government is desperately eager
—or says it is—to give the Lands Inquiry Com-
mission full and effective powers, but those who
know reckon that there will be found some excuse
for delays and limitations which will make the
good resolve not of much good after all.
“ Come outside,” said Norton, M.L.A., to
.Tessep, M.L.A., “and I’ll beat you to death with
a bunch of your bananas.” But a good deal
depends on the quality of the bananas. Some
bananas would squash into an unpleasant mass in
the air before they came down once on the back
regions of an M.L. A. who got in on the pious and
anti-borrowing ticket.
A rev. Scotchbyterian parson, giving G. H.
Reid a hand the other day, solemnly told his gilt-
topped Sassiety flock that, though Socialism didn’t
imply such things, yet, if it did, it would he very
wicked. Also, things were not incompatible, and
the Almighty must not be forgotten, and we
mustn’t drive capital out of the country. But he
imputed no motives. On the contrary— Then
the Plate went round.
When it isn’t thinking, Queensland official-
dom still drifts into the old extravagant ways.
Recently a boundary-rider was wanted for a sec-
tion of the rabbit fence near Thargomindah.
Scores of men were available locally, and yet
Jawbell’s department engaged a man in Brisbane
at £8 13s. 4d. a month and sent him out. This
little piece of idiocy cost train fare from Bris-
bane to Cunnamulla, and coach fare thence to
Thargomindah.
WHILE WORK WAITS.
The newspapers continue to dr[?]arily print about two
columns of “Reid on Deakin” and “Deakin on Reid
daily.
THE KID: “ Well, you fellows, when you get
through with each other perhraps you'll remem-
ber me."
Although Premier Seddon professes to be
extremely anxious to reform the much-cursed
Legislative Council, there wasn’t u word about it
in the Governor’s recitation at the opening of
Parliament. Wigram, M.L.C., who is disgusted
with the constitution of his own Chamber, has in-
troduced a Bill to amend the Constitution by
making European members elective (Maori mem-
bers to be appointed as hitherto), and limiting the
number of seats.
Bent told Toorak the other night—
The Government had intended to insert a compulsory
clause in the Closer Settlement Bill last session, only it
knew the Upper House would not have passed it.
Bent says to his fair-haired darling—“My cherub,
I love you, and wouldn’t hurt a hair of your head ;
but there’s a bad man who doesn’t love you, and
he’s lying in wait to kill you. So I’ll kill you
myself, and save him the trouble. _ It s .very
generous of Bent, no doubt, but it isn t either
sense or politics.
M.L. brewers, with a sense of impending
danger at the coming elections, leave no
stone unturned to capture the
stray voter who still has a
thirst. A placard in every
M.L. pub. asks in loud type,
‘ ‘ Are you on the roll ? If not,
why not?” Hebe waits for
the second “ round,” and in-
sinuates a pen into the re-
fresher’s fingers, and suggests
that he should apply to be put
on.
What a lot of effort is
thrown away in preoaring
petitions to
Parliament!
Scarcely a day
passes in any
Australian
Parliament
without some
member grave-
ly presenting a
petition “res-
pectfully word-
ed and in or-
der,” which is
received, and
promptly cast
into the dust
heap the mo-
ment the House
rises. The other
day teetotal
Hawthorn (Vic.)
waxed excited
because it had
got together
enough names,
protesting
against the
granting of a
liquor license to
a local club, to
make a strip 30
yards long. This
ponderous reel
will, in due time,
be rolled into Parliament and rolled out
again. It took weeks and weeks to get
up, and it will take three minutes to put
it respectfully in the ash-box, and one
minute to forget that it ever existed.
Exactly 50 years previous to the day when
the Labor Conference, sitting in Melbourne,
decided to again slink the fiscal issue, the People’s
League drew up an outline Constitution for the
then unborn State of Victoria. Clause 8 ran—
“ That the Government and Legislature be pro-
hibited from borrowing any money; and also be
restricted from undertaking works of a public
nature without funds in hand ; and that all Cus-
toms duties or excise taxes of every kind be for
ever abolished.”
A small joke lightened the dullness of
N.S.W. Assembly during last week. Many of the
Government’s ardent supporters were banging the
atmosphere in the interests of the Yellow Pup at
Sydney Town Hall on July 12, when some unre-
generate person telephoned that the Government
was in a large minority in an impending division
and the whip was calling in all stragglers. Then
the members left the Yellow animal to paw the
air without their support, and raced helter-skelter
to Parliament House, to find the Assembly dron-
ing over a machinery clause in the Children s Pro-
tection Bill.
The great anti-Socialist party will fall into
its last slimy ditch when, at the re-opening of
Parliament, it appoints J. Cook to act as its
“deputy leader”—to all intents its full leader,
for G. H. Reid will take no more interest in the
Federal Parliament now that office is off for good.
Cook was lifted from out the coal-hole into Parlia-
ment by the workers, won their stupid confidence
by his smug sanctimoniousness, became leader of
