“Shirley” writes
Walter Preedv, of N.S.W. Agent-General’s staff, has
been visiting Canada, and has, according to Sydney
Evening News, made the easy discovery that the'Stink-
ing Fish party is not much in evidence there. The News
sums up the booming of Canada as 1 A Confidence Trick,
hut fails to locate the manipulator. There is a trick
played on the emigrant, not in untruthful statements as
to work and wages, but as to the possibility ofreturnfor
dissatisfied men; and the culprit is the ghoulish C.P.
Railway monopoly. The fee from London to Calgary
(emigrant rates) is £lO Bs._boat and train. From Calgary
to Montreal (train only) is $6B, or, roughly ,_ £l4 3S;, t
which add boat from Montreal to London, £5 10s. Thus
the emigrant without capital who once sets his foot in the
north-west is trapped. Usually he curses loudly for 12
months. Then, discovering that really things are not so
had after all, he settles down.

Pressmen’s presentations to politicians are
mostly hollow affairs. Barton, Deakin, Watson,
Reid —each has got his pat of shitless butter, and
the barrel of stuff will be drawn on as Prime
Minister after Prime Minister falls downstairs.
Of course, the politicians are deeply moved, and,
of course, the pressmen are deeply moved, and
everybody is more or less moved.
Federal Labor Party platform now declares
for “ the cultivation of Australian sentiment based
on the development of a self-reliant community,”
for “the protection of Australian shipping
against unfair competition,” for “the support of
Australian production by Australian workmen
and Government preference to Australian goods ;
and after all that refuses to endorse straight-out
Protection.
The results, so far, of the
Centennial Park land sales
form an object lesson m the
methods of finance of the great
Freetrade party in N.S.W.
M‘Linen, when Treasurer in
one of the Parkes Ministries,
announced a surplus of
£600,000, which consisted solely
of the estimated value of this
land. A complacent Assem-
bly applauded this remarkable
announcement. Now, after
full 15 years, several well-ad-
vertised sales have succeeded
in placing nearly one-third of
the land for a little over
£40,000, and that goes to make
up another surplus. First you
borrow the money and buy
some land. Then you have an
enormous surplus by crediting
yourself with the enormous
sum you think you could get if
you sold the land. Later you
sell a bit of it, and out of the
scanty proceeds make another
surplus.

N.S.W. Premier Carruthers did consider-
able of a wail last week to a meeting of jewellers
about some very bad people who won’t believe
that his alleged surplus of £187,000 is genuine.
After all, is it wonderful that there are such
people ? When a Premier uses as revenue about
£1,000,000 raised by the sale of the State assets,
reduces the payments to sinking funds by £50,000,
charges about £400,000 of ordinary expenditure to
loans (Carruthers even charged outlay on hos-
pitals, schools, police-stations, penitentiaries, fire-
brigades, libraries, benevolent asylums, and an
inebriates’home to loans as “permanent and re-
productive” shirks), it seems quite reasonable
that people should call his £187,000 a disgraceful
swindle rather than a surplus.
At the Horsham (Vic.) convention of the
Chamber of Agriculture, the other day, ex-
Premier Irvine was billed to administer the open-
ing oration. He devoted three-quarters of his
hoot to a tirade against Deakin, whose action had,
he declared, sent a “thrill of indignation through-
out the land.” The Iceberg almost boiled with
rage, and, after informing the delegates present
that the principal, if not the only achievement of
the Federal Parliament had been to put a duty on
reapers and binders and to place diving apparatus
on the free list, he descended from the stump
without doing what he climbed up for—which was
to declare the convention open. Irvine has been
away for his health. He evidently returned before
his intellect became convalescent. An obliging
pressman put him right, after the meeting, a ou
the duty that never was levied on reapers ana
binders, and Irvine chased up the reporters an
induced them not to blazon his ignorance to
unfeeling world.

Mountain on the jaw. But instead of this it was

on the floor, a pale, agitated Carruthers would
have rushed out to borrow £10,000,000 on the sp
and supply work for everybody.
Digby Denham, Minister for Agriculture,
rarely plays the same tune as the rest of Morgan s
Queensland orchestra. The other day he hoped
a larger amount of loan money would be available
next year”—this immediately following Premier
Morgan’s cable to Bull that “the era of borrowing
had ceased.” The day after Denham had tootled
on the loan theme, Morgan again remarked
that there had been so much borrowing already
that it was hard work to make ends meet, and he
wasn’t going to be fool enough to try - and get out
of the bog by taking the same road that had led
them into it. If Queensland has thrown over
the worn-out system of party Government,
and doesn't profess to have any set Minis-
terial policy, and chooses a Minister for his
administrative capacity and for his views with re-
gard to the one department which lie bosses, well
and good—it will be another thing to be glad about.
Some good may even come out of the showing, m-
an irregular way. that it isn’t necessary for Minis-
ters to pretend to think the same about everything
in order to do good work. The trouble is that,
until the new sensible system is adopted m a regu-
lar way, Morgan and Kidston are liable to be
thrown out on account of Denham s remarks.
Morgan had better bring in an Elective Ministry
Bill and save the situation.

Judge Ileydon’s first important decision
in N.S.W. Arbitration Court was given against
the employers —a large “universal provider
firm. Though, especially at bargain sale times,
its vans are to be found roaming over the distant
suburbs at all hours, the firm objects to pay its
luckless drivers the standard rate of wage fixed
by award of the Arbitration Court. Reiving on
the decision of the High Court re the leasing of
barbers’ chairs, this providery leased its horses,
vans and harness to its employes, who had to
accept the “contract” or the sack. The con-
tract,” of course, panned out for less wages than
those ordered in the award. Happily the Arbi-
tration Court declared that the cases were miles
apart, and unless the High Court can unearth
another mouldy precedent, the mean subterfuge
will not avail. If the leasing principle were
permitted, it could be extended quite indefinitely.
The universal providery could even ‘ lease a
few square feet! of counter to each employee, on a
contract providing that, over and above a few
shillings per week, all takings go to the boss.

VITADATIO
Cures
Gastric Ulcers.
Read this Interesting Testimonial.
20th June, 1904.
Dear Sir,—About twelve months ago I became very ill
with gastric ulcers, so the doctor said. I had terrible fits
of coughing night and day. I could not get sleep,
which completely prostrated me. My appetite fell away
until I could eat scarcely anything ; I was wasting away
from day to day until I was almost a shadow. The
doctor advised me to take cod-liver oil and get away
-for a change as soon as I could, but I got no better, in
fact I kept getting worse ; at last I became so weak that
I thought I would die. About six months ago I was
advised by Mr. Trealoar to try a course of Vitadatio.
My wife purchased one dozen large bottles of Vitadatio,
and by the time I had finished the first bottle I began to
get sleep. When I had taken the twelve bottles I felt a
new man, and was able to attend to my business again.
Right glad I am that I took Vitadatio, and I heartily
recommend it to any person suffering as I did. The
doctor told me he could not do any more for me, and
now I am well and strong.
Yours faithfully,
WM. O. FREEMANTLE.
Dairy Produce and Egg Merchant,
382 Bridge-road, Richmond, Yic.
I can certify to the correctnees of the above ; he was
very low when I pressed him to take Vitadatio, and I am
glad to say he is well again.
J. TRELOAR,
Grocer, Wine and Spirit Merchant,
Nicholson-street, Carlton.

Now Joseph Carruthers threatens that if
the Australian Government does not promptly
found the capital—to prevent the founding of
which he says he will die in the last ditch—he will
refuse to i allow the use of Sydney Government
House to the Governor-General. What a dis-
loyal, rude Joey ! But probably the Australian
Government won’t be intimidated. The G.-G. can
sign Bills and things in Melbourne, or Adelaide,
or Hobart, as well as in Sydney, and the notion
of keeping up two huge Government Houses for
his use is really an absurd extravagance.
The gaol-keeper at Mount Gambier
(S.A.) has just given all the State J’sP. (and some
police magistrates) a lesson in law. A local
prisoner, being sentenced for a fortnight as a
drunk, and for another month on a second charge of
theft, the gaoler calmly released him at the end of
two weeks, explaining that, in the days of
Attorney-General Mann—long* dead —orders had
been sent out that cumulative sentences are
illegal. The present A.-G., being appealed to, has
decided that illegal they are, in spite of general
practice to the contrary. And now the hosts of
delinquents who have served cumulative sentences
during the last 20 years are lamenting that their
luck did not land them in the gaol of the one man
who really understood the law.
An utter foolishness—more foolish even
than the debate on it —is the function of present-
ing the Address-in-Reply to the Governor. The
Speaker and his staff, together with as many
members as can be cajoled into the game, troop
with the non-committal document, which the
Government Printer has spent much time in titi-
vating up, and present it to his Ex. As likely as
not, a month has been spent in angry recrimina-
tion since the Governor addressed Parliament,
but he says nothing about the want of manners in
keeping him waiting for an answer. Sometimes,
as has happened in the Federal Parliament, there
is a crisis, and the luckless potentate does not get
an answer at all; which fact does not worry him,
either.

A BOM A FIDE PASTOR ALISTS’ INSTITUTION.
A Chinaman is hard to kill. In the Bairns-
dale (Vic.) district, 1; st week, Ah Look, aged 68,
got separated from a couple of octogenarian com.
patriots as they were crossing some ranges, and
for three days he wandered about in the bitter
cold, blanketless and almost foodless. Finally,
Ah Look was restored to the bosoms of the two
80-year-old Chows in an all li’ condition. The
restoration, by an energetic policeman, was a
merely formal proceeding. The lost one, when
discovered, was doddering along to his destination
on his heathen “own,” quite unconscious of the
fact that he ought to have been dead under the
circumstances.
The man who calls loftily for “ English,”
and turns up his nose at Australian beer, should
note this: Brewery investigators in the Cold
Country have discovered that “ the full develop-
ment of the adulteration system is seen in the
export trade, where preservatives are used in
excess to keep beer which, from the poverty,
uncleanness, or inferiority of its ingredients,
would not otherwise stand the voyage through the
tropics ; till a case of apparently most innocent
lager, bright, light, and wholesome, is really full
of cumulative chemical poisons.” Perhaps, after
all, the beer responsible for the recent Melbourne
poisoning case was simply a bottle of adulterated
English.
“G. Elburg,” re the Adelaide correspond-
ent who wrote about the Fatman’s charity (P.
22/6/’O5): —
This posing by the employer person as a wholesale
philanthropist is sickening. Since the S. A. State ele< -
tions numbers of people have gravely assured me that
“Stummick and Co. have given notice to 100 men,”
“ Hog-son’s are discharging 50,” &c. Such tales are too
asinine to impress any but those of no intelligence. The
employer does not employ a single hand more than the
volume of his business demands, and the election of a
dozen or so Labor representatives, who have as yet no
voice in Parliament, has not obviated the necessity for
wearing boots or clothing, nor have the people’s appe-
tites (except, perhaps, in the case of a few Tories)
diminished since the elections.
Some day a disgusted country will be
driven to pass a law absolutely prohibiting any
registered newspaper publishing the remarks of
any priest or parson delivered between the 10th
and 15th July. S. Australia has already a law
which decrees certain close seasons for Parliamen-
tary candidates ; and Dill Macky will yet drive
this country to protect itself against blastiferous
sectarian strife and the prostitution of its Parlia-
ments, by making a close season also for the
insects who lose their heads annually on the anni-
versary of some poor little irrelevant foreign
scrap in a bog down Limerick way when a French
general got his head blown off. It will really
be a charity to many of the insects. Some of
them, of course, are professionals at the muck-
raking business, and deserve only contempt and a
brick ; but the rest are, at other seasons of the
year, well-meaning enough fellows, who would as
lief drink with a Micky as a Macky; and it would
be a kind law which would burv their annual
disease in oblivion with the rest of the unpublished
matter. Probably, in the phrase of the Argus
and the Herald, the time is not yet ripe for the
new law; but Dill Macky and his “push” are
ripening it fast.
From “ Saints and Savages,” a story of five years’
experience in the New Hebrides, by Dr. Robert
Lamb: —
In the first place these natives (Kanakas) are not con-
stituted for such hard work (the carrying’ of green
bunches of bananas to the trading vessel). We, as mis-
sionaries, live with them, study them, and know them.
I tell you they are not able for it. Look at those “ boys ”
on the jetty yonder. One of them came to me with a
ruptured vessel at the back of the eye. He was carrying
a sack of potatoes, and suddenly went blind in that. eye.
Another was lifting a sack of flour and strained his back.
Inflammation followed, and he was carried to the hospital
and his pelvis found to be riddled with abscesses. The
sacral wedge, or the keystone of the pelvic arch, had
given way. There was no hope for him; and he died
after a few weeks of profuse suppuration. It is the
same with the natives who go to Queensland. They can’t
stand the long hours, and the sudden changes of climate ;
and you know the great reproach against the trade is that
it kills three or four Kanakas to one white man. Their
tissues are too soft. They get consumption ; come home
to die ; and infect their fellows It is sheer nonsense
to say that these Kanakas can do work on plantations the
white men cannot stand. Say, if you will, that the black
skin is the cheaper article, despite the death-rate, and
we understand each other.
The germs of disease wage war upon the human
body. They enter insiduously the human frame, attacking
it in its most vital part, undermining the constitution
until the whole is irretrievably ruined. Science is taxed
in every way to cope with the source of universal and pre-
mature death. In such extremes where should the sick
man seek relief ? He should consult the specialists
of the Freeman and Wallace Institutes, Sydney and Mel-
bourne, who are renowned for their skill and reliability in
the treatment of men’s dangerous deadly diseases. They
excel because they have made these diseases their special
study, and they cure more men of their troubles than
any other firm of specialists in Australasia. Is it not
reasonable to expect that they should excel where they
treat thousands of cas s, when the average medical man
only treats hundreds? If unable to call personally, a
letter or post card will bring the sufferer into active tomb
with the specialists in a few days from any part of
Australasia.*

Alfred seems to be going in on a good
wicket. With the assured support of the Labor
party on a definite programme, and the assistance
of D.C.L.— not the degree, but the whisky—the
Deakin team should be enabled to score heavily
in their first session. After all, the best policy is
the policy of seeking the best policy from D.C.L.
the “spiritual ” foster father of Wisdom.*
Most Politicians need a box
To drown their ravings in,
The Poet wants a few hard knocks,
The “Dead-beat ” wants some “ tin.”
The Pillman makes a bold display
The public to allure—
But the man who sneezes night and day.
Wants Woods’ Great Peppermint Cure.
Indigestion.
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills Cure it Per-
manently, Cure it so it Stays Cured.
The causes of indigestion are many, but more
frequently arises from improper kind of food,
hasty eating, over-indulgence in spirits, foul air,
excessive brain work, and worry. The eating of
rich foods at all hours prevents the from
properly digesting them, and decomposition and
fermentation take place, which cause a gas to form,
which, pressing on the heart, makes it palpitate,
or there is a disagreeable gulping of wind, or
perhaps the food sours and lays like a lump of lead
in the stomach. This produces weakness of the
stomach, and before the person is aware of it he
has dyspepsia fastened upon him. For this dis-
tressing complaint there is nothing like
Morse’s Indian Root Pills. They cleanse the
stomach of all its impurities, aiding m a mild and
gentle way the digestive and nutritive organs m
assimilating the food.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
bn Saturday week, Sydney saw the N.S.W.
Rugby team trodden in the dust by the Maorilanders (19
to nil). On the following Wednesday, the Metropolitan
team was beaten by 22 to 3. On Saturday, nigh upon
40,000 people gathered to see the crowning defeat. The
N.S.W. team was pale blue, while the Maorilanders were
black as their own shingle beaches. From the first, it be-
came apparent that the Cornstalks had seen the folly of
waiting till the ball came to them, and their forwards
hurled themselves on the leather in a way that was good
to see. Maoriland was astonished. This was something
it never expected, and the Maoriland wing-forward came
rushing round the scrum to collar the N.S.W. scrum-half,
and hold him, before he got the leather —and was penalised.
Referee Howe had got over his attack of fetish worship,
and every trick found his whistle blowing. Still, in spite
of the unexpected alertness of both N.S.W. forwards and
the referee, the Maorilanders slowly forced the light
blues down towards their own goal line. Then they came
with a wonderful rush, but N.S.W. full-back Penman was
a gem far above rubies, and he kicked coolly and accur-
ately on the line, and rush after rush was sent back by his
beautiful footwork. It was a strenuous struggle, and
raged back and forwards between the central lines till
the Maorilanders were struggling right on their own line
and only avoided disaster by forcing. Then the Corn-
stalks began to take long shots at the goal from marks,
and the Maorilanders, playing a hideous off-side game, fell
in for a whole succession Of penalty kicks, which neutral-
ised a lot of their beat deeds. Their trouble was that
they could not get the ball out to the backs on account
of the smothering tactics of the Cornstalk forwards,
who were virtually playing a wing forward. When at
last they did score it was after a struggling rush, in
which the ball bounded across the line. One of the
Maorilanders managed to rush over and -fall upon it.
The shot at goal failed. Up to half-time the people had
not seen one of the brilliant passing charges for which the
Maorilanders are so famous—every attempt to get the
ball out to the backs having been smothered. At the
interval the score was Maoriland 3 (for a try), N.S.W. nil.
“Wait till the second spell, and we’ll go
through you like a geyser through mud 1 ” said a hard-
faced Maorilander beside the writer. Both wind and sun
were now in favor of N.S.W., and the blues dribbled
right down on to the Maoriland line. After a desperate
struggle the blacks were beginning to work it back again,
when Russell, of N.S.W., took a mark, and Smith kicked a
goal. The waving of the straw hats on the hill looked like
foam caps on a heaving sea of ink, and a roaring as of
many bull-elephants marked the fact that N.S.W. was
leading by a point. It sent the Cornstalks quite mad,
and the Maorilanders grew desperate. Hitherto the
Cornstalk forwards had been the centre of attraction,
but now their backs came Out, and with strong passing
rushes carried the ball right down the field. The Maori-
lander full-back Gillet kicked steadily for the line. When
with a great effort the Cornstalks hurled the struggling
Maorilanders right back over their line, and a long,
blue figure leaped for the sphere, Gillet delibe-
rately tried to kick the ball in among the crowd.
It struck the barbed wire, was punctured, and
he managed to save the situation by falling on it.
The Cornstalks got the ball again, and passed it back and
forwards across the front of the Maori goal like a crowd
of jugglers. Every attempt of the blacks to smother it
was eluded till, from a seven-man pass, Burleigh, who had
already bandied it twice, got the ball again, and kicked a
beautiful goal with his left foot. After that the
general condition of the crowd was one of frenzy.
Murnin was playing a wing-forward game for
N.S.W., and, backed up by Anlezark, led a
rush which looked like an inevitable score, when Anle-
zark was badly injured and had to be carried off. When
he recovered and came on again he was still groggy, and
the respite saved Maoriland. Time was running out, only
five minutes to go, and then from a scrum in the centre
the Maorilanders got the leather. For once the Corn-
stalks made no attempt to smother. Like a flash the
Maoriland backs were in motion, and across the field it
went. All in a moment the Cornstalks recovered them-
selves, but it was too late, the try was scored and the goal
successfully kicked. By just one moment of the old inert
play, N.S.W. had lost its chance, and the game that should
have been a victory for Australia ended in a draw-
eight points each.
■* " * * *
Otago University men had better luck than
their countrymen at Sydney Cricket Ground on Satur-
day. The so-called combined team of past and present
players was a very disorganised rabble, and the Maori-
landers stamped on it. Score was 14 to 3.
* * * *
Under Australian rules the Y.M.O.A. broke
loose again on Saturday, North Sydney being curled up
by 44 to 30 points. It was a good game, each side
attacking and scoring in turn, but the Y.M.O.A defence
was stronger than that of the Hill-men. Paddington
managed to re-assert itself at the expense of Sydney,
winning by 59 to 37 points. The Redfern-East Sydney
match, at Redfern Park, was like a battle of giants ; the
side that had the sun in its favor won, and the inter-
change of ends seemed to favor Redfern, which came out
on top by 51 to 42. The Newtown-Balmain struggle was
a romp over, Newtown winning by 75 to 41. Y.M.C.A.
and Sydney are now level in the competition with 18
points each, and Newtown, by virtue of its win over
Balmain, comes next with 16 points. The others are just
visible in the distance.
....
Football in Melbourne continues to supply
interesting games, with good crowds to watch them. The
only notable occurrence last Saturday was the disaster
that overwhelmed Fitzroy at Geelong, where the ex-Pre-
miers went down bump, scoring but 2 goals 15 behinds to
the weasiders’ 9 goats'B behinds. As ’Boy was heretofore
level with Gollingiwood at the top of the list (one
defeat each), and Geelong was at the other end of the
ladder, this can only be called a “ surprising reversal of
form”; but this is about the season when one looks for
the gentleman named S. Reversal —sometimes he is a
person who meanders round with a bag and a hooked
nose. Of course, ’Roy’s kicking was vile, but even had
they- scored 17 goals to the others’ 9, they would
still have been short of their true form, and there were
no notable absentees from the side. “Sowth” defeated
Carlton on latter’s ground —a feat it hasn’t accomplished
since ’01 —by 3 points ’(6l to 58); but, though the best
teanv won, it was only.by good luck, as Carlton’s la.st two
behinds should have been sure goals. In the Association,
Williomstown, West Melbourne and North tie in the
lead,’ with Richmond a defeat to the bad, and the
regrettable “ Ports ” another loss more. These same
“ Ports ” met “ North ” on Saturday, finishing with 29
pointsto 54, and their failure to trample on their opponents
so annoyed their barrackers that after the final bell they
rushed the Northern players, and started to hammer the
pavilibn with them and otherwise induce gravel-rash and
similar excrescences. Players on both sides and
promiscuous barrackers had a lively mix-up for several
minutes before a couple of troopers got in and started to
ride over everybody. The umpire wasn’t killed—but that
was probably because he got out of harm’s way before the
discussion commenced.
* * * *
They take their “College football” very
seriously in Adelaide. This year’s match between the
two leading schools, played on the Oval, had a test-match
attendance so far as the stands were concerned. Prince
Alfred beat St. Peters by 7 goals 11 behinds to 4 and 12,
and a city sharebroker presented the two best players on
the winning side (chosen by the team’s own vote) with a
Great Boulder share each. Dolling, who made 311 for
the same side in the cricket match and received a B. Hill
Proprietary share for it, was playing, but is nothing out
of the common at football.
* * *
A little District Union, controlling Rugby foot-
ball at Coventry (England) is up against a hard thing.
Bilton won the local cup final, and the trophy was handed
over; after which NeiVbold, the losing club, appealed (on the
ground of encroachment by spectators). Appeal upheld,
and a fresh game ordered. Bilton refused to play again.
Newbold men turned up at the appointed time, went out
in their ordinary clothes, scored a try, and were awarded
the game. Now the Union has to get the cup back from
Bilton, and there seems to be no process of law that will
do it.
A * -n *
Poor old Harry Trott wrote to the Bendigo
Advertiser last week, complaining that Warren, the
Derbyshire fast bowler, didn’t play for his county against
the Australians because “ the English cricketing authori-
ties do not wish him to give the Australian Eleven any
more practice against his bowling than they will get in
the next test match.” Considering that Trott has
captained one or two teams through an English tour,
he ought to, know that county cricket teams are under
local management. The Derbyshire people care- a lot
more for their own “glory” than for the question
whether Warren will be fresh or stale to the visitors when
he meets them at Manchester, if he does meet them.
Warren was not such a huge success in the last test, any-
how. /
* * * * -
The “off” and “leg theory” practices of Aus-
tralian trundlers in first and second tests roused the
English papers to fury. Considering that in the third
test these tactics were even more patent, the next batch
of English papers will probably be quite unfit for reading.
The Australians do not seem to be put out by the storm
they have raised, and remark that they did not visit the
dear old mother land to bowl so that her stay-at-home
children might win. If the tactics are illegal, well there
are umpires ; and, anyhow, the team is there to win if pos-
sible, and if that can’t be done, to be beaten by as little
as it can. The Englishmen are free to try any theories
of their own, and as long as the umpires don’t make
any remarks the Australians will just have to put up
with them.
* * * *
Armstrong’s deeds with bat and ball in England
stamp him as the best all-round player of the XI. In bat-
ting he has left them all far behind, with the exception of
Noble, and with the leather he is a good second to La ver.
It makes one’s hair stand on end to think what might
have happened to Australia if Armstrong hadn’t gone
with the blessed team.
* * * *
He Armstrong’s 303 not out. Previous big Aus-
tralian scores in England are: Trumper, 300 not out;
Murdoch, 286 not out and 211; Noble, 284 ; Armstrong, 248
not out; Graham, 219; and Massie, 206. This last was made
in 1882, and, being the first big score from Australia, it
created more excitement than Armstrong’s.
* * * *
Next Saturday will see the struggle between
Towns and Stanbury, on the Parramatta River, for the
sculling championship of the world. Towns and Stan-
bury, being good business men, beat the Boat Trust and
the people who own the foreshores, and made them dis-
gorge a fair proportion of the prospective lucre that will
be paid by the public to view the race. Towns is a light-
weight, and will probably row at about lOst. 101 b.,
while big Jim Stanbiiry will have a displacement
of somewhere about 12st. 81b. Stanbury has the
strength, Towns has the skill. In the days
when he was champion in the long ago, Stanbury was
gritty and had the endurance of several men ; but
whether that has come back to him in the course of his
long and strenuous training no man knoweth. That
Towns can last the distance irrespective of pace no one
doubts, but there are many who have a firm belief that
the first mile will exhaust Stanbury. It is generally
understood by those who pin their faith to him that Stan-
bury will make the pace hot from the first, and hurl his
new Neilsen boat through the water at such a rate that
Towns will have no hope of making up the leeway. If he
can get far enough ahead in the first spurt he may win,
but the writer’s idea is that if Towns holds him the first
mile, Stanbury’s name is plain Mud. All the same,
although he will back Towns at level money, he will take
odds on either man.
* * * • ■
Unaffected by the excitement of Maoriland v.
N.S.W., at Sydney Cricket Ground, where players foamed
at the mouth and lady barrackers fainted, the lacrosse
players of Sydney went on with their premiership com-
petition. At Petersham Oval, Balmain and Marrickville
played a fast and well-sustained game. Balmain had the
advantage of the sun, and scored three goals in rapid
succession. In the second quarter, Marrickville at-
tacked for all it was worth, but the Balmainiacs were
as strenuous in defence as they had been in attack,
and kept the ball out. The whole trouble with
Marrickville is that it goes in for brilliant indi-
vidual play, and an ounce of combination goes further
than a ton of individualism. The third quarter found it
struggling desperately, but every man played more or
less on his own ; and the result was that the attack was
beaten off, and a sudden rush saw the enemy score
another goal. After that the Marrickvillers lost hope,
and the game ended with Balmain 6 to nil. The Burwood-
North Sydney match did not attract much attention,
both sides being represented by weak teams. Still the
passing was unexpectedly good, and play was fast and
open. Altogether, it was a much more exciting game
than was expected, and North Sydney won by 9 goals to 7.

Australian cycling promoters are inclined to
back down on their former decision to pay nob.gappear-
ance fees and give the extra cash as prizes. They begin
to whisper that the foreigner will noo come on the mere
chance of winning big sums ; and they have an idea that
if they cut the prizes down by 50 per cent, they would ge
just as large entries of local riders. In the meantime, the
wheelists are putting on all the pressure they can in a
noseless, underground sort of way, and it’s an interesting
situation. * # *
As anticipated, Major Taylor has, in conse-
quence of his suspension by the Yankee governing body,
announced his definite retirement from the track. He
will devote the rest of his days to driving round Worcester
(U S A.) in his motor-car, ana collecting his rents. Aney
amount to enough to keep him in neat if not gaudy
luxury.
* * * *
“Cyclist ” : Re “Down the Range” bike yarn,
by O. A. Jeffries (B. 29/6 ’O5). Anyone who attempted to
ride down such an awful descent as he describes with his
feet on the front forks, deserves the catastrophe he will
most probably get. Let me advise anyone who has to ride
down a real hill: If a fixed wheel, back-pedal and brake
as well; then both wheels are steadied, and the strain on
front forks lessened. If brake or chain goes, jam one foot
into front fork, over the wheel, and brake with it. If hill
is very bad and the bike a free-wheel, or you want to be
on the safe side, drag some bushes behind by a stout cord
12ft. or so long. This is the safest brake cf all, and the
dust it creates is very impressive—for the next man.
Have put in two years among the hills in the Hill End
(N.S.W.) district, where 1200 ft. steady drop in three miles
is by no means out of the way, and have disted all these
hills with the bush brake.
* * * *
Famous sire Far Niente, descended from Gal-
liard and the Doncaster mare Lotus, died at Tucka Tucka
(N S W.) t’other day. He was foaled 18 years ago, and
imported five years'later. Far Niente did well racing.
Among the best-known of his get were Ampler, Kenley,
The Idler, Undaunted, Sequence, Pendant, and Bivalve.
* * * *
Somebody, with a lot of misdirected energy, has
gone into the matter and discovered that last year 84
saddling-paddock, 108 Leger and 52 Flat (flat refers to
locality and not to character) bookmakers paid £3700
to the’A.J.C. for betting privileges. If the same men
pay this year they will contribute £9520 to headquarters,
£1845 to Rosehill, £3OBO to Canterbury Park and Moore-
field, or a total of £14,445, for the privilege of betting.
Add the wages of clerks, their registration (£1 each),
price of admission and other incidentals, and consider the
way they live, and the diamonds they wear, and the result
will give some idea of what an almighty idiot the punter
is—for it is the punter wh > pays for it all.

“Dux” : Re racehorse nomenclature. St.
Leger opened up the saintly horse calendar in M.L., and
his offspring blossomed forth as Saints Peter and Paul ;
Saints Clement, Hilda, Andrew, etc. Then high-toned
Saints Ursula, Olga, Vincent, etc., came on the turf.
The fashion has spread to the hacks, and Saints Bill and
Joe, also Jimmv the Saint, are forerunners of possible
Saints Dinny, Andy, Jerry, Mickey, and worse. Other
atrocities are Scrap-iron Jack, Noxious Weed, Local
Option, Taxpayer, Shanks, Swop, and Wet Blanket.
The M.L. Racing Calendar publishes with
monotonous regularity the “ unpaid forfeit list,” every
week of the year, and sportsmen are beginning to find it
an eyesore and a genuine turf nuisance that ought to be
obliterated. It is a long list, and some of the amounts
have been owing to clubs over a dozen years. The total
amount is something over £l5OO, and the conference is to
discuss two propositions—one that all amounts over six
years old be wiped out, and the other that all amounts over
three years ditto. The moral is that racing clubs should
do business on a strictly cash basis.
* * * *
“ Ku-Klux ” : Outside the Caulfield racecourse
is a spot locally known as Battler’s Hill. Here is con-
gregated every race-day a marvellous collection of pun-
ters. Betting'is openly carried on, the “bookie” giving
no ticket. He simply takes his client’s initials, which are
mostly jotted down on his shirt-cuff, if he has a cuff,
which he mostly hasn’t. You can sometimes trust him
with a “ tray.” Bet a “ sprat,” and you should attach
yourself to him with a padlock till the race is run. Bet a
“ deener,” and it is necessary to hire two pugilists of good
staying power to stand one on each side till pay-out time.
Between races the man who can “ head ’em ” a score of
times unfailingly does a good business. The man with
the wheel of fortune also plies his trade “as low as a
penny and as high as you like,” for he “ bars no stakes, no
way of playing.” Somehow the needle always stops at
the number on which nobody has bet.
* * ♦ *
“ Beau S.” : Years ago I knew an old fisherman
in Sydney town who swore he knew every fish in the har-
bor. Well, if he didn’t know every fish, he could tell
most of them by the way they bit. He was to be found
almost every day on a little jetty near Mosman’s with a
hand-line. As he’d get a bite, he’d say, “Ah, a trum-
peter,” or whatever it was, and he was invariably correct.
One day fish were scarce, and he dozed off there in the
sunshine. We hauled up his line, hooked a tin on it, and
put it down again. By-and-bye he awoke. Something
seemed to be nibbling. He jagged. “ Feels like a tommy-
rough,” he muttered with a puzzled air, and hauled up
vigorously. “ No, it’s a skate ! . . . No, it’s a yellow-
tail.” The tin skirled about as he pulled it up. “ No,
no—l know what the dashed thing is. It’s a schnapper—
yes, a schnapper—no, that’s not a schnapper’s bite—it’s %
dog-fish.” But just then the tin came up. “ No,” he
said, “it’s a blanky fool.”

The progress of the motor ! In Adelaide an
old church has been turned into a motor-shed.
* • * *
M.L. Government has been tinkering with the
Gaming and Lotteries Act once again, the principal
clause in its new measure being directed against agencies
for the sale of “ Tatt.’s ” sweep tickets. But the legisla-
tion will prove of little avail, as money can always be sent
to “Tatt.’s” through the banks. And the Government’s
own bank sends as much as any.
* * * *
English sporting papers record that dog-racing
is very popular in the East End of London. The dogs are
regularly trained —and it is rarely that one running at
catch-weight scores a win. The distance is usually 130 or
150 yards.
* * * *
A London paper informs an admiring world
that Edward VII., “at Dunrobin Castle in September,
1870, organised a cricket match against the servants on
the estate, and scored six before he was bowled. ” And
what minion dared to bowl him when he was just about
to score seven ?
* * * *
Woollahra still leads in N.S. W. baseball compe-
tition, and on Saturday downed Leichhardt by an innings
and 10 runs, a most unholy d feat. Paddington came a
great smash in the Centennial Park, University trampling
on it by 25 to 9 runs. The close match of the day was
that of North Sydney v. Chatswood, when the hill
men downed the Middle Harbor suburb by one lonesome
run, the scores being 20 and 21. Petersham beat Burwood
by 9 to 7, after an uninteresting game. But then baseball
is always uninteresting.
Geo. Cockshutt, the Hobart boxer who recently
defeated the best man the visiting fleet could produce,
and more recently belted light-weight Maher out under
the ropes in less than two rounds, is a sturdy lump of a
footballer with some pugilistic cleverness. A year or two
ago he was first in the most murderous go-as-you-please
contest ever held in Australia—the whisky trophy race
from Hobart wharf to the pinnacle of Mt. Wellington and
hack. The run was accomplished in excellent time, and
two of the competitors perished in the snow. Cockshutt
was in football training at the time, and won by a few
yards from the next man, who, half blind from exhaus-
tion, fell several times within the final half-mile.
• * *
The Harold Smith versus J. Blackmore scrap at
Sydney Gaiety on Saturday night was not an exhilarating
sight. Blackmore was obviously the better man, and the
curious way Smith fought suggested herculean effort on
the part of a trussed fowl. The only things worthy' of
mention were the amazing antics by which Smith escaped
some severe-looking swipes. There is no one at present
in these parts who hits such a dreadful-looking hit as
Jake Blackmore, and some of the smites he smote at
Smith, and which Smith escaped by turning himself
inside out and swallowing the lot, were dreadful to view
with the naked eye. But a scrap in which one man con-
tinues to save himself by a series of contortions becomes
wearisome, and it was only a case of Blackmore getting-
one or two in. Smith was down repeatedly, and at last
the unfortunate contortionist was caught in the sixth
round before he had time to again turn himself inside out,
and was extinguished with a succession of blows on either
side of the point.

“K.K.”: A few weeks ago, while crossing
Albert Park one evening, I came on an excited group of
young men earnestly refereeing a healthy fight. During
a lull I recognised the combatants as two well-know n
cyclists. I learned that the fight Was the result of a race
which had been “ fixed up ” beforehand. After the race,
the winner (one of the fighters) had refused to part the
other man’s share of the spoil. I watched the melee .or
some time and then inquired the amount involved. It
was 255. Shade of Zimmerman ! Two men, if not actually
first-rankers, at least well up, battering each others
features for 255. !
* * * *
A pair of full-grown lads who quarrelled about
a girl, and agreed to fight it out in a paddock at East
Brighton (Melb.), were brought to court and fined 40s.
apiece for assaulting one another. What a shocking dis-
couragement to honest sport ! If it had been a gate-
money entertainment, the winner to take 75 per cent, of
the net profits, the same two lads might have fought and
bled with impunity, but because they did it for love the
Bench charged them forty shillings each.

“Jab.”: Re “H.G.” (Bulletin 13/7/’O5) on
amateur boxers’ lack of condition as compared with pros.
Explanation is that the amateur almost without exception
does 10 times the work of a pro. in the ring. Boxing in
public is new to him—his brain is at high tension alway s,
which is a big strain in itself; he is at the extreme of
nervous anxiety every moment, and he Jigs and jumps
and keeps his arms moving as if galvanically controlled.
A pro. need have no condition at all—may be absolutely
hog fat and thick in the wind—and yet he will see the
best conditioned average amateur out at boxing quieaei
than at any other game.

spring of a lodger—and nearly got it, too.
“P. Winkle” : Why doesn’t our working
man, who says he wants a White Australia, help
us to get it? The Waranga Basin Camp (Vic.)
carries over a dozen Hindu hawkers, who coin
money while the white brother starves, the
toilers in bowyangs robbed one white hawker to
his ruin. Now, white men with wares to sell
shun the Basin like Sheol. But the blackfellow
grows fat. They don’t rob him, and the women
simply adore his white teeth. That s about the
extent of white feeling at the scene of Skinner s
murder.

Be the coal-pit trapper boy
You meet him in the chilly dawn of a raw winter morn-
ing, in the dusk of the middle shift, or at midnight on the
dog-watch, but he is always bubbling over with devilment.
Fear he doesn’t know the meaning of. No terrors for
him has that fallen-away feeling in the pit of the stomach
when the driver drops the cage from the ground-level, and
makes his run at full-pelt to the three-quarter limit. The
trapper seldom even troubles to catch hold of the hanging-
bar, but stands in the cage whistling and singing uncon-
cernedly, with a heavy lamp slung in a ring and collar
round his neck, or stuck in his belt, or dangling between
his legs.
The trapper’s work is not hard, but it’s lonely. The
way to his post is often long and rough, and an hour’s
walk below ground is no pastime. His chief work is to
open a door to let the wheeler and his set of skips pass
through and then immediately close it again. Thus is
his name derived, for he traps the main air-current
against his door and diverts it to the inner workings. The
jumping up every now and again to open the door seri-
ously interferes with his manufacture of horse-hair snares
to catch blue “ mountains,” greenies, and red-cheeks in
parrot-time ; so the trapper sometimes fixes a rope on the
door and works it with his foot. He may also give the
wheeler a slew round a curve with the skips and assist
him in spragging-up.
Wonderful is the dexterity with which these little
gnomes can, in the semi-darkness, thrust wooden sprags.
about 2ft. long, into a set of skips going at full speed. In
addition they may have to look after a skip-greaser. One
boy can do' it better than three, but three can invent
more jokes than 12 ordinary mortals.
Generosity is the trapper-boy’s strong point. If he has
only one cigarette-butt of two-draw s-and-one-spit capa-
city, he’ll give you the two draws and have the spit him-
self.
THE CRICKET CRAZE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
This is just by way of answer (lying fever-racked in bed)
From one who never played the game for loot,
To those verses in Thb Bulletin with “Cricket Craze.”
at head,
And the name of one Grant Hervey at the foot.
You are sick, you say, O Hervey! of the cricket things
they talk
At your hash-house,'at your bank and at your club,
In your train and on your tramcar, and by ways you have
to walk,
Not forgetting e’en your theatre and pub.
And you heave a rugged sonnet in a heedless sort of way
At the barrackers who barrack for the game,
Who are “cricket-fiends” and “hoodlums,” cigarette*
stained, so you say,
Beery, ignorant and dull beyond reclaim.
They have mouths just like portmanteaux, “stretching
round from ear to ear ”
(Rather large, that little order, don’t you think ?\
And the players and the playing, so you’d make it all
appear,
Are no class for soulful wallopers of ink.
Right! O Hervey of the hash-house! But some be who
love the play
Who do not soak themselves at city bars ;
And who hold the game, as exercise, a much more manly
way
Than chucking mental hand-springs down the stars.

Melbourne public stayed away considerably
from Sudermann’s “ Fires of St. John,” mainly owing;: to
the azure reputation of the third act, and the rumor that
the Boston authorities had stopped the; play during its
American run. The alleged impropriety is due to the
limited capacity of those shocked rather than to any
shocking intentions on the part of >;,the author or
actors. Strong- the passage undoubtedly is in every
sense, and the curtain falls just jh time. Still,
it represents the straightforward of ; a
pair of lovers, and is decency itself beside .the attempted
criminal assault over which Kerristian iafitilies raved iin
“ The Sign of the Cross,” or the tawdry coarseness of the
first act of “ Zaza,” which the same public swallowed
whole. The people who accept these scenes without
scruple belong to the same class which is horrified at a
nude masterpiece and enjoys giggling over a silly under-
clothing joke.
Now that publication of the fact can bring no harm to
the person concerned, it may be announced that
“King Dodo” was re-written by J. F. Sheridan. The
Widow ranks his roles thus : Ist, author ; 2nd, manager ;
3rd, actor.
Bland Holt shows a strange lack of business enterprise
in not organising a No. 2 company to take some of his
valuable melodramas on tour. If Anderson can make
money with his “shockers” outside of Sydney and Mel-
bourne, there must surely be a demand for spectacular
melodramas of the sort tiiat Bland has a large stock ,of.
Leaving his heaviest productions—“ White Heather,”
“The Great Ruby,” “The Flood Tide,out of the
question, his stock includes some powerful plays with not
more than about 20 characters on the programme. For
instance, “ A Woman’s Revenge,” “The Bells of Hasle-
mere,” “The Fatal Card,” and “The Cotton King,”
wherein there is not a horse-race, nor an army of super-
numeraries to be staged. *' , Jj-
The reports of Henry Irving’s physical decline seem to
have been exaggerated. At latest dates he was contem-
plating another revival (at Drury Lane)-of “The Bells”
and “ Louis XI.” in which be has to play the most arduous
of old man characters. In his actual age and aged
appearance Irving was never so suitable to his mpst
popular parts. He doesn’t lag superfluous .on the stage as
compared with the heavy ancients who impersonate young
men in a.gay, but mildewed, spirit, and he doesn’t invite
ridicule like the antique famous actresses who revive
themselves after a long lapse of years. Lady Bancroft is
said to be itching to face the footlights again, and if she
reappears it will be in “ Diplomacy,” masquerading as a
fascinating creature of 30 or thereabouts, as though she
had the vitality of Bernhardt.
Jack Thompson— son of Joe Thompson, the one-time
Australian bookmaker—has lately made “a pronounced
success ”at one of the London theatres. But how comes
it that his father’s name is dragged into the mention of
Thompson junior’s success? Perhaps the paragraphs
were inspired by the vociferous Joe. ]
“ J. A.C.” : “ Melb.” (B. 6 7/’O5) is slightly in error when
he says that Melbourne Opera House had Habbe’s copy of
his drop at the Sydney Victoria—a Gondola' and Palace
scene. .The original picture was at Sydney Prince of
Wales Opera House, now Theatre Royal, and occupied the
whole of the drop. The reproduction in Melbourne was
on a smaller scale, in the form of a central medallion.

A graduated land-tax has been added to
the fighting platform of the Federal Labor party.
No better idea could have been conceived. It
seems useless to wait for the States to make a
serious attack on the great land monopolies, and
there will be very little real progress till some
one attacks them. Besides, the Commonwealth
wants revenue for Federal old age pensions, for a
public debt sinking fund, for defences, and for
public works, and it must hustle for the revenue
in some direction.
When a Premier has no sense, no policy,
no constructive capacity, no political honesty, no
surplus that can stand investigation, no courage
to tackle large questions, and no genius for detail
to enable him to do much good with small ones, he
has to Yell About Something Easy. The “secret
machinations of Rome” come in very handy. The
“ necessity for combating the evils of Socialism’
is better than nothing. The “development of
our great national resources ” has helped many a
tenth-rate politician out of an awkward difficulty.
The “ gross injustice practised on N.S.W. in the
matter of the Federal capital ” is assisting Joe
Carruthers just at the moment to divert attention
from his awful loan expenditure, and his bogus
surplus, and his wholesale jobbing away of public
lands. And when everything else fails a resolute
desire to “ preserve the integrity of the empire ”
has been found a very useful platitude. Even
“reform of the Upper House” and a desperate
resolve “to restore true Constitutional Govern-
ment in its integrity ” have filled the bill before
now.

Recent advt. in Melb. Aon : “ Theatrical elephant, don-

As the result of a recent U.S.A. magisterial decision,
24 members of the New York Theatre Trust are to be
tried for conspiracy for refusing one Metcalfe, a theatrical
critic, admission to 47 theatres under their control. The
managerial allegation is that the boycott wasn’t a result
of Metcalfe’s “ abusive criticisms ” of their plays, but was
solely to show their virtuous disapproval of his “ scur-
rilous and libellous attacks on the Jewish community,
from which a large number of our patrons are drawn.”
Van Biene got into a newspaper argument in Adelaide,
sundry patrons writing to complain that there were only
six players in the orchestra. The ’cellist replied by sim-
ply quoting his contract with Musgrove, the latter being
bound to supply “an efficient orchestra, in number
according to the towns visited.” V.B. somewhat ambigu-
ously offers to forward from London an article on his
Australian tour, “which, I promise you, will be most
interesting copy.”

“ E.” writes :—“ Music-maker l’etersen has been reap-
pointed Melbourne University Professor for another five
weary years. During his first attempt, he hasn’t given
one really good concert; and his best tide to fame is that
he wears nice long hair and waves the baton in a shivery
kind of style, like grass blowing in the breeze. Also, he
thought bandsmen’s wages should be reduced (he comes
from the Cold Country). But he is a typical professor,
and that’s saying the best and the worst of him, and cast-
ing no needless aspersions on his hair.”
The young men of the present Age know how to turn a
pretty compliment with the beaux of the past. For
instance, in the Age notice of the first Melbourne per-
formance of “ Beauty and the Barge,” the writer says of
Mrs. Brough, who occupied the managerial box : “An
audience which packed the house from orchestra stalls to
gallery doors were quite content that she should occupy'
a box, and the first appearance of the well-known figure
there was the signal for a clamorous and continued out-
burst of applause.” What could be neater?
Samples of humor from the new Brough co.’s play,
“ Beauty and the Barge ” (1) The subtle (military man
damning freely) : “ William ! such language before the
girls.” “ Oh, I don’t mind, auntie ; this book Pm reading
is by a lady novelist.” (Deadsilence.) (2) The Jacobsy:
“ YVell, lam surprised about ’im. Such a nice quiet chap
he appeared first time I met ’im. I found out afterwards
he ’ad a ’eddick that evenin’.” (Faint smiles.) (3) The
cheap and obvious : “ Why, we had a young lady, same
as it might be you, on board last voyage.” “And had
she a chaperon ? ” “ Chap of her own ? Oh, I expect so.”
(Roars of laughter.)
“E.”: What is the secret of American humor? Fuller
Golden tells his yarns, and they’re not hard to take, and
they have the right Yankee flavor. But what is the
flavor? It may be brevity, or idiomatic speech, or pre-
cise exaggeration, or some triviality given philosophic
dignity ; whatever it is the Yankee jokes better than any-
one else. Sandy is supposed to joke “ wi’ deefeculty ” ;
and Bat inclines to fruity verbosity'. John Bull jokes
solidly, like a mass of roast beef. When Leo Dryden
cracks one you hear the Nasmyth hammer fall on the
beef, and it seems to hurt him more than it does you.
The Cornstalk seems to see the humor of tragedy. That’s
Lawson’s way, and, to an extent, Steele Rudd’s. And he
clothes his woe in a scanty but multi-colored garb of
speech. But we get no Cornstalk humor on the stage,
and we could do with some.
Melbourne Playgoers’ Club, started a year or two ago
by a few theatre-reform enthusiasts, has flickered out.
The Play'goers indulged rather in the study of printed
plays than in theatre-going. Works by such writers as
Ibsen, Maeterlinck, Rostand, Bernard Shaw and Yeats
were read, and the performance of some of them by
members was mooted. Difficulty was experienced in
finding enough books to go round ; the Melbourne book-
seller takes no risks of import ng unsaleable books, and
even those of Ibsen and Maeter.-nck are not easily
procured. Since the Play'goers’ opening lecture, when
Blamire Young irritated some members of his audience
by speaking derisively'of Clement Scott as a critic, the
club failed to flourish. After all, the type of drama
praised by Clement (even if his egotism made him an
“unspeakable Scot”) is what goes down w.th most
persons who take any interest in plays.
There’s a broad hint for Australian lady' vocalists in the
following par from the London Referee “ Songs ah -
childhood, or, to describe the majority accurately, child i
songs, are much in vogue just now, but they are 01. i
productive of moments when one has that most uncom-
fortable sympathetic feeling which may be described as t
mental blush for one’s neighbor’s absurdities. In singh g
these songs it has become quite a tradition to deliver tl j
words with an affectation of childishness, with wide-Opent 1
eyes, raised ey'ebrows, and a general endeavor to depii c,
ignorant innocence, which is very trying to behold, par-
ticularly on the face of an artist whose memories of child-
hood must be more or less remote. Children are mor;
often old than young in their ways, and they are alwa. s
serious. Life to them is a wonderland full of strap :
experiences, sounds, and apparitions. Imagine yours i
in a country where the people are giants of 18ft., wh< j
horses stand 30 hands, and dogs tower over your head, a d
you have the proportion of measurement in the mind of a
child, and the reason why they take all things seriously.
Would that parents took them as seriously. If singe.s
would only be as children in their art we should have less
smiles on the platform, but far more sentiment.”
Whatever others may think, the opinion of the drama: ic
“ star ” is that one’s company.

A Woman’s Short Story.
She Teles How Mother Seigel’s Syrup Cured
Indigestion, Piles, and Other Ills.
Mrs. Catherine Stevens, whose home is at 6,
Station-street, East Brunswick, Victoria, has
af- n - a £ sufferer from indigestion. Her
affliction was a particularly painful one, owing to
the complications that followed ; the worst, of
which were constipation and piles; severe
bloating, from wind in the stomach and bowels; a
bad taste in the mouth accompanied by sour
scalding bile rising in the throat; with pain’
lassitude, and general weakness.
She sent a letter on January 28, this year, to
the proprietors of Mother Seigel’s remedies
telling her experience, and just how she was
cured. Here it is:—“For eighteen months I
suffered intense agony from indigestion, constipa-
tion, and piles. I took many medicines and
hospital treatment with no benefit. The indiges-
tion created excessive quantities of wind or gas.
I always had a disagreeable taste in the mouth,
and my food soured on my stomach, causing fear-
ful attacks of heartburn. I took large doses of
carbonate-of-soda to relieve this, but it would
often-times be worse than ever within an hour. A
nasty sour, acrid fluid, too, would frequently rise
in my throat, leaving a sharp burning sensation
along its track.
“Eating generally produced pain at the pit of
the stomach and between the shoulders. On
stooping or bending, these pains became so severe
that I was obliged to lie down until they had
passed away. The constipation, which was of long
standing, resulted in the formation of piles, which
caused me great pain and discomfort. After
attending the hospital for months without avail,
a triend, a little over a year back, gave me a small
quantity of Mother Seigel’s Syrup. I took it, and as
it appeared to do me good I bought another bottle
when the other was used up. This had a splendid
effect on me ; my bowels became regular, and the
piles disappeared, as well as the heartburn and
flatulency. A second bottle of the Syrup com-
pleted my cure, and I have had none of the
complaints since. I cannot recommend Mother
Seigel s Syrup too highly,”*

The Inky Way.
The Bulletin’s London solicitor has secured from
1 iioto. JliTs- a publication which was guilty of wholesale
tnefts-from The Bulletin's columns—an undertaking not
to f urther oftend ; and on that undertaking proceedings
against P Bits have been withdrawn. This paper, whilst
not objecting to reasonable (and acknowledged) quota-
tion, is determined to stop wholesale piracy of its pic-
tures, stones, verse, and articles by foreign papers.
G. H. Reid told the Ballarat Courier lately that Mel-
bourne Age was, to all intents and purposes, established in
tne front parlor of his father's house. Reid, sen., was a
bcouChbytenan parson ; and just about that time the
A.igus was tramp.ing heavily on the brittle Scotchbv-
terian conscience, and making sparks fly from it. George's
father subsequently had his own trouble with the con-
science, and started one of the “free” churches with
which most lands are dotted.
Died recently in London John Augustus O’Shea, a good
Bohemian and a journalist of the first rank. For a quarter
of a century he represented London Standard as a special
and war correspondent. He was with the Oarlists in
Spain, and in t ans during the siege. These latter ex-
periences he told in a series of brilliant letters to his
journal—he always got his copy through, even if he had
a balloon for the purpose—and in a book en-
titled Ilian Iron-Bound City.” His best-known works
-O L I a u es f, ° m t l le ¥ fe of a Special Correspondent,”
Roundabout Recollections,” aud “Romantic Spain”
and he was a voluminous writer of short stories. Writer
hrst met him in Milan some 20 years ago ; he had gone
there to describe the autumn manoeuvres of the Italian
Arrny. He did not trouble the manoeuvres much,
and took no great stock in the Italian Army
lit he managed to conceal both his ignorance and
Ins contempt in the vivid account he sent to the Stan-
dard. In fact, the Italian army manoeuvred better in the
Standard than it did outside Milan. O’Shea sometimes
took liberties with his paper which would have landed
any other man outside on his ear. On one occasion
writer met bun in Fleet-street, when he was goin<>- to
report a race meeting. A preliminary visit was paid to
the -Mitre. One pressman after another, including the
now-famous T. P. O’Connor, came in, and the races were
not that day graced by O’Shea’s presence. However he
n handed in a two-column report, scissored out
oi tne Echo, and said it would do. An editor who was
too .nil for words tried to say something w'orthy of the
occasion, but failed. J
* * * *
The habit of saying that the policeman had a hard and
desperate struggle before he secured the miscreant grows
on the reporter. Grey River Argus (M.L.) lately reported
tba-t the constable had seen‘two men carrying a sus-
picious-looking sack; chased them, and might have
caught them if he had.had more pace. Later on, it was
found that there had been a burglary. But, though the
law had never got close enough to the burglars to know
who they were, the old “ hard tussle ” figured familiarly
in the head-lines.
* * * *
Miss Fanny Elmes, for three years past woman-reporter
of Melb. Argus, leaves Australia by the Afric to take up a
position on London Morning Leader. Miss Elmes
possesses journalistic instinct, as witness her enterprise
in donning old clothes and putting in a day’s work at a
leading jam factory in order to write up the subject from
the inside. She can write as well as journalise, and has
done some good work for The Bulletin.
* * * 4c
“Scribe”: When a journalistic knockabout, I met Gil-
bert Probyn Smith in sundry places. He was a strict
Wesleyan when he first came to Melbourne from India
Considerations of health brought him to Australia. In
India, there must have been wonderful openings
lor hitii. In London he mig’ht have forestalled
Kipling, so far as regards accumulation of Indian
pabulum. I imagine he knew more India than Kip-
ling does. He would depreciate his astonishing know-
ledge of languages by saying that perfect Hindustanee
carried a man all round, but he seemed to know also
such as Tamil, Gujerati, Madrassee and Telegu. Melb
journalism has been rich in Smiths. There was another
G. P. Smith, the slaughterous Age writer, afterwards
M'Culloch’s Attorney-General. He was succeeded on the
Age by Harry J. Smith, known as the Dunolly baker.
He out-Heroded G. P, in ferocious writing. Then to the
bewilderment of the juniors, he became the Argus chief-
of-staff. The eminent James Smith needs only to be
mentidned. W, Jardine Smith wielded a dashing rapier
on the Argus, as chief leader-writer in the Berry era’
Gervase Smith was, and I believe is, a useful journalist
from London, Another Smith is known as the editor of
the Sporting Judge. Then there have been the two sons
of James Smith, viz., Charles Lamb Smith and Tennyson
Smith. Also rope in two Smythes and a Smyth.
. The junior Bennetts have taken over the Lachlander an
influential N.S.W. outback paper. Walter Bennett, the Dad
found newspaper proprietorship the way to politics the
Moruya Times and Dungog Chronicle leading even to the
Cabinet. The sons profess ambitions in the same direction.
* * » *
Speaking at the complimentary dinner to Frederick
Greenwood, the journalist, who began his career with
his “Amateur Casual” articles, and crowned it by in-
ducing Disraeli to buy the Khedive’s shares in the Suez
Canal (which now bring to Britain a revenue of £l,OOO 000
a year), J. M. Barrie stated he was a non-smoker when he
wrote in praise of tobacco for the Pall Mall Gazette
His chief, Greenwood, induced him to write the papers'
and it was not until he read them in book form, under the
name of “ My Lady Nicotine,” that he was converted to
the use of tobacco by his own arguments.

“Croson”: Happened across a quaint specimen of
journalistic enterprise recently in the shape of the
Brunswick (Vic.) Medium. It is a weekly organ, issued at
Id., and contains, in its four pages, six columns of advts
eight columns local news, nine of “ lifted ” literary matter’
and five solid columns every week of this sort of “ per-
sonality,” contributed gratis by friends and admirers •
“ Who is the Carlton girl who goes with Bully, of Preston ?
Surely not Bertha?”—“ Who is the girl of Coburg who did
a mash at the Fitzroy Gardens with the boy who took the
donkey down for his face? Surely not Dorothy G.?”
Some of the “personals” are wildly insulting, and in many
cases'there can be no mistaking- the victims ; but, so far,
the paper hasn’t been sued for libel.
* * * *
The Adelaide ’Tiser reporters have a great admiration
for their employer, Langdon Bonython—an admiration
that is expressed at every opportunity, for the fight for
promotion is exceedingly keen ! If a mothers’ meeting is
to be held, or the foundation-stone of a lost prawns’
home is to be laid, or any other such important function
is on hand, the secretary appointsLangdon chairman, and
is rewarded by having a column report in the paper
instead of an inch or so. Probably three-quarters of the
said column is devoted to what Langdon said. Mr. Bony-
thon recently presided at a small charity meeting, and the
report of the affair ended, “Three cheers were then given
for the King and Mr. Langdon Bonython.” When the
paper reached England, and was delivered at Windsor
Castle, Edward VII. was much affected to find his name
put first. He cabled out that he wasn’t worthy of it, and
that in future the people were to cheer for Bonython first,
and he would get on the best way he could with any ap-
plause that was left over. This cablegram is carefully
preserved among the archives of the State.

One Adelaide weekly is run on sound financial lines.
It has a multitude of correspondents who send in very
personal pars, mentioning only the initials of the very
unimportant persons concerned. To secure insertion a
penny per par must be paid. Thus it costs a penny to
tell that “ Sarah Z. was sitting on P.J.X.’s knee under
the railway bridge.” Sarah Z. buys the paper to see if
the secret has been revealed, and that costs her 2d., and
P.J.X. buys the paper to find out whether anyone has
discovered that Sarah Z. sat on his knee, and that costs
him 2d. And not another soul in the country cares 2d.
what P.J.X. and Sarah Z. did, though it is quite willing
to believe the worst about them.

electorate, as do two other Labor members—Hugh Mac-
donald and Gustavus Miller.

Pierrot : Have just glanced at the children’s column
of a weekly paper. At the foot of every childish letter is
a comment by kind Auntie Jane (most likely the oldest
male member on the staff), who runs the column. Com-
ment No. 1 tells little Jack to name his new pup “Tobv ”
Comment No. 2 incites little Mary to call her new kitten
VVhisk. Further on Aunt Jane urges little Tommy to
designate the new calf “ Dolly.” This sort of literature
must be a great strain on the pressman’s brain
* * * *
Ernest Favenc : Notice the little, affected mannerisms
in the M.S. of writers. As a rule, it is young writers who
a,feet them —writers who have never really done enou<>h
writing to acquire mannerisms. For instance, some never
dot an i or cross a “t”—a very annoying piece of
affectation. Some never write the word “and,” but use
some mysterious Chinese sign instead. Some never punc-
tate, or do it so carelessly that the marks are scarcely
visible. Some carefully punctuate, but affect to ignore
the use of capital letters. But the affectation of illegible
handwriting is the most common, and does the most to
prevent the proof-reader ever getting anything better
than a back seat in the Heavenly Kingdom. Two of the
worst offenders in this respect that I ever knew were
however, old hands. They both wrote a paw that looked
like a cat-fight, and ever since seeing it I have had an
augmented respect for the compositor. The man who
could find a meaning in these fearsome hieroglyphics
could find the North Pole any morning before breakfast.
Both these amazing persons were well-known Bulletin
contributors. Yet with all his ingenuity there are three
words in our language that always undermine the com-
positor’s intelligence. The first is “ lovely ” ; every com-
positor and reader agree, dead against the context, that
it must be “lonely.” Then there is “doze,” that is
always rendered “dose,” even though you print it, as I
have done before now. Lastly, there is “cheery”; no
man with type in his hand can sleep soundly o’ nights
unless he has made it “cherry.” Beyond this I have
nothing but grateful feelings towards compositors, and
hope to meet the whole tribe in Heaven.
* * * *
What glorious opportunities are open for the
smart man in a country of 80,000,000 people. In the
United States there is probably no.conceivable subject
which wouldn't provide a few shrewd bunco men with a
good living when they had gathered their cranks to-
gether. The other day I saw a mass of literature—leaf-
lets, pamphlets and daily paper, all splendidly printed
and illustrated, and evidently turned out by men who
knew the craft of journalism from end to end—devoted to
the intere ts of a sect whose fundamental belief is that
people live inside, not outside, the world. An excellent
sectional illustration on the cover of one pamphlet showed
the earth as a hollow shell with the usual map on the
concave surface, and the sun, moon and stars hovering
about in the middle. There were, apparently no ventila-
tors provided, and I did not read the work carefully
enough to find out why the air didn’t become vitiated.
According to the paper, the sect already numbers 100,000
adherents, and is going strong.
In answer to much carping criticism of the
Melbourne Board of Works, Chairman Fitzgibbon
stresses one point—and with justice. The State
Railway Department and all State and Common-
wealth offices get their water for nothing. At first
glance this looks a matter of mere book-keeping,
but it isn’t. The consumers in these cases are the
whole people of the State—in the Federal case they
are the people of all Australia ; whereas the Mel-
bourne citizens alone have to pay for the conser-
vation and supply of the water, and the amount
thus filched from them means about Id. or in
the £in their rates. Fitz. suggestively points out
that not one of the country water trusts (though
their works were mostly paid for out of the State’s
money) gives the Railway Department a bucketful
for nothing. They aren’t built that way.
THE MEN WHOM I REPORT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Some big in front, and some behind,
Some long and thin, some thick and short;
They are of very varied kind,
The men whom I report.
Some month out platitudes by miles,
And some are cynical and plain ;
Some greet me with obsequious smiles,
Some with reproachful pain.
The alderman, with pompous mien,
“ Puts little items in my way ” ;
The bobby whom I should have seen
. Will hunt me for a day.
The politician scorns me quite
When high upon the platform set ;
He waits upon the mat next night,
A paragraph to get.
One wants to buy me with a drink
(An offer far below my dreams),
And one seems modest—but I think
He is not what he seems.
I meet them sometimes off the chain
In spots where they should not resort;
They wink at me as plain as plain—
The men whom 1 report.
And some are sharp, and some are dull,
And some teach Greek, and some keep stores ;
Some have achieved, and some are null—-
And mostly all are bores.
But on them all I keep my eye,
And to them all 1 listen well,
Because I know that by-and-bye
They’ll have some news to tell.
Big fleas, and I’m the little flea !
At times it seems a sorry sport;
But yet they keep my job for me,
The men whom I report.
VIG.
A.

Devon Consols South Extended, Kalgoorlie,
which holds two leases at north end of Kalgoorlie belt,
shown on old maps as Euclid and Rising- Sun, has been
floated in Adelaide. New co. has a capital of 200,000
shares of ss. each. Vendors receive 120,000 paid up, and
promoters 30,000. Remaining 50,000 are underwritten
and are to be offered to public, with 4s. to be called up.
This means £lO,OOO, of which vendors get half, and other
half remains for working capital. Balance of £1 per
share, or £B5OO, is to be held in reserve for future issue.
During the last year or so this property, from above 80ft.,
has turned out several thousand tons, averaging fully an
ounce. Recently lode has been broken out 17ft. wide,
and even then gave an average of 14dwt. In a winze
below Soft., on junction of sulphide and oxidised zones, a
telluride vein has recently been cut assaying 30oz. for a
width of 18in. Lode runs north and south with a westerly
underlay, and the proved gold shoot is about 300 ft. long.
Mine is a legitimate venture, but whether the large capi-
talisation and small cash working capital are legitimate
are for public to judge. A test parcel of the rich sulpho-
telluride is to be sent to Fremantle smelter, and will
give a high return. Of course, if this class of ore con-
tinues, the venture is beyond cavil. In any case, as a
spec., co. is a long way ahead of shypoo stocks hitherto
dumped by Adelaide market manipulators on unwary
investors.
* * * *
Cumberland, Norseman (W.A.), manages to
maintain its high average, June return being 6960 z. from
531 tons. Burkett, before resigning in favor of O. L.
Fernald, gave ore reserves as 10,000 tons, worth loz. per
ton. Costs have always worked out very high, and,
unless Fernald can materially reduce them and maintain
developments, outlook is not very hopeful. Mine may
continue for some time earning divs., but scrip can never
be regarded as an investment on present basis of working.
* • * *
Golden Zone (W.A.), which has been in the bag
for a long time, has acquired an option on Maori Chief,
Menzies. Manager Hancock is much elated over the deal,
and no doubt scrip will come on the market again.
Maori Chief, like all Menzies shows, is patchy, rich in
places, and the right kind of mine for speculators to deal
in. Probably most of shares have been forfeited long
ago, but a market is sure to be made, and anyone in
search of a gamble will be accommodated.
* * » *
Bellevue, Mount Sir Samuel (W.A.), seems to
be almost on its last legs, judging from poor returns.
Fully six months ago chairman Hoffmann told share-
holders that chlorination plant was about starting to
treat accumulated tailings, which would yield profits of
£lOO,OOO. Bulletin then said the plant would never do
any good ; and it never will. Tailings are assayed to
contain sd'wt. to 6dwt. gold and 2 p.c. copper, and latter
knocks things endways. Years ago, VV. J. Koehler, from
Broken Hill, erected a plant to treat these tailings, but it
failed. When co. was reconstructed, 3£ years ago,
230,000 shares were issued to secure a new 40-head mill
and accessories, and for about 200 acres of sand-patch,
described as deep levels. New mill has now been running
12 months, and all the rosy predictions have been belied.
During last three months co. has lost £6OOO, exclusive of
depreciation and London exes.
* * * *
Some recent Westralian fancy yields. Hidden
Secret, Kalgoorlie, has smelted 8 tons for 1530 z. gold and
23400 z. silver. This brings its total to date to 483 tons
for 79860 z. gold and 37,3220 z. silver. Rich ore is still
showing iii a rise above 200 ft. level and a winze from same
level. Idaho, on south end of Kalgoorlie belt, has treated
60 tons for 3600 z. ; total, since .December, 368 tons for
14460 z. There is still rich ore in this mine, and, while it
lasts, there will be little legitimate mining. Battlers, who
have suffered semi-starvation for years, once they get on
gold follow it ; and one can hardly blame them.
* * * *
Of the 690,000 shares in the newest Chillagoe.
reconstruction, only 355,914 were applied for by the old
holders, leaving 334,056 to be nursed by the underwriters.
The prospects of a market for Chillagoes are, therefore,
about nil, and this should be the ending of one of the
most ghastly tragedies in Australian “ mining.”
