For June Charters Towers (Q.) mills crushed
20,205 tons for 14,7250 z., worth £50,121. Cyanide treated
17,857 tons for 85780 z., valued at £15,608. Smelters and
other returns bring*the total value to £83,656, or a hand-
some £4 2s. per ton crushed. Twenty-three eo.’s and cats
made calls totalling-£8956, including Vesuvius’ll6th of
3d. ;an increase of £4458 compared with May'. Dividends
for June were £ll,lO0 —St. George, 145th of 6d. ; Br.lliant
Central, 64th of 6d. ; Queen Cross, 53rd of 6d. This is
£15,960 less than in May. Totals for half-year : Calls,
£49,852 ; dividends, £105,945. Three Ravenswood callers
and Kalamadu divs. are not included.
« * * •
Block and Wyndham leads in Charters Towers
month’s tonnage, 2876 tons for 21140 z. As it takes £12,460
to pay a div. of 6d., the Block is seldom heard from.
Brilliant Extended put through 2700 tons for 9640 z.
Of this, 200 tons treated separately gave only
lOoz. In May the co. crushed 174 tons from the
same level (No. 7) f<jr soz. Stone was from development
work. Brilliant Central turned out 2410 tons for 14340 z.
Output compared with May has decreased 1000 tons, while
returns arte only 400 oz. less. Mine is pulling itself together
remarkably well. By keeping quartz clean, tonnage could
be further reduced without materially affecting returns.
Something has gone wrong with the Phoebe. Co. had to
turn out 1124 tons to get 878 oz. Concentrates and resi-
dues brought the value to £4547. A serious drop from
previous crushing—Bos tons for llOOoz. Market is flat for
this stock, although co. is in a position to pay divs. nearly
equal to present value of mine. New Queen, a Bull-run
show, 301 tons for 3390 z. Mine has a battery of its own
two miles away, but no stone to keep it going. Lease is
between the B. Central and Q. Cross.
* * * *
Queen Cross, where Bull was crucified at Char-
ters Towers, is having a bad time. For past month 2195
tons were crushed for 2103 oz.—worth,—worth, with concentrates
and residues, £8629. May returns (six weeks) were 3095
tons for 42730 z. ; total value, £18,893. Dividends have
been reduced to 6d. June returns won’t pay that, as it
takes £bo9o to pay 6d. ; this would only leave £3629, or,
roughly, 355. a ton, to cover costs. No mine on the
Towers can turn out stone and crush for that. Dividends
should see a further reduction shortly, as development,
which is really prospecting work, has only just started on
two formations, the Victoria and Victory. These forma-
tions wereHeglected, while the rich patch was worked for
all it was worth. A three-inch leader of white stone and
the stulls make up the Cross’ immediate hope.
t * * *
Wallis’, Charters Towers, is saving up for a 300
tons crushing ; 200 are now on top, and the pile is being
built up at the rate <s( 25 tons a week. Report says that 18
inches of good stpne is going under the old workings. If
so, it will be a good thing, as stone from Wallis’ for the
last six months averaged £7 10s. a ton. Strange thing
about this show is that, as the paddock increases in
tonnage, the market value decreases. Co. should be
above call-making, as six weeks ago there was £lOOO in
hand. Mine is but a small, pokey hole, though, with no
large chances.
* * *
Band of Hope, one of the Towers has-beens,
is prospecting a formation at 1429 ft. So far the reef is
small and broken, 6in. on the footwall and 14in. midway,
in a formation 30ft. wide in places. Co., while on stone
at 982 ft., never paid .any attention to the deep ground.
Everything was left until the old workings could only
turn out 374 tons for the half-year, valued at £3 9s. a ton.
The new formation produced 475 tons, worth £2 18s. a
ton. Total for.s'ix months, 850 tons for £2479. Tributors
collected 435 tons, paying in royalty £246, or 11s. 3d. a
ton.
* r * * *
Carrington’s P. 0., near Charters Towers, seems
to be a mixture of copper, galena, gold, and dead silence.
Show is reported to be looking well, and is to be worked
on advanced lines—for the Towers. An assay plane is
almost complete ! Tests from a lode at about 55ft. give
10 to 14 per cent, copper. Not a word, though, about
gold. A winding plant has been erected, and the erection
of a mill on the site depends upon the water shaft getting
a supply, which seems probable. 00. is still prospecting
with a pilot shaft. Main shaft is down 126 ft., and 140
tons of ore are on top.
« • ■* *
Telegraphed that T. Bennion has struck quartz,
prospecting 30z., in sinking for the Lady Mary reef, Croy-
don (N.Q.). Development promises to be important for
the whole field. At 152 ft., where quartz was tapped,
there was a heavy flow of water, and it is reported that
the Iguana reef is underfoot. Importance of development
attaches to locating the reefs on the east side of the
intrusive granite. Popularly supposed that the old Iguana
reef, Highland Marv reef, and Lady Mary reef were one,
and that present Golden Gate is a continuation of same.
Iguana and Highland Mary, in years gone by, turned out
much gold, former 84,9220 z. from 38,51 S tons, and this
with poor appliances and equally poor methods. Both,
however, lost the gold-bearing stone when an intrusion of
granite came in dipping westerly, and against the
underlie of reef. Right along the line, the lode was cut
out at anything from 300 to 500 feet, and, except in case
of Bennion—who has now tackled the Lady Mary—reefs
were given up as lost. In view of present development,
it is interesting now to recall what Rands, ex-Govt.
Geologist, said a few years ago when shafts were sunk and
bores put down in 'an unsuccessful effort to locate
the reefs on east side of intrusion. Rands said
“ The reef should have been struck in these bore-
holes at somewhere about 200 ft. in deptli bad it continued
on its regular grade. They were all sunk in the hard, in-
trusive granite, and were put down too close to the line
on which the reef was lost. Had they been put down in
the metamorphic granite, further north-east, the High-
land Mary reef would most probably have been dis-
covered.” Rands looks like coming out on top.
• * * *
Ravenswood (N.Q.)is, on the whole, a poorly-
equipped field. Bar New Ravenswood, Ltd., and Ravens-
wood Deep Mines, there’s not a decent mill on the place.
Ravenswood Deep Mines cannot turn out at present
sufficient stuff to keep its plant going, so it is fitting up
same for public crushing. About time some of the
smaller co’s. amalgamated. At present too little money
is spread over too wide an area. Result —very little suc-
cessful mining, and no milling worth the name.
London, Ravenswood (N.Q.), has had a good
crushing ; 346 tons has yielded over £3060, and in the
space of a few weeks the London reckons it has changed
from an impecunious dwarf to an affluent giant. All
on three thousand pounds. Directors have fallen
over themselves in their feverish haste to pay a
div. : 60,000 sixpences will absorb £l5OO on August
Ist ; remaining £l5OO goes to reserve. The idea of
the London talking of a reserve is humorous, for
it has practically no capital. Mine will doubtless
continue to be a “ oue-crushing show ” until it is hit by
two bad consecutive crushings, and then probabilities are
the co. will snuff out. Just like many another Queens-
land show, it won’t see that by a reasonable expenditure
on development the mine might (in this case almost cer-
tainly would) become an industrial concern of long con-
tinuance. In Queensland mining there are three opera-
tions, and three only. They are : (1) Get out some ore ;
(2) get out a crushing; (3) get drunk. Just at present
the London is at the pub.
» * * *
Smith’s Creek (Q.) reports : —“Assays taken in
presence of director, 5 per cent, tin oxide.” Sounds con-
vincing. But why does not the co. give tonnage treated
per week, and value of concentrates produced '! The fact
that 4 p;c. or 5 p.c. of tin oxide is being obtained in a
crosscut 28ft. wide, if the sampling and assays are done
properly, means greater permanency and value for Smith’s
Creek than were indicated in Warren’s report. Two units
of tin, when recovered as a concentrate worth 70 per
cent., should, at present prices (265. per unit), be profit-
able. Mt. Bischoff recovers under 1£ P er cent, tin on an
average.

“Poorly” is the term that the Long Tunnel
(Vic.) autocrats employ when telling how the stone now
going through the battery is doing. It about describes
the sentiments of shareholders in respect to how the board
is doing. Though low-grade stone was going through,
yet no inkling of it could be got till the market told its
little tale. Why not give the amalgam returns weekly,
and let all shareholders know where they are ? There is
no sense in locking up news in a leaky box.
* * * *
C. E. Nicholas, late of the Lyell Blocks, has
been interesting himself in reviving a mine at East
Metcalfe (Vic.), about the past of which some glittering
stories have gained circulation. Out of a north and
south shaft old workers got, according to such yarns,
rich stone; when, taking a rest as diggers do (still
according to the yarns), the water rose, and they never
again got below to the patch. It has taken 40 years for
anyone to enthuse over this “ has been,” but Nicholas is
awakening the Rip Van Winkles of the place. Lyell
Blocks must have knocked all the poetry out of him, or he
could never have encouraged such a title for the co. as
Eliza Hill. There is something homely about that ;
and if ’Liza turns up a trump, what’s in a name ?
In the midst of the tin-boom, Melbourne was
startled by a yard of wheeze about a show called the Star
of Maldon. First of all, it isn’t at Maldon, but poses as a
“virgin show” at Sandy Greek, which district, of late
years, has not borne a reputation that should tit it for
close association with anything so .young and unsophisti-
cated as this pleasant little Star of Maldon. There
never was a mine worth the name in the neighborhood,
and if people are going to put up cash to buy shares in so
unproved a venture m such a locality they deserve all
they may get. Two bits of shafts, the deepest about 30ft.,
and some trenching, constitute the work done. On this
a 32,000 share co. has been floated. Yet it is said that
the Stock, Exchange is dull, and that times are hard in
Melbourne.
* * * *
That deal over the Briseis Extended (Tas.)
shares isn’t too clear, save that it proves that the market
move which carried shares to above £44 rested more on
finesse than on mine results. One fact stands out: that
is, that the board has fixed a valuation on the scrip of the
co. at £35. How the deal could work for the London
firm is easy to see. Firm got 10J shares at £35 prompt;
option for three weeks on a second 10J at the same
figure ; and a month’s option on a third 100 at £4O. Prices
went to £44 2s. 6d. after the option was given. If the
firm sold, as wise men should have done, its first 100
shares when rates were up, it probably netted £4 a share.
But there is no need for the firm to take up the second
100 under three weeks. Hence the firm could have the
market at its command for that term. If rates kept up
the option could be exercised and delivery made ; if they
dropped, the best plan would be to stand out. Profits
must come either way to people who could put 2 and 2
together, and it would be nice if one could only lift the
veil to see if the London firm looked at it in that light.
* * * *
Taranaki (M.L.) lately went off its head over
its petroleum bore, which local people have been carrying-
on since the mostly-Adelaide crowd gave it best. At
2100 ft. oil was “ struck.” Half Taranaki rushed off to see
the stream of mud, water, and oil; £5 shares ran up to
£lO with one mighty leap ; then slowly to £l5 ; butchers’,
bakers’, and grocers’ bills piled up—all cash went in
“oil shares.” But the knowing hands were selling out,
and shares dropped steadily : they are now quoted at £7
sellers, no buyers ; and it isn’t safe to mention oil in Tara-
naki.

* * * •
“ C.L.” : Most people who have nothing to do
with mining in the West, think it is a good game, with
good pay and only eight hours to work each day. But
they do not know anything about the dull side of the
business. In the mills we have to work seven days a
week, holidays included ; but the men looking for work
consider we are very lucky to be working at all. And so
we are. We realise that, when we see the crowd of strong
men seeking to get a shift in now and again to try and
keep the home together. Morning, afternoon, and night
there are always about two score waiting eager to get a
shift in if we happen to be a man short, through over-sleep-
ing or accident. You will see the same faces day after
day, sometimes for weeks, turning up regularly every eight
hours. Sometimes we put some poor beggar on for the
shift just out of charity. But we do not make a practice
of this. Then sometimes we happen to want a man, but
the foreman will not go out to engage him. He says he
has not the heart, but he will send out a man to get one
on the quiet. Just to show you how eager they are to
get work. Last Saturday,we put a man on day shift; he
knocked off at 4 p.m., and at 12 the same night, when it
was raining like blazes, he put in an appearance again,
and stood outside, wet to the skin, to see if he could get
another shift in. As he was the only one that turned up,
the boss put him on keeping the gutters clean to fill in
time. And he deserved it.

“ Delegates to the recent inter-State Label
Conference didn’t bring their wives with them to
any notable extent, and their social entertainment
was mostly of the manly type. Solicitor .Joe Woolr
—who solicits for Melbourne Trades Hall and the
Labor bodies generally, and who has the repute of
being able to see rather farther through a brick wall
than most —entertained the delegates at a banquet,
and the Federal and State Labor parties also fed
the delegates in public. There was one solitary
lady delegate, who helped to represent Tasmania,
and never shirked a debate or a dinner, and could
talk well even in a smolce-liazed room.
“A returned globe-trotter who witnessed Ada
Crossley’s marriage, describes Ada as towering in
most regal clothes above a modest groom, who
struck the eye as the most timid that had ever
tackled the Voice that Breathed. Ada, in a word,
looked like Queen Elizabeth led to the altar by a
modest subaltern on half-pay.
“ Toorak found Julius Knight better in health
and spirits than it expected. He hasn’t lost a
scrap of his Superbus in hospital. That raises
a dreadful doubt as to whether those beautiful
limbs are the sort that waste under typhoid.
Or are they the sort that the moth consumes?
“Society has suffered the loss of another very
bright, popular identity by death. Mrs. Sam
McCulloch had been ill for months, but her con-
stitution was such that husband and relatives
hoped it would triumph. But the dominant
soul died last Monday.
“Motley’s the wear. The Scandinavians of
Melbourne propose to hold another domino hop
in September, the funds to go to the Melbourne
Hospital fund. Last year, the Scandinavians
gave an object-lesson in the art of how to be
frivolous without being foolish.
“Nance O’Neil bares her roes in the Greek
show, and the Melb. audience endeavors fran-
tically to focus the sandalled members. But only
two or three begrudging times are the draperies so
drawn that a gleam of pink toes flash on the sight,
and this always happens when the glasses are
lying idle. The neatest and prettiest foot this
scribe lias seen bared on the Melb. boards belongs
to the other Yank, Tittell Brune. In ‘ Camille ’ a
neat limb, not even stockinged, was lowered from
the big bed, and for a thrillful while audiences
fastened their glowing regard on the pretty,
nicely-moulded foot.
“ Certain big private hospitals have trouble over
the guest-lists of convalescents. The trouble is in
the confusion of gentlemen relatives. A sick girl
has her brothers, her cousins, her uncles and her
father all on her list. Other side patients gossip
and point out that the number of relatives, their
ages and their sizes are out of all reason and pro-
portion, and that really some supervision should
be exercised. Henceforth, at one place the frivo-
lous convalescent is only to be allowed one brother,
one father, and in extreme cases (where proof of
bona tides is forthcoming), one male cousin.
“The Cumloden Old Bovs annually break out
into hospitality, always in the form of a jolly, but
quite particular hop. This tune, they propose to
give the girls a treat in sweet September, at the
St. Kilda Town Hall. The Toorak girl loves the
Cumloden Old Boy, and generally buys fresh
chiffons wherewith to enchant, if she can’t enchain,
him.
“ A warbler has been telling me that the joy of
singing at State Government House is a mixed
one. The fee paid doeSn t cover the expenses.
There’s the cleaning and- pressing of the swallow-
tail togs, new gloves, and a frfesh tie. Then there
is cab-hire for the night. ‘lt costs more anxiety
and time and trouble than the fee is worth,’
wound up the singer.
“The smart woman carries about an eyebrow
pencil as well as her carmine lip-stick. This
because of high winds that, blowing fiercely on
Beauty’s brow, carry off temporary eyebrows and
cast them into the Yarra. The awkwardness of
hurried application cause-home to a she-contingent
the other day, when, after taking tea in a big cafe,
one of the number surreptitiously applied a stick
to her lips, and it was the wrong stick, or rather
pencil. When the other girls looked her way,
they stared hard, till; one, seizing on the facts of
the position, told her cruelly her eyebrows had
slipped down and she.was in danger of swallowing
them. Whereat her other kind friends giggled
themselves into fits.
“A nurse who says she is strongly in favor of a
white Commonwealth, reluctantly yields the hos-
pital cake to John Chinaman. He is, she main-
tains, the cleanest, most respectful, least trouble-
some’patient taken into the charitable institutions.
She shuddered and squirmed when her first China-
man was submitted ’.to..her .care. At last she
girded up her nerves and faced the job. It was
the easiest and most satisfactory case she had
tackled. He wore silk pyjamas ! That evidently
took the girl. Silk pyjamas ! She said it softly.
So woman is converted to the saffron race. There
are numbers of the sex who would be won over to
a cannibal if he only took a small size in tooth-
picks.
“ A widow, who had been led to trimming her
lamps anew, as it were, in rosy hopes of a
Melbourne desirable coming to a proposal, has
suffered a violent shock. Ihe brute became
engaged to a sweet girl under her very nose. The
blow'was such that the little widow took to her
bed for a week to cry her temper out. It is a new
idea to chain the temper up to the bedpost, and
on the whole a good one; tantrums are best
indulged in private.”
“ Dress is becoming a dear luxury. The Collins-
street makers now demand 21 yards of material to
clothe you d la mode. They will not stake their
names on less. The voluminous skirt that rolls
over the hips and flows out into the spreading
tail takes most of that expensive length. A scrap
of lace and a whalebone or two are found sufficient
to cover the upper slope. There is sure to be a
revolt against the 21 yards. Why, woman used
to be covered stylishly for a matter of merely nine !
There are some venerables extant now who still
have a mere nine-yard length of satin smoothed
over their frames, and look well covered at that.
“ A motor-man was lately landed' into difficul-
ties at a house he was visiting in an Outer Circle
suburb. A child fell ill or fell down, and in great
perturbation the parents appealed to the visitor.
He recommended that the oldest daughter in the
house might have a spin with him to the doctor s
(some seven miles distant), and either get direc-
tions for treating : the child or bring the
man of physic back. It was agreed, and
they started before dinner, the dusk just gather-
ing. But, alas, little Willie suffered his pain for
many dreary hours. The parents fumed and
fretted, and the brothers and sisters were out on the
roadside looking for signs of a lost, stolen, or
bolted motor-car. At 2 o’clock in the morn the
machine, or part of it, was towed into the grounds
by a doctor’s buggy with bandaged patients
aboard.

W. N. Willis has been discharged from
custody by the Westralian Supreme Court on the
case sworn against him by John Haynes,
of Sydney, so he will not be extradited to
Sydney on that accusation. A fresh charge
has been, however, sworn against him, and
he was re-arrested as soon as released. Proba-
bly the second charge comes from the same
accuser, but that was not stated at time of
writing. The prospects of Mr. Willis’sea-trip da
not look too good; there seems a possibility of his
being detained in Australia until the Carruthers
Government is goaded into giving the Lands
Commission power to compel the attendance of
him and other witnesses. Meanwhile, some good
Cornstalk money is gladdening Westralian
lawyers.

“ M.R.T.” : Consider the Morman inva-
sion of Seddon’s country:
The Maori when under Selwyn and Archdeacon Henry
Williams, was the most orthodox of Anglicans. Then
came the Maori war, when the pious natives chewed up
their Testaments to make wads for the muzzle-loaders ;
and since then they have only been church-going:
Christians when there seemed to be money or blankets in
it. The average Christian creed is pre-eminently the
creed of respectability ; and the average Maori does not
understand what that means. So he is going over by
the thousand to the Mormon Church. There are now
some 200 Mormon “ elders ” in Maoriland, and they are
doing excellent biz. They forswear liquor, yet tolerate
harems. Joseph Smith founded his religion on the wig-
wam life of the American continent, and that is just the
sort of thing that suits these natives.
Then this’Murkan missionary knows his business. He
comes to the Maori in his pa and lives with him in his
whare. He is not as the priests and the missionaries are,
a thing apart. He advises on things social, on things
sanitary, on matters agricultural and pastoral. To all of
these, as a practical man, he brings practical knowledge.
And it must be owned up at once that the Mormon mis-
sionary has made the Maori a much more decent person.
He is no longer a w'’ne-bibber or a gambler, and he takes
some pride in his surroundings. The elder is always
setting before his imagination the delights and beauties
of the City of Saints in great and glorious Utah. And the
learner tries to live up to the picture.
The Mormon elders are, moreover, accompanied by their
women, who supplement the work of their fellow har-
vesters in the mission field by aid of a most practical
sort. They teach cooking, nursing, everything that has
to do with the life of a woman ; and they have benefited
the Maori home as no other creed has done.
So the British priest and parson ride by and wonder at
the decline of the faith and the falling off in subscrip-
tions ; and while they wonder, through back Taranaki,
through the Upper Rangitikei, in Southern Wairoa, the
spirit of J. Smith is spreading. It has done good in a
physical sense, but grave evils are developing. The Maori
was never quite averse to polygamy, and some of the hot
young spirits, and many of the older bloods, are adding
to the cares of their households. The elders scold and.
argue ; but, says the logician of the pa, if we are torn by
the thorns of our creed, may we not pluck the blooms !
Another rock ahead is the practice of faith-healing.
The elders have got their cue from the old Maori
tohunga, and already have cunningly taken over his
duties, and in some places have entirely supplanted him ;
and when, to the powers of persuasion, they will be able
to add those of magic, and the power of life and death, it
will be a very brave or a very well-educated Maori
kainga that will block their progress.

The Awful Sausage Trade.
One of the proposals of the Yic. Government for
Tvr 6 iu ew sess i? n a Food Adulteration Act. The
elboume Board of Health has, every now and
nen, pointed out that the unfortunate citizen who
asus tor bread is being given a stone, while his
demand for fish results in his being handed a ser-
pent, or something to that effect. Next to the
milk trade, which, by reason of its bearing on the
pirth rate, is the most important, comes the traffic
in meat. Australians are large meat-eaters, and
it is of very great consequence that the meat they
consume should be wholesome. As a matter of
tact it is, in a large number of cases, nothing
or the kind. Last year The. Bulletin drew
attention to the vast trade that was being done in
that most unwholesome victual, the carcase of
newly-born calves, or “slinks,” as they are
? a Phomously termed. Thousands of calves,
killed either at birth or within a few days of it,
are sent to the metropolis from the dairying dis-
tricts. These slinks are paid for at an average
rate of* from 10s. to 12s. per slink, and there is
always a good demand for them. The fact that
they are bought and paid for by Mel-
bourne butchers leads to the unavoidable in-
ference that they are afterwards sold to and
consumed by Melbourne citizens. As no one in
his right senses will buy plain slink, the butcher
passes it on the unsuspecting customer colored, as
it were, in the shape of sausage. The unpleasant-
ness is added to by the fact that old, diseased, and
worn-out bulls from the dairying districts also find
their way to Melbourne, and the sausage faotory.
Bulls which are hardly able to stand up in the
truck bring from £3 to £5 per head, to the delight
of the provident dairyman. Also, old, toothless
boars, with flesh as rank as that of a crow, go the
same way. Every old and toothless animal, no
matter how tough, fibrous, and unwholesome,
which the cocky farmer has no use for, is now put
on the train and sent to the Melbourne market,
where it is annexed by the meat-purveyors.
An inspection, for a continuous week, of the stock
trains arriving at Melbourne from the Western
District and Gippsland lines, would furnish
material for another royal commission. It is bad
enough for the Melbourne housewife when she
buys vinegar and gets diluted acetic acid, and
when the packet of imported candles turns the
scale at twelve instead of sixteen ounces; but
these things are of small account compared to the
slink sausage that she serves up to her husband
as a foundation for his hard day’s work. The
sausage is a thing in which June and December
meet—a mixture of extreme old age and immature
youth. It is composed partly of the slink and
partly of the slink’s grandfather, and this affecting
family re-union on the table is bad for Melbourne’s
health.
Japan and Australia.
Mb. It. L. Outhwaite writes from London :—
It may interest you to know that the views expressed
in your articles on the introduction of Chinese into the
Transvaal are those held by the great mass of the people
of England—a fact I have learnt from speaking through-
out the length and breadth of the land in exposure of the
infamy.
I desire to point out that this is a matter of deep con-
cern to the people of Australia, since it is quite within
the bounds of possibility that before long the same power
which brought about the introduction of Chinese into the
Transvaal may bring about a terrible crisis in Australia
over the Asiatic question.
The introduction of the Chinese was contrived by the
cosmopolitan financiers of the Continent, of whom the
so-called “mine-owners" of the Transvaal are only the
agents. This wonderful group of men, whose headquar-
ters are at Berlin, and whose power was created by
Bismarck to offset that of the Rothschilds, dominates,
with the latter, the finance of the Old World. South
Africa is entirely in their grip. The Boer Government re-
fused to introduce a forced black labor system at their
demand, and refused to allow the introduction of Chinese,
and the Boer Government was swept away. No sooner
was that accomplished than they again formulated their
demands and crushed the community into seeming
acquiescence.
To indicate their power in the realms of finance, the
dominating firm of the Rand, Werniier, Beit, Eckstein
and Co., lately called for capital for a £6,000,000 “ Trust ”
to control the Kaffir market, and over £100,000,000 was
subscribed, chiefly on the Continent. This firm controls
Cape Colony through De Beers, and Rhodesia through
the British South Africa Co. The power that cosmo-
politan finance exercises to-day over the destinies of Great
Britain it would be impossible to exaggerate.
Now, to indicate how Australia may before long feel
that power. Within a few months the alliance of Great
Britain with Japan must be renewed, and if peace should
at any time be declared, the question of the future rela-
tionship of the two countries will have to be determined.
The Times correspondent at Tokio has written stating
that the Japanese Government will require a complete
alliance, offensive and defensive. The Conservative press
is beginning to advocate such an alliance, and Mr. Cham-
berlain, in a speech delivered last week, declared that he
hoped to see such an alliance concluded. It is generally
considered in political circles in London that Japan would
make one of the terms of such an alliance the right of
entry for her citizens into the territory of her ally. Here,
then, would arise a grave crisis. To refuse this alliance
would leave Japan free to accept Russia as an ally.
Against this would be set, in the eyes of the Conservative
party and the Imperialist section of the Liberal party, the
“ sentimental" objection of the Labor party of Australia
as they would call it.
Then will oome the time for Australia to feel the
unseen power of cosmopolitan finance which has turned
the Rand into an Asiatic sink. The cosmopolitan
financiers, with their race hatred of Russia, with their
hatred of Australian democratic institutions as evinced
again and again in South Afrioa, with their vast invest-
ments in Japanese stocks —for they are financing Japan—
will throw their influence into the scale for the Yellow
Man. The Balfour Government is believed to be clinging
to office in order to be in power when the matter comes
up for determination.
There are fateful possibilities thus ahead, and unfortu-
nately Chamberlain, by his crusade on the basis of his
utterance “ To prevent separation you must tax food,”
has aroused antagonism against the colonies amongst the
masses of the people.
I think that you will admit that these considerations
indicate that the 'people of Australia have an intimate
concern with the Chinese question in the Transvaal,
seeing that there is a possibility that they will have to
contend in the near future against the power which
brought the Asiatic to the Rand, to preserve their own
land from the Yellow Peril.
The Value of Small Things.
When the Australian Tariff was being debated by
the House of Reps, the scorn of the Foreign-
trader for our small embryo industries was some-
thing tremendous. When he found one that was
represented by a couple of factories, employing, at
the most, say, 200 men and boys, he would point a
derisive foreleg at it and bray forth an indignant
query as to whether the House seriously contem-
i plated penalising “thousands and thousands of
our ‘ primary producers * for the sake of two fac-
tories and a couple of hundred employees—mostly
bovs ? ” Usually he wound up by snorting that if
this “ wretched industry ” had to be coddled, it
would be better to give the “ primary producers ”
the benefit of Freetrade in the articles produced,
and pension off the factory people, and thereby
keep a lot of money in the “pockets of the
people.” The fact that the money that was sent
abroad in return for foreign goods left the pockets
of the people in the process of being sent, was
carefully ignored. The Foreign-trader always de-
clines to consider the manufacturing industries
of the country as a whole, and insists on viewing
BLACKMAIL.
ENRAGED SMALL BOY (who has been refused a copper by johnny): “ Don’t you have
nothin' to do with him, lady. I see him take another girl in there yesterdee.”
them in detail. He speaks scornfully of the iron
industry as if it were the only one, instead of one
of many, and all the abuse and hate he has for the
whole of the manufacturing industries is heaped
on each particular fraction in turn. He poses as a
large-minded statesman, who has no time for
small, piffling matters such as the welfare of 100
men employed in one galvanised-iron factory. He
looks over them to where the vast interests of the
thousands and thousands of “primary producers”
rise like mountains against the horizon of his out-
look. When a country finds that it has a legislator
like that, it should pole-axe him, if there is no
other way of getting rid of him, for he is a fool, or
something worse—probably the agent of a foreign
exporter. The expert is the man who knows the
value of small things. J. D. Rookefellek, of
Standard Oil notoriety, who may be allowed to
know something of how to make money, was a man
whose master-stroke of genius consisted in getting
a saving of 50 cents per barrel on the freight over
250 miles. It doesn’t look very brilliant, but it led
to the making of the largest private fortune the
world has ever seen in the amazingly short space
of 20 years. The proper standpoint to consider
an Australian industry from is not, “ How many
does it employ?”, but “How many will it employ
if we secure it the first call in its own market?”
An industry that is confined to one factory and em-
ploys only 100 pair of hands seems a small matter
for a nation to consider. But the nation doesn’t
live by one industry alone, and if it has 1000 in-
dustries employing only 100 people each, it means
a manufacturing population of 100,000, who will
probably support 300,000 people directly, and far
more indirectly. And the “great primary indus-
tries” of driving a cow to the byre, cutting wool
off the back of a sheep, or carrying stinking hides
up the side of a ship, do not employ many people
if you come to consider the driving of the cow to
the byre as one industry, shearing the wool as
another, the packing of it into bales a third, the sort-
ing of the smellful hides a fourth, and so on. A
manufacturing artisan is worth to the community
about three times the value of a “ primary prod ucer. ”
Carried to its logical conclusion, the doctrine that
the Foreign-trader preached in the Commonwealth
Tariff debate was that because our manufacturing
industries were little we should strangle them.
Applied to the population question, the doctrine
of the Foreign-trader is that we should kill all our
babies because they are small, and because each
infant is only one infant, and one infant doesn’t
really count. It is a weird doctrine, and the man
who originated it probably intended it for a joke,
and never dreamed that there were fools on the
planet who would take it seriously.
The Yellow Peril.
“Nagasaki,” from Adelaide, asks these ques-
tions :—
1. Will not Japan, in Tiib Bulletin’s opinion, be
content with the fruits of her victories, and not attempt
—at any rate for many years—to cast longing eyes
towards Manilla and Australia as an outlet for her
increasing population ?
2. If Japan had lost in the war, and been thereby shut
out of Manchuria and Korea, would not the Japs have
had to make a big bid for either the Philippines or
Australia as soon as they possibly could ?
3. Under present circumstances, isn’t it just as well for
Australia that Japan will have an outlet into Korea and
Manchuria, and thus postpone her descent (if it must
come) to Australia for some years ?
To which, in reply :—
1. It is not possible to say with certainty what the Japs
will be content with. The only means of guessing at
Japan's future intentions are to study the overt actions
of the Japanese Government and the articles in Japanese
papers, and they all hint at an early attempt to “ deal
with ” Australia.
2. If Japan had lost in the present war there would not
have been enough of Japan’s power left to menace
Australia. Japan would have been kept so busy
keeping Japan for the Japanese that there would have
been no time to think of taking Australia for the Japanese.
3. The “outlet" for Japan in Manchuria and Korea is
to some extent only a phrase. Korea is a smaller place
than Victoria, and the population is estimated as high as
16,000,000. Manchuria is about the size of N.S.W., with
13,000,000 people. These “outlets” are not likely to
satisfy the Jap for long. In Korea, especially, there is
really very little room except by killing off the present
inhabitants, and even now there ii bitter anger in
Korea with Japan’s overlordship because the Japs are
striving to dispossess the natives.
Generally speaking the purport of “Nagasaki’s ”
queries is this: If a man you know to be your
enemy is fighting with a stranger, and promises,
after finishing with him, to attack you, isn’t it
best to sympathise with his attack on the stranger
in the hope that one victory will satisfy him?
This paper doesn’t think so.
Parkes and B. Smith.
There were men after the heart and aspiration of
Bruce Smith in Sydney over 60 years ago. This
from Henry Parkes’ letter to his sister—23rd
J anuary, 1842
At present we have also too much labor in Sydney,
great numbers of workmen, mechanics, and laborers,
“ old hands in the colony,” unemployed. The new-
comers fare worse, of course... Wages are much lower than
they were a few years ago. You will feel surprised that
in this state of things there should be such a cry raised in
the colony for increased immigration, not only from Great
Britain and the continent of Europe, but from India and
China. The fact is, the parties who are foremost in the
endeavors to inundate us with workers look only to the
depreciation of labor as the sure result. They have been
accustomed to have the convict’s toil for nothing, and
they cannot bring their minds to paying for that of the
free man. Hence they would fain have the poor coolie
from India bound to them for a number of years—a slave
in everything but in name.
Again, 26th January, 1843, Parkes wrote
Wages are very low and employment not very plentiful
in Sydney. In the country the only demand for labor is
as shepherds, and for this, purpose the flock-masters are
striving their utmost to obtain hill coolies from India on
the ground, they state, of not being able to give the price
of European labor, which is at present from £l5 to £2O
per annum and rations.
It seems questionable if there is a single industry
in Australia which hasn’t, at one time or
another, pointed out that its ruin could only be
averted by cheap nigger labor. Perhaps an ex-
ception could be made in favor of the legal in-
dustry. No one has yet heard the eerie, windy
wail of Bruce Smith demanding the encourage-
ment here of the cheap Hindu barrister—the in-
dividual who hangs on a rotting wall outside a
noisome alley in Delhi or Lucknow the sign “ Ram
Blather, Pleader.” Yet Ram Blather is won-
derfully cheap, and as a pleader he will drag his
client through the eye of a needle, even if he has
to hire ten witnesses to do it and also buy the native
Judge—who is always for sale.
*
“N.Q.” : As one with a good knowledge of the
Queensland tropics, I am getting horribly tired
of this eternal sugar (and nigger) cry. Those who
assert that sugar is the be-all and end-all of N.Q.
are suffering from a thick coating of ignorance.
From Bundaberg to Cooktown there is a coastal
belt, 800 miles long and from 50 to 500 miles
inland, that will produce any sub-tropical fruit,
cereal or tree known to agricultural science.
Professor Skelton stated in writing that
the soil in the Burdekin Delta would
grow anything on God’s earth—-except ice-
bergs and sand. When the Federal exploring
party visited the State nursery at Kamerunga,
near Cairns, they found growing in profusion
scores of fruits, shrubs, and economic plants that
the “sugar or nothing” man : only dreams of.
Between Port Douglas and Maryborough there are
thousands of miles of lush grass country able to
produce the finest dairy cattle on the eastern
coast, and the beef and mutton from north and
west of Hugheuden hold their own in the markets
of the Cold Country. Even the ignorant sugar-
grower has been shown by the Government ex-
pert how to improve his land, and get 82 tons
per acre as against 54 tons by his old, crude
methods. Some of the small growers are emerg-
ing from the ruck, and increasing their bank
accounts; the others sit and moan, “ Give us our
Kanaka.” If the Australian Government ever
starts a Department of Agriculture, its first act
should be to send a man up here to point out to
the slothful and Kanaka-loving white the iniquity
and depth of his ignorance, and the folly of crying
over-ripe fish.
“B.”: A feature of the Reid-Symon attack on
the High Court is that as long as sittings of the
Court are held in Sydney, and the judges
reside there, “the concession is to be made of
expenses during the Melbourne sittings.” Ihe
Justices are either entitled to their travelling
expenses during the Melbourne sittings, or they
are not. If they are, they don’t want them as a
concession. If they are not, the Government has
no right to grant them either as a concession or as
anything else. It is no more justified in spending
public money on the Justices than in spending it
on apple-tarts for the Justices’ children. The
Order-in-Council, after conferring on the Justices
certain monetary privileges to which, according
to the Attorney-General, they are not entitled,
proceeds to announce that this “concession ” is to
be “subject to revision when circumstances arise.”
The “circumstances” which may induce the
Government to withdraw the concession are not
specified. They may, of course, include the cir-
cumstance that the Court has given a decision
which happens to displease the Government. It
is hard to believe that the men who devised such
a scheme as this have the faintest notion of what
is meant by the independence of the judiciary.
Thus the London correspondent of the Age :
In the event of an attack being made on India, the
troops there would have to be reinforced by the despatch
during the first year of 130,000 to 160,000 men from
Great Britain. Further drafts would have to be sent
at intervals if the struggle were much prolonged. The
regular army at home would be reduced to meagre pro-
portions in comparison with any of the highly trained
European armies which lie near at hand. This would be
a direct temptation to a continental enemy, possibly
Germany. The risks of invasion and of the interruption
of sea-borne food supplies would be so obvious to the
English people that it is expected they would insist upon
having all the available ships of the navy confined to home
waters jor the purposes of coast defence, leaving several
other parts of the Empire, including Australia, without
any effective protection from attack.
The Bulletin has pointed out, over and over
again, how Freetrade and landlordism have so
far destroyed British agriculture that Britain
is now almost wholly dependent on foreign
lands for its food supplies, and if a really
serious naval war comes about, it will need,
not only a sufficient fleet to defend its own
two islands, but one sufficient to protect its
food ships coming from Canada, the United
States, and perhaps Argentina—else it will starve.
And even if Britain maintains a navy equal to
that of any two possible enemies, it doesn’t follow
that it will have a ship to spare for Australia’s
defence, despite the £200,000 a year we pay for
the privilege of looking at a nice, clean squadron
in time of peace. Australia must have its own
navy. The Bulletin told its people that, in days
when British authorities described any suggestion
of having our own navy as something like “dis-
loyalty.” Now British authorities begin to give
us notice that it is time for U 3 to get our own
navy, because Britain itself may find more than
enough to do in its local waters ; and The Bulle-
tin view is no longer “disloyal.” It is only
prophetic.

From the annual report of the director of the
Imperial Railway Bureau, Tokio, Japan :
On 31st March, 1904, the staff and employees of Govern-
ment and private railways comprised 487 officers, 65,478
general employees, and one foreign employee.
The Jap gladly employs foreigners from whom he
thinks he can learn new ideas, but when he has
acquired the ideas, the foreigner is sacked, merely
for the unpardonable crime of being a foreigner.
Therefore, before Australia gives the glad hand of
welcome to Mr. Yoshiwarra it might perhaps be
well that he should learn to be a little less reticent
in the matter of extending his own paw of brother-
hood. Japan, by the way, has another notable
peculiarity in this connection. There is hardly an
instance on record of a foreigner winning a case
against a Jap in a Jap court. Whether the white
man is plaintiff or defendant it is all one. The
courtesy which the brownish magistrate shows
towards him is perfect, and he hands him butter
on a lordly dish, but no matter how overwhelming
may be the evidence in favor of the white litigant
the brown litigant wins. The European plaintiff
gets the oil and grease of affability, while the
Asiatic defendant is treated almost rudely by
comparison, and gets a verdict for the full amount,
with costs, handed down to him in a way that
nearly hurts his feelings.
4.
“P.” : In the past five years Victoria swept into its
Treasury, and used as ordinary revenue, £1,304,984
derived from land-sales ; and yet, compared with
N.S.W., Victoria was amazingly honest in this
respect. Irvine and Bent, in one year alone
(1903-4), grabbed £286,408; and then they talked
about a surplus, and it is a dreadful fact that
theirs was actually the most honest State surplus
in the Commonwealth that year. If a business
man borrowed money on his stock, and then sold
his stock, and paid nothing off the debt, but
went on issuing faked balance-sheets showing that
his business was flourishing, he would be spoken
to severely when he faced the inevitable insolvency
court. Very probably a warder would subse-
quently draw the attention of No. 964 to the fact
that his cell hadn’t been tidied up. But the issuer
of the faked public balance-sheet fears nothing like
that. At the worst he is made a K.C.M.G. The
political crime of collaring capital and treating it
as revenue might be partly smudged out if a
decent land-tax were put on—if a land-tax brought
in to the State as much as the Treasurer stole from
the State. But while Victoria’s land, of the value
of £1,304,984, was collared and used as revenue,
the land-tax in the same five years only totalled
£503,344.
♦
Australian Torydom, which never pauses in its
perennial shriek against the Immigration Restric-
tion Act, should once more consider the Bill which
the Home Secretary of Britain (the place we are
eternally told to look to for guidance) has intro-
duced into the House of Commons. It provides
(shame !) that aliens—white aliens too —shall land
only at such ports as shall be prescribed, and then
only by the leave of the immigration officer. This
official is empowered to debar the entrance of all
undesirable immigrants, and an immigrant is un-
desirable if he cannot show that he is in a position
to “decently” support himself and his depen-
dants ;if he is a lunatic; if, through infirmity
(such as being a “blind tourist”), he is likely to
become dependent upon the rates or a detri-
ment to the public; if he has been sen-
tenced for a non-political crime; and if an
expulsion order has been made against him.
The Bill also goes a step further than anything
yet proposed in Australia and gives power to the
Secretary of State to expel aliens once admitted,
on their conviotion for crime or if, “ in the opinion
of a court of summary jurisdiction, their expulsion
is advisable.” It would be interesting to hear
George Pile and Bilnox and the rest of the
Australian Fat party “ viewing ” everything with
“alarm” if Watson suggested such a measure.
A Bill which authorises the exclusion or expulsion
of the alien for a whole host of specified reasons,
and then adds a general power of expulsion in
addition, goes miles beyond anything yet suggested
here.

The Medical Journal,
OCTOBER, 1904, SAYS:
“ The uric acid problem affords an ever-increasing interest, not
only on account of the many attempts at solution and various
theories set forth, but because of the 'wide field of investigation
which is opened up with the fascinating prospect of bringing to light
metabolic facts which have ever been shrouded in obscurity. We
do know, however, whence uric acid is derived, but the metabolism
produced thereby has yet to be determined. Regardless of diet, rich
and poor suffer alike from the accumulation of uric acid, which some-
times occurs without any apparent failure in health ; but more often
are the kidneys and bladder affected, and the digestive organs im-
paired ; hence excessive uric acid is frequently responsible for heart-
burn, flatulence, and liver trouble. Lithia has been tried and found
useless, for when taken by the mouth it chemically combines with
the phosphates in its progress through the stomach and there forms
insoluble compounds. The best remedy we know possessing a solvent
action and eliminative properties is Kutnow’s Powder, which is
largely used by the Medical Profession and has always given the
highest satisfaction wherever it has been tried.”

How is this uric acid headache distinguished ?
When it is due to uric acid poisoning, it is
generally one-sided or partial, as over the
brow, and more or less intermittent pain
on pressure of the scalp, and darting or
shooting pains from temple to temple.
Is uric acid dangerous ?
Not immediately so; it is very insidious, but
ultimately, if not checked, its ravages are
disastrous.
Is nervous Headache a first sign of uric acid
poisoning ?
Yes ! when the uric acid is absorbed by the
blood it is rapidly circulated and deposited
in the nerve centres, notably the spine
and brain.

To its baneful influence may be ascribed in-

“ Jimmy Pannikin” writes :
Let me sketch for you a few of the changes wrought in.
the most outback Public School of New South Wales, by
intelligent, sympathetic administration of the foolishly
derided New Syllabus. The said school is a prison-like
stone structure in Tibooburra, a tiny, tinny township,
almost on the Queensland border. Its old name, by the
way, was “The Granite Rocks." The town lies on an
auriferous flat surrounded by millions of granite boulders,
heaped together in curious fashion. Geological folk call
the place “ Gibberburra,” which is neat. But to get back
to the Syllabus-sweetened school. The school interior is
brightened by numerous colored pictures, black and
white illustrations, engravings, photographs, collections
of local grasses (gathered after the rare rainfall), timbers,
rocks, etc. The library (contributed by parents and red*
dents) contains a large number of-interesting, healthy
books (not of the goody-goody, preachy, wishy-washy,
weary kind), magazines, illustrated periodicals, weekly
and daily newspapers; and is a live institution, run-on
lines that encourage the reading, remembering, and
thinking habit. The girls have a properly laid-oiit
croquet ground, and a good supply of material. They
play daily. The boys play criqket and football with up-
to-date kits. Every pupil’has a set of dumb-bells (many
being home-made, but effective). The physical training
is fine. The children exercise vigorously. They stand
well, and move easily; obey promptly and cheerfully;
and practise deep nasal breathing. There are no truants,
no late-comers. Children have found that, even in dry,
dusty Tibooburra, there is much of interest in their daily
surroundings. Parents cannot keep their children at
home from school. School is the one bright spot in the
lives of these right-out-back children. Tibooburra parents
bless the Syllabus—they do not sneer at it. Ask them if
’tis so.

The schooner Swan, which carried George
Coppin and his theatrical co. (including Mr. and
Mrs. Charles Young and Rogers) from Hobart to
Melbourne in ’46, has had a more extended, if not
more adventurous, career than the player whose
fortunes she bore 59 years back. The Swan came
into existence over 90 years ago (how much over it
is hard to say) as a t rench cutter. Finding its
way to Tasmania about ’37, it was rigged as a
schooner, and afterwards traded between Victorian
ports and the Flyspeck. In the ’Bo’s or ’9o’s,
Captain Loch, of Westernport (Vic.), bought it in
Hobart. It was then lengthened with blue gum
and converted into a ketch. Since that time it
has been trading between Melbourne and Western-
port. Its timber for’ard, being oak, is now just as
sound as on the day it was launched. At present
it is in the hands of Captain F. Jansen, who runs
it as a cargo boat in the Melbourne to Westernport
trade. The Swan may be twenty years older than
George Coppin, who is in his second youth at 86.
It is spending the evening of its days in easy work,
which is chiefly done on the placid waters of
Westernport Bay. Possibly it may last longer
than the man whom it first assisted to rise on the
Australian theatrical horizon. But the Swan has
liad new timbers put in. It is not likely that the
father of the Australian stage will be similarly re-
'fitted. All the same, George must have some oak
in him, both for’ard' and aft.

From W. H. M'Glew, Esq., J.P., Springside, Smith’s Mill, W.A., 11th November, 1908:
“Seventeen years ago I was suffering with Bright’s Disease of the kidneys. I was treated by two
doctors, and by them was given up as incurable. They had been attending me for some time, and
finally decided that it was impossible for me to recover, and told me that I must prepare for the worst.
The announcement came as a great shock to me, and more so to my wife and family, who were greatly
distressed. At that time my head and body were in an alarming state, being swollen right up to the
eyes. I had a vague feeling of unrest and tenderness in the kidneys $ I suffered from extreme wakef illness,
and gradual failure of strength also, as I was not able to keep anything on my stomach.
The fact that I am alive to-day is, however, proof that doctors are not always
correct when they tell you you are going to die. _I did not give _up hope myself,
and was determined to live as long as possible. Providentially, about this time, one of Warner s
Safe Cure pamphlets was placed in my hands, and in it I read a letter from someone who had
suffered from the same complaint, and had been cured by taking Warner’s Safe Cure. lat once
decided that, as the doctors said they could do no more for me, I would give Warner’s Safe Cure a
trial and see if that would do me any good. I sent immediately for a supply of Warners
Safe Cure and a vial of Warner’s Safe Pills, and commenced taking these medicines
without delay, keeping strictly to the diet rules given, which I believe to be the most
important, in order that the medicine may do its work without hindrance. I did not at
first inform the doctors that I was taking Warner’s Safe Cure, for fear that they might
object to my doing so. After taking a few bottles I began to feel better, and this gave me faith to
continue. The swelling in my head and body went down gradually, and other distressing ailments
were greatly relieved. I continued to improve in health, gaining strength each day. The doctors were
delighted at my rapid and almost miraculous recovery, but when I informed them what medicine
had produced the result, they would scarcely believe me. In a few more weeks I had quite recovered
nay normal strength, and have since had no return of the complaint, and am in as good health to-day
as ever I was in my life. To prove that this is so, agents for Insurance Companies have offered to
insure my life for any amount.”

Tobacco instructor Neville, of Queensland,
told the Tariff Commission some valuable facts at
Brisbane as to the tobacco industry :
The effect of the Commonwealth duties had been to
increase the consumption of the imported tobacco, and
to diminish correspondingly the local article. Almost
the whole of the tobacco admitted to the Commonwealth
under the tariff as unmanufactured was in reality an
article which had gone through the first stages of manu-
facture . . Under the Queensland tariff, the consump-
tion of locally-grown tobacco had increased m six years
hv 338 per cent., and at the end of the period referred to
4(5 ner cent, of the tobacco consumed in the State was
!... own 0 wn in Queensland. Analyses showed that the sods in
certain districts of Queensland were identical with those
of the United States. Queensland was capable of pro-
ducing- vo 000,0001 b. of leaf annually. If properly handled
the industry would at least yield one-half the leaf con-
sumed by Australia.
Wilson, who pleads the employers' side of
the case in the W.A. Arbitration Court, had liis
nerves jarred somewhat during a recent mining
wages cases. “ What,” he asked one of the work-
men’s witnesses, “ were you before you became a

got'this job,” was the reply. The cross-examina-
tion of that witness stopped with a jolt there and
then.
An Australian squatter, evidently a mem-
ber of the great Stinking Fish party, sold recently
to an English buyer a high-priced lot of wool; the
first bale of which, upon being opened up at Brad-
ford, England, was found to contain a stone
weighing many pounds. The manufacturer
promptly rejected the consignment, which thus
falls upon the hands of the original buyer, who in
consequence probably says things about the Labor
party.
Apropos Federal capitals. Washington,
as the capital of the United States, was a matter
of compromise. Ottawa, as the capital of Canada,
was chosen nominally by Queen Victoria. The
original capital of Maoriland was Russell (Bay of
Islands); then it was shifted to Auckland, and,
finally, in 18G5, a committee consisting of three
(Joseph Docker, M.L.C., of N.S.W., Speaker
Francis Murphy, of Victoria, and Mr. R. C.
Gunn, of Tasmania) selected Wellington as 1 the
capital for all time.
The Fullerton memorial church, just com-
pleted in Surry Hills, Sydney, was built out of
part of the moneys paid by Government for the
site of the old Fullerton church at the Hay-
market. The Government made a gift of the
original site to Fullerton for religious purposes far
away back in 1838, and after sixty years bought
the land back, giving, in effect, another site and
another church and £6OOO cash besides to the
Presbyterian Assembly. That’s how the church
gets ahead of the State every time.
A complaint with some truth behind it: —
The Civil Service members of the Victorian Assembly
are absurdly unsatisfactory persons. Lately “ Bob ”
Solly informed a crowd of rural democrats that he had
travelled all over the world, but had “ never seen a worse
state of pauperism than existed in Melbourne.” By such
wild exaggerations the demand for practical Socialism is
brought into ridicule. Solly also remarked that, although
he had been doing well enough as a bootmaker in a
factory, he was enjoying a much better job as a member
of Parliament—just the sort of candid remark which en-
encourages Fatman to snort at all Labor members as
“ fellows looking for a billet.” There’s too much “ Bob ”
about Mr. Solly ; too much Tom, Dick and Harry about
all representatives of the Labor vote. They should at
least stand on their legislative dignity like John Burns,
who has contrived to avoid being popularly known as
“ Jack.” A man may stand on his own dignity without
appearing to ape the vainglorious personification of im-
portance who puts a pedestal on his dignity, and stands
upon that with his stummick proudly protruded into
space.
A correspondent supplies some notes about
a policeman with a gruesome career :
Melbourne coroner's orderly Davidson has relinquished
the post after nearly 20 years’ service, to take up duty as
sub-inspector of police. He succeeded senior-constable
Hoey in the orderlyship in 1886, when the morgue was a
ramshackle barn standing on the present site of the fish
markets, near the Australian wharf, and bodies had to be
covered with wire cages to protect them from rats. In
1887 the advent of five bodies from the wrecked Cheviot,
and, a few months later, of seven from the Windsor
railway accident, suggested to the authorities that the
old building could easily be overcrowded, and they
hurried along the present one, completed in 1888. Mr.
Davidson was officially connected with six coroners, viz.,
Dr. Youl, D. M. Daly, Dr. Neild, Dickson (P.M.), Morrison
(now Under-Sec.), and present officer Chandler. He esti-
mates thftt about 16,000 bodies have come under his
official eye, upon 6808 of which inquests were held. The
idea of preserving the head of the victim of the notorious
boot-box tragedy in 1898 was his, and the bodies of
Deeming and many other executed criminals are entered
up on his list. He says that he knew of only one case of
wrong identification—that in which a body was identified
as ex-constable Svenson’s by the latter’s sisters and several
intimate friends. Svenson turned up alive later on.
Davidson has a notion that husbands sometimes fail to
identify the bodies of their wives to avoid funeral ex-
penses. A Dr. Youl yarn Woman in black accosting
the genial coroner—“ Ye’re inquestin’ Denny O’Brien,
aren’t ye?” “Yes, madam.” “Then, I’m his sorrowin’
widder. He swallowed a half-a-sovrin onst. If ye find
it ye can keep two-and-sixpence for yerself.”
“ 0.P.” discourses upon the expansion of
the empire:—
When John Bull sees the country he wants, he sends a
“ scientific expedition,” with bottles and things to put
grubs in. But he doesn’t want the grubs ;he only wants
the country. Sometimes he takes the bottles with beer
in, too, and the natives like the beer. The natives don’t
know what it is that has come, and they get frightened
and climb up the trees. Then the expedition sings out
friendly, and the natives come down out of the trees.
They don’t know what the expedition has sung out, but
they know it is friendly. They come round in a
ring and look at the expedition. And the expe-
dition is verv polite. It takes off its hat, and
drops the bottles with the grubs in, and shakes hands
with the king. Then the natives know it is all right, and
thev come close, and look in the bottles, and smell the
clothes of the expedition, and jabber at it. And the ex-
pedition smiles all the time. Then the expedition gives
the king some tin tags off the tobacco and some stoppers
from the lemonade bottles, and he bucks in the air like
the lieutenant in the Salvation Army. The expedition
goes out after the grubs and runs down the locusts so as
the natives won’t be suspicious. And the natives follow
the expedition, and laugh when it chases the locusts and
bottles them. But the expedition is winking at itself all
the time, and looking for the gold, and seeing what kind
of land it is to grow things. When the expedition has
had a good look over the country it comes back to the
coast making a frightful noise, and all the natives come
out to see what it is. It is the locusts in the bottles. And
when the scientific men see the captain of the ship they
want to go back home in he says he can’t stand that noise,
and he won’t have them. But then they wink at him too,
and he says “ Alright.” Then they throw the locusts over-
board, and go home, and tell John Bull all about the coun-
try. “Is there any diamonds or gold there?” says John.
“Yes,” says the expedition, “big as potatoes.” “Cripes!”
says John. So he gets a lot of missionaries and tells them
the biackfellows with the gold and diamonds are in a
terrible state of “spiritual darkness,” and sends the
missionaries to shift some of the darkness. They shake
hands with all the biackfellows, and call them brother, and
try to shovel away the spiritual gloom, and the black-
feilow is astonished. Then the missionary wants the
blackfellow to wear trousers, and gives him some, but
they fall down sudden when he is running, and
the poor blackfellow falls down too. He is wild
with the missionary, and boils him for supper.
When John Bull hears about the poor missionary he
sends a “ punitive expedition,” which is different to the
“ scientific ” one, and he proclaims a “suzerainty” over
the country, which means he shook it, but it doesn’t look
that way. Then the poor natives are sorry. They
thought it was funny about the locusts at the start, but
they can make it out quite easy now. Some more of
John Bull’s men come, and give the tobacco tags and the
glass marbles for elephants’ teeth and gold and diamonds.
And some bring machinery, and start to dig and build
houses. Then the blackfellow, who has been learning all
the time, thinks it is best to hunt away John Bull’s
men or to kill them before they shake everything, but
just about then John says to the biackfellows that the
other biackfellows, which live further up the country,
have been talking about them. “ You make them prove
it,” says he. So the two lots of biackfellows start to
fight, and while they are busy doing that John takes the
best land, and gets some more guns in quick. And when
the fight is over there are not many biackfellows left to
chase anybody away, and they haven’t made each other
prove anything. John is all right then, and he talks
kind to the biackfellows, and says the white man is their
brother. But if the black man says that to the white one,
the white man hits him so sudden that you would never
know the difference.
According to Outtrim, M.L.A. (Vic.), who
was a member of the Shiels Cabinet, the Premier
of that Administration was offered the position of
Chief Justice before it was given to Madden.
Had Shiels accepted, he would have been iff the
unique position of a Chief Justice who had never
held a brief in any of the courts of the State. _ By
the way, there is something humorous in the idea
of Premier Shiels offering himself the Chief Jus-
ticeship and then nobly refusing it, and saying he
would stick to his present job and save the coun-
try.
An electoral registrar in Victoria refused
an old-age pensioner his State elector’s right
lately, saying he was barred by the eleemosynary
relief clause of the Victorian Act. Instructions
from the Chief Electoral Inspector promptly put
his jack-in-office straight, but he is only one of
many who regard the pension as a “charity”
instead of being just as much a right as any other
State pension—say that of an old, superannuated
Supreme Court Judge.
