When the Moon was in Eclipse.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT was night upon the grey downs,
And the moon was near eclipse
As they lay beside the sheep ;
One, a-babbling in his dreaming
Of white arms and clinging lips,
Stirred and muttered in his sleep.
And the other crouched and listened
To the black swans in their flight,
And the tinkling of the team—
As a shadow leaned across him
That was blacker than the night,
Did it break the sleeper’s dream?
“ Waken, Una ! Raise the window !
. Let me climb into your nest! ”
Thus he muttered as he slept,
“Nestle closer, dear one, closer !
With your breast warm on my breast.”
And the shadow nearer crept.
“ What of bonds and creeds and symbols ?
And the name words and the rite—
They are not for us, mine own !
See above the looming grey downs,
Sits our priest, the cross of white;
And athwart your window thrown
“The moon-shine and shadow mingle—
Even as your soul and mine.”
So he murmured in his sleep.
“ Like a fair and folded flower,
Drooping as your warm limbs twine,
And your arms about me creep;
“Love can know no law but loving—
Let them curse with Book and Bell,
As I swoon upon your lips!
In the glory of my heaven, 1
While their lying mummers tell ”
And the moon was in eclipse.
One was strong with strength of hatred,
One with dazed and maddened brain :
The low moon hung, shadow-spanned,
And the night was dark as passion,
And the wind was cold as pain,
As it moaned above the sand.
Bob, the collie, leaped and listened :
If the silly things would rove,
Then goodbye to peaceful sleep,
And he panted through the darkness,
As he trotted round the drove —
There was peace among the sheep.
As the moon and shadow parted,
Dawn, the stealthy tracker, came,
Gained upon him as he crept;
Till he leaned above her bedside,
Calling harshly on her name—
Strange, how heavily she slept!
Freshly through the little window
The keen, early wind blew in,
And a blurred and blotted sheet,
The last witness of her falsehood,
And the seal upon her sin,
Fell and fluttered at his feet:
“ Can you hear me, my beloved ?
Where the sloping headland dips
With its tussocked greys and browns,
1 can hear the wild swans crying,
And the moon is near eclipse
High above the looming downs.
“ And my heart is strange to-night, dear,
How it flutters and is still!
But it may be better soon ;
And a subtle, deadly menace
Seems to hold me like a chill —
’Tis the failing of the moon ! ”
Never more for clasp of passion,
Never more for man’s desire !
Slow he lifted up her hand,
And a ray, blue as her eye flash,
Glittered from a pale sapphire
On her finger, jewel-spanned.
It was day upon the grey downs,
And the early sunshine fell,
As he muttered with white lips :
“You have met your lover, Una;
For you followed him to hell
When the moon was in eclipse ! ”
n.s.w.
LOLA RIDGE.
Bill’s Wife.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
air for miles around was blue and
heavy, and the rays of the hot, January
sun smote through the smoke like sword-
blades. For many days there had been fires in
the forest. In the close, oppressive evenings, and
through the torrid nights, could be heard the
crackle of burning timber and the crash of giant
trees that fell, bitten through by teeth of flame.
With every crash new fires started, for the burn-
ing trees in their fall tore through masses of
scorched undergrowth and saplings tindered ready
for lighting by the heat, which seemed to turn the
whole forest into one great oven. Tales of settlers
who had been burnt out were brought to the town-
ship at almost every hour of the day.
* * * *
Upon a lonely clearing high up on the hillside
a pretty little woman was battling all alone with
the enemy. The bush fire raged on all sides of
the selection, but there had not been a soul to help
her except a nine-year-old daughter, who had
become temporarily blinded with the smoke.
For thirty-six hours she had not slept nor taken
her clothes off. Axe in hand, she had gone round
the outside of the small, fenced paddock, in the
midst of which stood the little shingle cottage,
and had chopped down every sapling that looked
likely to blaze. The big trees she could not
manage ; and there were four or five monster red-
gums that, if they got alight, and fell towards the
house, must inevitably burn her out. She watched
them, and tried to suppress every spark that fell.
There were two baby boys inside the cottage,
with a little girl of five in charge of them. It was
the house tnat she was fighting for—fighting
with a courage that never faltered, with a nerve
that never llinched. Her eyes were red and sore
with watching and smoke and heat, but her will
was such that she would not for an instant own to
herself that she was tired.
A man rode along the rough corduroy road, up
the hill, on horseback. He carried an axe over
his shoulder, wore a policeman’s old leather
helmet on his head, and had some wet bagging
tied round his legs, arms, and shoulders. There
was also wet bagging tied round the legs of his
nag. A quaint, shaggy, Don Quixote-ish figure he
looked. He had been sent along by the shire
engineer to clear the track of fallen trees, so that
the mail coach might pass in safety. The little
woman on the selection put down her axe, pushed
into place the wisps of brown hair that had
become disarranged, and smoothed her clothes.
She was a very neat woman at all times. She
gave the horseman a smile of recognition, and he
called out:
“Mornin’, Mrs. Gascoigne.”
‘‘Mornin’, Mr. Atkins.”
“ Yer ’ard at it, I see.”
“Yes, have to be these times.” She spoke
quite cheerfully,
“ Where’s Bill ? ”
The question was a natural one, but it caused
her some embarrassment. “Well,” she said, hesi-
tatingly, “Bill ain’t here. I wonder you didn’t
see him on the road.”
“What! you’aven’t been doin’ all this fireman
work alone, ’ave yer ? ”
“Oh, yes, Mr. Atkins. I managed it all right,
except for a little help Tilly gave me; but the
poor child got almost blinded by the smoke.” She.
described how she had beaten out the falling
sparks and flakes of burning bark with woollen
mats soaked in water, had chopped down under-
growth, and kept constant watch for two nights
and a day.
“Well, you are a good ’un, Mrs. Gascoigne.
I’d be glad to stay and ’elp yer a bit, but I
dussn’t. I’m afraid there’s bin some big burnin’
further on, and I must make the track clear for
the coach.”
“Oh, don’t you bother, thank you, Mr. Atkins.
I’ll pull through.”
“ But what about Bill ? Doesn’t he know ? ”
“Oh —Bill —well, Bill knows I shall be all
right. He promised Jack Carmidy to give the
missis a hand if anything went wrong, when Jack
went away to the west. I suppose the fire has
been pretty bad down that way.”
“Not so bad yet, but it will be if this wind
keeps up. Well, good mornin’, and good luck.”
Atkins rode along, muttering curses through his
thick beard. “ Bill Gascoigne’s a darned skunk,”
he said to himself, “leaving that little woman to
battle alone, while he’s helping that yellow-haired
Carmidy woman. Helping her ! My oath ! I
know.”
Mrs. Gascoigne sat down on a log by the fence,
and gave her byes a rub. She was not crying ;
she could not have cried. But she thought. Bill
had told her that he had promised Carmidy to
give a hand if anything went wrong down at his
place in his absence. She had made no comment.
Perhaps Carmidy had asked him ; perhaps he
hadn’t. Anyway, Bill had been there often, and
had stayed late. She knew Bill, and her instinct
summed up the situation.
A couple of hours later the coach came along.
Mrs. Gascoigne always put on a clean white apron
when she went out to look at the coach. It was a
point of pride with her to be neat. Neatness was
as much a part of her nature as industry. Even
on this morning she snatched a few moments from
her anxious labor to make herself “clean and
tidy.” The driver called out “How’s things?”
Mrs. Gascoigne replied cheerily that they were
all right.
“You’ve ’ad it ’ot,” he said, looking at the
blackened trees round about.
“ Yes,” she replied, “ but we’ve kept it off the
house. There’s not even a fence burnt.”
“ Ah, you must ’ave worked ’ard—you and Bill.”
He looked at her closely when she spoke, but
she only answered “ Yes.”
He was not satisfied, and queried: “Bill in-
side?”
“ Not just now.”
He handed to her a parcel he had brought, and
said: “Suppose you know that Carmidy’s come
back from the west? ”
She dug her finger-nails deep into her palms, but
•said steadily: “ No, has he ? ”
“Yes, he’s in Geelong. He’ll be coming up to-
morrow, I believe. Ah, well, I’m glad you’ve got
off so lightly. Good day.” The coach rattled
along. “Did you ’ear what she said about ’er
’usband?” said the driver to the box-seat pas-
senger ; “’e ain’t inside ‘ just now.’ No; not ’e ;
Bill’s got another game on.”
“Do you mean to say,” remarked the passenger,
who was a stout, domesticated man, and a stern
critic of the improprieties ot others, “that the
woman’s husband has left her alone in the house
with a fire raging all around her ? ”
“That’s what’e’s done.” He related immoral
circumstances concerning Bill, and the box-seat
passenger said “ Disgraceful! disgraceful! ” He
puffed out his cheeks and added that he had
never heard of such a thing.
Once more Mrs. Gascoigne sat down and
thought. She had an over-wrought, trembling
feeling that something was going to happen.
* * * *
Carmidy’s selection was four miles down the hill
from Gascoigne’s, and about half-a-mile from the
coach-road. Hitherto this part of the forest had
escaped the fire. But as night came on the wind
shifted a few points. The flames leapt from tree
to tree. Sometimes there was a roar from the
very heart of a giant gum trunk, and presently
there would be a report like the explosion of a
gun, and a tongue of flame would shoot from the
top, and a battery of sparks would assault the
darkness. The constant crackling sounded as
though the fire had teeth, and were gorging itself on
timber.
A woman seized the shoulders of a man who lay
on the bed. “Bill,” she half-screamed in his ear,
“ did you hear that ? ”
“ All right, Kate, I’ll get up,” he muttered, half-
asleep.
“It isn’t Kate, you fool,” she cried m terror.
“ Get up ; I’m sure the fire is on us.”
He woke up with a start, and as he opened his
eyes he saw through the window-blind the scarlet
glow of flame and the red radiance of a thousand
sparks, which the masterful wind blew like a
battalion of fiends, to kindle where they fell.
“It’s here,” the woman gasped. “What shall
Ido ? Oh, God, save me. I thought we were
safe. Oh, what shall Ido ? ”
The man, without a word, sprang, from the bed.
In less than a minute he was outside the door, axe
in hand. The woman, half paralysed with fright,
could scarcely move, but sat staring at the red-
clotted darkness with her grey eyes agape. A
mass of yellow hair lay around her white fleshy
shoulders. .
The sparks came principally from a burning
tree, about 50 yards from the pack of the house.
The woman on the bed heard the sharp ring of a
swiftly swung axe for a few moments. Then
there was nothing to be heard but a roaring,
crackling, crashing—an infernal noise with *n
infernal heat. She stood up and listened, her hand
to her ear. Her face was grey, and pulled by
fear to a gaping length. Her eyes yawned on a
vacancy reddened in the distance. Did she hear
aery? No ; only the breaking of branches. Her
limbs—heavy white limbs—would scarcely move,
so terrified was she. But she huddled some
coats around her, went to the door, and looked
out. “Bill!” Not a sound, except the crackling,
The large tree lay on the ground. It had fallen
away from the house, otherwise the building
would have been already ablaze. “ Bill! ” louder
than before. “ Bill! ” in a maddened scream,
The wood crackled, crackled, crackled, and the
smoke was overpoweiing. Bill did not answer.
With a burning red-gum branch across his spine
he lay, and never even groaned.
Four miles away Mrs. Gascoigne sat and watched
a red glow in the sky, and saw a few sparks float
athwart the stars. She might have gone to bed,
as there was now little danger of the fire doing any
damage in her neighborhood. But she felt that
she could not sleep. She was thinking—thinking,
Shortly before dawn the rain began to drizzle,
Atkins galloped up to the clearing. He was a
kind-hearted man, but the story had to be told,
and he got it over quickly. She clasped her knees
and rocked herself for a few moments, but she
could not cry. Instead, she said, “ Thank you for
coming to tell me. I think I’ll take a little sleep
before they bring him home.”
Then she crept into the house—very softly, so
as not to awaken the children—and slept,
D. BOUVERIE.


“THE WINTER IS AT HAND!”
When clouds appear, wise men put on their cloaks;
When great leaves fall, the winter is at hand ;
When the sun sets, who doth not look for night?— (Richaed 111,, ii., 3).
Nature never takes us by surprise ; she always gives a clear signal when change or danger is near. A sharp pain or dull
ache in the back is Nature's warning of Kidney Disease. Is it any wonder that the sufferer from Kidney Disease is short-
tempered and irritable, nervous and despondent P His back
gives him no peace, day and night, the least change of the
weather tells on him, and he is afraid to venture outside the
door if it’s damp or cold, he can’t stoop, can’t walk far. Women
with Kidney Trouble are just the same; they imagine them-
selves to be suffering from all kinds of “ women’s complaints.”
They have no patience with the little ones, are easily annoyed,
and always tired.
During the winter months you are more likely to get colds.
And colds very often affect the Kidneys ; therefore, at the first
sign of backache, or any other symptom of kidney derangement,
take Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills, the special kidney and
bladder medicine for men and women. Bead the following

The Dance.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE music sobbed
And the dancers swung—
Whispering feet
Made rhythmic beat
That pulsed and throbbed
While a perfume clung—
Icent of roses—roses rare,
lose of lips and raven hair.
The music swelled
With a burst of sound—
Through casement-bar
It echoed far,
And its rapture held
Us from the ground,
Until it died as the sea-waves do,
vVith a passionate call—my call to you.
There were roses red
In your fragrant hair,
At your bosom white
They glowed that night.
And you held your head
With a royal air,
Proud in a beauty that thrilled like wine,
But your eyes—your eyes laughed into mine.
Why is the song
Of the violin
So filled with woe ?
I do not know.
Yet if Love be wrong,
Then I would sin
Just for the joy of your wondrous charm,
And your lissome grace on my yielding arm.
Rhythmical
Were the moving feet.
As the waltz went on
Your grey eyes shone.
And whimsical
And passing sweet
Were my lady’s moods as we swayed and swung
To the passionate songs the fiddles sung.
Black as the night
Was your gown of laoe.
Such dazzling charm
On shoulder and arm.
And there was a light
In your up-turned face
That held my eyes in a magic thrall,
While thrilling and low came the music’s call.
Beauty of lips
And grace of limb—
Wondrous charm
Of throat and arm—•
Music that grips
Till the senses swim !
fet sweeter than these, though they thrilled like
wine,
Was the soul in your eyes that yearned to mine.
M.L.
WILL LAWSON.
In Buenos Ayres.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
JAMES M‘GREGOR FINLAND, son of
James M‘Gregor Finland of that ilk in
Scotland, of the great coal mines of Val-
divia in Chile, educated at the grey old University
of Edinburgh, inheritor of his father’s estates,
master of half-a dozen languages, and—son of a
Chilian mother. He had the look of it in the
flattened Indian face. He had the way of it in his
great brute strength, his magnificent physique,
and, above all, English-taught as he was, in his
typical South American, South European dis-
regard of English ways of decency.
I knew him very well. We stayed at the same
house. We sat at the same table. More than all,
we spoke English where everyone else spoke some-
thing else—Spanish, French, Italian, German,
Dutch, or Danish. As for his religion—-God
knows.
I never forgot the Indian in him. and, truth to
tell, he never let one forget it. Yet somehow I
liked the man in spite of myself and my English
up-bringing. There was about him an air of
strength, of virility, that attracted one, especially
a woman—a capacity for immediate action, action
absolutely devoid of the element of the uncertain,
that drew admiration and attention in spite
of a constant under-current of repulsion. One
looked at his strong, white teeth and felt strength.
One heard him speak of his country, and dreamed
patriots. “My country, Chile!” he would say,
and as he said it the snaky eye would deepen and
flash under the depressed Indian eyebone, the
head thrown up with all the pride of a Highlander
among Lowlanders. “ My country is Chile, and
I am Chileno,” and so saying, all was said.
Sometimes he would talk of his sister (he had
■only the one). Of his mother he never spoke—
perhaps it was the Highland bloou, perhaps it
was that he had loved her. Of his father he
talked occasionally, with an undercurrent of dis-
like constantly breaking out in the voice. From
other sources I learned that his mother had been
very wealthy. In fact, the mines which consti-
tuted most of his wealth had been hers. She had
belonged to the highest class of society in Chile,
was well-educated (from a Spanish point of view),
beautiful, accomplished, and fascinating as only
Spanish grace grafted on virility can be. In a
word, she charmed all the senses, {esthetic and
physical alike. Hard and proud to a degree, the
father had been bewitched. He courted her. He
married her.
After marriage, passion died. He never forgave
himself for having married a Chilian. When his
son was born, he never forgave him his Chilian
mother —which was hard on the boy.
As for Don Jaime, he gave back hate for hate.
He grew up as one might expect, wild, reckless,
half-savage. The combined physique of the High-
lander and the Indian drove him back to the
excesses of primitive man. Also, it kept him
strong in spite of himself.
While yet a boy, his father had to send him to
Edinburgh— to tame him. A couple of years after
he came home, he had to shut the door in his face
for his credit and his daughter’s sake. Then
Don Jaime disappeared, and for years no one
knew where he was; whether he was dead or
whether he was alive.
His mother died. A year or two later his father
was shot, during a riot at the mines. Then his
sister communicated with her own and the neigh-
boring Governments, they in turn with the Jefes
and Cortusarios throughout the different countries,
and in due time, in fur and in wool, Don Jaime
came home from the snow and the ce, the cold
a„d the unspeakable wictaW of Patagonia.
* * . * *
Ihe first time we were in Buenos Ayres, the
thing that struck us most (that is of the minor
things) was the iron-barred windows, and the
barred or iron-studded doors or gateways into
the patios (no private dwelling opened directly
upon the street) from which one entered the
house, h.very street window had bars half an
inch thick, suggesting nothing so much as gaols
or private lunatic asylunis, and looking almost
grotesque in combination with stuccoed or
marbetronts. I he next persistent and remark-
a e t ung was the flattish roofs, each with an
ex raoulinanly strong parapet of stone or brick,
It was by no mean 3 decorative; it was too
massive and the slits and loopholes top small for
ia . e learned the use of these things later.
Crimes of violence are common in b. America
rgen ina averages three murders a day, most of
which are committed during the recurrent periodl-
cal three days of the assassin s wind viento de
Wmd haVmg a hornble effect on
.nerves. .

country, and you can shoot from behind a parapet

The last time we were in Buenos Ayres—the
time when we met Don Jaime—the country from
end to end, from Argentine Atlantic to Chilian
Pacific, was in a state of tumult over the Chilian-
Argentine boundary question. Don Jaime was
over in the Atlantic city on business, but owing
to the state of affairs could get nothing done,
Indeed, his life was not safe in the streets . . .
and . . . there was always the parapet, you know,
He was too big a man to be mistaken for an
Argentine, who is a small, “neat” man, leaning
more to the Spanish type than does the Chilian,
Moreover he was too wealthy a man not to be
known, and too daring to keep quiet.
The consequence of his enforced inactivity was
that his temper became atrocious; the flattened
eye-bone, with the gleam of white, and the tawny
eye under it, looking daily more and more vicious,
“ What will you do if war breaks out while you
are here ? ” I asked, one evening. It was a foolish
question, but the tension of the times bred folly.
He answered without looking at me. “I will
go out and cut Argentine throats till my own is
cut,” he said, brutally.
I looked at him, resentment drowned in a feel-
ing of sympathy for unlimited strength tied down,
He seemed strangely restless, pacing up and
down like a caged panther. Suddenly he stopped,
I made as though to speak.
“Hush !” he said abruptly, his head bent for-
ward, every muscle tense, listening and hearing as
only an Indian can.
Something of his condition of nervous tension
communicated itself to me. I listened, too,
strained up to the breaking point, expecting I
knew not what, ready to fear all things; and
though I heard nothing, the room seemed full of
impalpitant breathing.
“What is it?” I cried at last, unable to keep
sil f" t * “ For God’s sake, what is the matter ? ”
, In tbe clty he muttered half-unconsci-
°T* y ' ,• , , .•.
l him bytbB a ™ w?- 0 *? t, »
“ Speak ' , mai ’ speak ! ,^ hat S ’tt J ?
away. The sweat stood in drops on his forehead,
\ uii very li ps were white,
“There is a stir in the town,” he said at last,
straightening his shoulders. And then more
clearly, “I must go out and see what it means.”
At the moment my husband entered the room,
“There is a rocket up from the office of La
Prensa,” he said hastily.
At the same moment, “Boom!” The sound
came ( n a t the doors and windows,
“Uno!” said Filomena, behind me. I looked
roull d. Everyone seemed to have come into the
room . The light glared in on white faces, the
stress of the time throbbing out like heart-beats.
“Boom 1”
« /> JS ,»' faltered the Spanish woman.
What terrible news had the great newspaper
got hold of? Two rockets had gone up. Would
there be three? Was it the air that whispered
<< war ”9
j n th' e silent room where we stood we could
already hear the tumult of a city pouring out of
doors. I looked for Don Jaime, but even as I
looked I knew he was gone-and then-
“ Boom ’”
“Trcsf said someone under his breath. We
looked at one another, each with eyes gone into
the back of the head . The ho use seemed
smothering; somebody flung open the door. We
™>hed 0/ God ! tile coofair, how it struck on
brow and cheek.
Down the street came the clatter of sabres
rattling out over the thunder of hoofs,
“Los soldados!” cried tbe multitude,
Down Calle San Juan, citywards, they galloped,
the crowd before them backing into patios and
houses like chaff before the wind, closing in again,
in its eagerness, behind the very hoofs of the
horses. Trains shot by without stopping. Men
sprang at them as they passed, and fell spinning
back for want of an inch of foothold. And above
it all was the infernal shriek of the sirens, the
bellowing of the hooter, and the ghastly glare of
the rockets as news and news came in to the dif-
ferent newspaper offices,
* * * *
Was it war? No, it was not war. At least,
not yet.
Everyone knows of that terrible time when the
negotiations between Argentina and Chile, “on”
one day, “off” the next, hung fire for week after
week, until people almost went mad of suspense,
The time when Argentina called up her legions,
and Italy promised her help, while Chile bought
warships and said, “ We are the British of South
America. . . . We stand alone,” one hardly knew
which country to be proudest of, or whose women
to weep most with. No, it was not war, though
why it was not war no man can tell. But it was
an attack on the Chilian Consulate. . . . You
remember reading of it in the papers.
And Don Jaime—James McGregor Finland, of
that ilk in Scotland, of the coalfields of Valdivia
in Chile, and son of a Chilian mother, saw it. . .
and seeing, all Chile rose in his blood,
And, in the morning, they found him in the
morgue, as he lay among a row of Argentines . . .
found him with his throat cut, his fingers strings
of bones, from which tbe flesh hung in shreds, just
as the knives he fought had left them.
M. GILMORE
